Then treat the place with an ointment which generates flesh, until it is healed.
But if they are not inverted, and frequently occur on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet: then apply a cautery with warm water to them: and it is so that you take a small tube of bronze, or iron, or from a vulture's feather: and place it over the nail, or the wart. Then pour a small quantity of warm water into the tube: hold your hand, and revolve it with slight pressure of your hand, so that the edge of the tube is pressed into the root of the nail, and the warm water finds a way to the depth, to the root of the nail: and let the patient wait a little for the biting of the warm water for one hour.
Then dismiss him, for the nail is eradicated with its roots.
Do this to them one after the other, until you come to all of them which are in the body.
Then treat them after they are eradicated, with that which generates flesh from ointments.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in Tremor.
When tremor happens to someone from cold in a nerve, or from a quartan fever, or something else: then it is necessary that you cauterize him with four cauteries, or five, on the vertebrae of the back, and between each vertebra a cautery, and on his chest a cautery, and on his stomach a cautery.
For tremor rests with olive cautery, and the healing of the cold illness hastens.
Concerning the method of cauterizing Albathir.
Chapter 57.
Sometimes foul things are expelled in the body, that is, pustules, which are made from corrupt gross materials.
Therefore it is necessary, when they appear, that they be cauterized over the whole head of the pustule with a thin cautery using myrtle wood, the end of which is lit with fire. Or with the root of long birthwort. Or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And sometimes they are cauterized at the beginning of their expulsion in this manner: and they do not increase; and the superfluity making them is broken up, and they are healed from them.
However, it is necessary that this be done after evacuation with bloodletting.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in a flow of blood from an artery in a wound.
Very often there occurs a flow of blood from an artery which has been cut near a wound, which happens externally, or near the incision of an abscess, or the cauterization of a limb, and similar things: and it becomes difficult to stop it.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then hasten with your hand to the opening of the artery, and place your index finger upon it: and press it firmly, until the blood is stopped under your finger, and nothing comes out from it.
Then put small olive cauteries in the fire, and many large ones as well: and blow on them until they become very hot.
Then take one of them, either small or large, according to the wound and the place in which the artery is broken, and place the cautery upon the vein, after which you quickly remove your finger: and hold the cautery until the blood is stopped.
But if it spurts out when you lift your finger from the opening of the artery, and you extinguish the cautery, then quickly take another cautery, from the cauteries which are prepared in the fire.
Therefore do not cease doing this with one after another, until the blood is stopped.
And beware lest you burn the nerve which is there, and another harm befall the patient.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, then it is not possible to stop it, especially when the artery is large, except with one of four methods. Or with a cautery, as we said. Or with extraction, when it is not extracted. For when it is extracted, its extremities contract, and the bleeding is stopped. Or it is tied with a strong ligature of thread. Or medicines with the property of stanching blood are placed upon it, and it is compressed with a suitable constricting pad.
But he who manages its excision with a ligature, and constriction with a cloth, or the application of cauterizing agents and the like, does not entirely benefit him, except rarely.
But if this happens to someone, and a physician or medicine is not present: then he should begin by placing the index finger over the opening of the vein, as we have related, and constricting it strongly, until the blood is obstructed from the upper part of the wound, and above the artery. And the finger should not be removed, pouring cold water of intense coldness constantly over it, until the blood is congealed and thickened, and is excised.
And meanwhile let him consider what he needs from a cautery, or from medicine.
Concerning Incision, Perforation, and Phlebotomy in wounds and similar things.
We have already said in the first chapter, every illness in which it is suitable with fire cautery and burning medicine, and its occasions, its causes, and its instruments, and forms of cauteries.
And I placed there chapters from the head to the foot.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same way: that relief may be given to the seeker of what he seeks.
And before I begin that, it is necessary that you learn, O sons, that in this chapter is from the terror that which is in the first chapter on cauterization.
And because of that it is necessary that caution be greater in it, because in the operation which is in this chapter, very often evacuation with blood happens, with which life stands at the opening of a vein, or incision upon an abscess, or opening of an exit, or cure of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision upon a stone, and those similar: from all of which tremor and fear are associated, and death happens in many of them.
And I warn you, lest that fall upon you in which there is hesitation, for sometimes there fall to you in this art kinds of men with kinds of infirmities. For among them is he who is angered by his illness, and death is light to him: because of the vehemence of that which he finds from his infirmity and the length of its malice. And with illness is from fear which signifies death.
And among them is he who gives his wealth, and enriches you with it, hoping for health, and his illness is pernicious.
Therefore it is not fitting that you consent to him who comes to you from among them, whose manner is such. And let your caution be stronger than your greed and your rapacity. And do not presume upon anything of it, except after certain knowledge, which verifies for you that to which a laudable outcome arrives.
And use in the treatment of all your sick, prognosis and indication of that to which health is suitably directed.
For in that is help for you concerning the acquisition of fame and glory, and memory and praise.
May God inspire you, O sons, with his understanding, and may he not take away from you guidance and inspiration: because that is in his hand.
And indeed I have arranged this chapter, distinguishing it by chapters, according to what preceded in the chapter on Cautery, from the neck to the feet: so that the thing which you wish from it, and which you need, may be lightened for you.
Concerning the treatment of water which collects in the heads of children.
Chapter 1.
Know that this infirmity often happens to children at birth, or when the midwife squeezes the child's head without gentleness.
And sometimes it also happens from a hidden cause which is not known.
And indeed I have not seen this illness in others than in children; and for all whom I saw of them death hastened; therefore it seemed to me to leave off operating in it.
And indeed I saw one of them, a child whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger every day, until it could not sit up by itself because of the size of its head; and the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture indeed, either gathers between the skin and the bone, or gathers under the bone upon the dura mater.
And the operation in that, if the moisture is between the skin and bone, and the abscess is small, then it is necessary that you make an incision in the middle of the head with one cut in width; and let the length of the incision be about two fingerbreadths, until the moisture flows out.
And this is the shape of the spatula.
If therefore the moisture is more increased, and the abscess is larger, then make two incisions, according to this form.
And if the moisture is under the bone, and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on every side: and the water is depressed when you compress it with your hand towards the inside, and that is not hidden from you: then it is necessary that you make an incision in the middle of the head with three incisions, according to this manner.
And after the incision remove all the moisture. Then bind the incisions with cloths and powders. Then anoint it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day.
Then loosen the bandage, and treat the wound with tents and ointments.
And do not allow the head to be bound with equal tightness. And feed the patient with all dry food of little moisture, until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And another method of incision is that you consider where the size of the abscess and the collection of water appear.
Because sometimes it is greater in the occiput, or in the sinciput, or on the right, or on the left.
Therefore, ascend with the incision where the abscess and the filling of water appear to you: and incise it as much as is possible for you.
And observe that you do not cut an artery, and that a flow of blood occurs, and that the patient dies from that flow with the evacuation of moisture.
Concerning the incision of the arteries which are behind the ears.
Chapter II.
When catarrhs occur to someone in the eyes, or in the chest, and it becomes chronic, and the cure of medicines does not help: then the ultimate cure in that is the incision of these two arteries.
Therefore, when you wish to incise them, it is necessary that the patient shave his head. Then rub the place with a rough cloth, so that the arteries appear. Then let the patient bind his neck with any cloth of his. Then consider where the vein pulsates. And the locations of both are the depressed places which are behind the ears: and it is rarely hidden, except in certain men.
Then mark with a cautery on each: and incise each with a sharp spatula on both sides, the incision reaching to the bone: and let this be done across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the spatula under the artery, and incise it upwards with an incision.
And let the length of the incision be about two joined fingers.
For when a vein is incised, blood flows out with a pulsating flow, which leaps inwards with a frequent leap.
But if the artery does not appear to the sense, then it is necessary that you measure from the ear a space of three fingers. Then mark with a cautery, and cut to the bone.
And that which it is necessary to let out of blood, is six ounces and perhaps you let out of it more, or less. All that according to the quantity which appears to you from the strength of the infirm and the fullness of his artery.
Then consider the wound. Then if something of the sifac remains at the bone, then incise it, so that a hot abscess does not occur: then bind the wound with linen cloths.
Then treat it with ointment, until it is healed.
Concerning the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
Chapter III.
When a man suffers from chronic hemicrania, or acute catarrh due to acrimonious humors and heat in the temples, or chronic headache, and similar conditions; and he is treated with medical methods of cure, and they do not help: then we have found in these illnesses the extraction of arteries from the temples, or their cauterization, as we have related.
And the method of operation in their extraction is that the patient should shave the hairs which are in the temples, and should stretch the artery apparent in the temple. For it is shown to you by its pulse, and is rarely hidden except in a few men, or by the intensity of cold.
If it is hidden from you, then let the patient bind his head with any cloth of his: then rub the place with a rough cloth. Or steam the place with warm water, until it appears to you in manifest appearance. Then take a spatula, which I formed a little while ago. Then scrape with it the skin easily, until you reach the artery. Then throw a hook into it, and draw it upwards, until you draw it out of the skin, and make it escape from the tunics which are beneath it on every side.
But if the artery is small, then wrap it with the end of the hook. Then cut from it a part according to the amount by which its ends are lengthened and contracted, and so that a flow of blood does not occur. For if it is not cut, and if it is not severed, the blood does not completely ascend.
Then evacuate from the blood six ounces up to three.
But if the artery is large, then it is necessary that you tie it in two places with a double thread, strong, of silk or of alhod cords, so that putrefaction does not hasten to it before the consolidation of the wound, and a flow of blood occurs.
Then cut the excess which is between the two ligatures. Do that in that hour, or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery to the bone, with a knife cautery, until its extremities are cut, and it will stand in the place of the same work.
And it is a better operation, unless the patient has a fever or is of a heated complexion. Because cautery is among those things which help to finish humidities. Therefore it is a better help.
And it is necessary after the extraction of the arteries that the place be filled with old cotton, and that properly made little cushions be placed upon it.
And after resolution it should be treated with dry medicines which cause flesh to grow, and with linen cloths, until it heals.
But if meanwhile, while you are operating, a flow of blood from an artery occurs, then hasten to cut it off with a cautery, or to fill the place with zegi, and bind your hand until the blood is cut off. But if none of these things is present to you, then place your finger on the place until the blood congeals. And bathe the place with very cold water until the sharpness ceases, and bind it as is necessary.
And of what is lighter and easier in the extraction of an artery, is to cauterize the vein with this cautery having two knives; and let the sharpness of the two knives be similar to an almond. Nevertheless, they are of much less sharpness than two knives. Because if they are sharp, like two knives, cold quickly affects both: and they do not cut the flesh with speed. Therefore, when there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire is held in them, and the flesh is cut quickly: with this, therefore, the work is better than every work, and lighter, and easier.
And this is the form of the cautery.
And let the space that is between the two knives be the quantity of the thickness of a finger, after you mark two places with a cautery; and place both very hot, until they arrive at the bone, and cut the vein in two places: so that what is between its extremities is lengthened. For they are not completely consolidated with this cautery.
Chapter IV.
Concerning the care of sharp, constant flow of tears from the eyes.
When the tears are constant, and their flow is from the veins which are on the outside of the skull, and nothing of medicinal cures with medicines helps in them, and you see the face of the sick person already reddening, and he feels a crawling on his forehead, like the crawling of ants, and his eyes are weakened, moist, so that their hairs are already corroded with corrosion, and his eyelids are heated from the sharpness of the tears: then the ancients needed in their cure this operation, and they named it a cure with the Knife.
And it is, that you should order the sick person to shave the hairs which are on his forehead. Then incise three equidistant fissures in the forehead according to the length of the forehead: and let the length of the fissure be about two fingers, and one fissure be equidistant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length a little from the movement of the muscles, and the third from the other side: and beware of cutting the arteries which are on the two sides: and lengthen your hand from the continuation of the two jawbones, and let the space of each incision be the quantity of three joined fingers: and let you have a prepared piece of sponge, or much dried cloth, with which you dry the blood.
Then insert a spatula with two sharp ends, of which the following is the form. From the incision which is from the side of the temples, up to the second incision: and flay with it all the skin which is between the two incisions, with the membrane which is on the bone. Then do that also from the middle incision to the second incision. Then remove this spatula, and insert into the first incision again another instrument, which is called a sharp, thin knife, smooth on one side, not sharp on the other side. And this is its form.
And place its sharp side towards the flesh connected with the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone: and push it until it reaches the middle fissure: and cut with it all the vessels which descend from the head to the eyes: except that the incision is continued until that which appears of the skin.
Then do that in the middle fissure up to the other fissure.
And after a sufficient quantity of blood has flowed, squeeze out the places of the coagulated blood clots.
Then place in each fissure a pledget of old linen, and place over them pledgets soaked in wine and oil, or vinegar. And see that a hot abscess does not occur.
And on the third day, loosen the bandage, and administer a plentiful infusion with warm water. Then treat it with basilicon ointment and other things with which wounds are treated, until it is healed.
Chapter V.
Concerning the treatment of acute tears and catarrhs affecting the eyes from within the head.
When acute, sharp, persistent catarrhs affect someone, and you see their eyes weakened, small, and their sight already impaired; and the eyelids ulcerated, and hairs falling from them, and there is acute, painful headache in the depths of the head, and sneezing follows: then understand from these occurrences that those materials and catarrhs do not come except from many veins.
And the best treatment in these cases is this. And it is that you order the patient to shave his forehead. Then make in it one fissure in the middle of the forehead, or another a little wider, and begin with the fissure from the left temple to the right temple: and let the incision reach the bone: and keep your hand away from the temples, which move during mastication: until when the bone is uncovered: and you shall dry all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate the two lips of the fissure with old lint, or with linen strips. Then bind it above with powders: and soak the powders with wine and oil, so that a hot abscess does not occur.
And when you unbind it, and you see that the hot abscess is now diminishing: then it is necessary that you rub the bone, until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that makes flesh grow: as if you take two parts of wheat flour, and four parts of colophony: and prepare a plaster from them, and apply it to the growth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter VI.
Concerning the treatment of that which falls into the Ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four kinds: either a mineral stone, or similar to a stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as a chickpea and beans; or it is a flowing substance, such as water and vinegar, and things similar to them; or some animal.
When therefore a stone, or a kind of stone from those which are not added to, nor grow in the ear, falls into the ear, expose the ear to the sun: if therefore you see the stone, then instill into the ear something of violet oil, or sesame oil. Then manage its extraction, and make him sneeze with sneezing powders, and block the nostrils at the coming of the sneezing, after you place around the ear pieces of cloth or wool, and extend the ear upwards.
For very often it comes out with this treatment.
But if it does not come out, then manage its extraction with forceps, that is, with fine tweezers: whose form is this.
If therefore it comes out with forceps, it is good.
And if not, then manage its extraction with a small, thin, blunt hook.
If therefore it does not come out by that, then make a cannula of metal, and insert the end of the cannula into the hole of the ear deeply, and fill what is around the cannula with wax softened with oil, so that there is no way for the wind except through the cannula: then draw it out with your strong breath.
For very often it comes out with that which we have related. But if it does not come out in this way: then take a little of helkalimbat, or of birdlime, that is, prepared glue, with which birds are caught: and put it on the end of a probe after you have neatly wrapped a pledget around it: then insert it into the hole of the ear easily, after you have dried the ear from moisture.
If therefore it does not come out with all that we have related, then hasten to make an incision, before a hot abscess or a spasm occurs.
And the method of incision is that the phlebotomist first bleeds the patient in the cephalic vein: and draw from him blood according to the measure of his strength. Then make the patient sit between your hands, and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear near its fatty tissue in a depressed place: and let the incision be in the shape of a crescent, until you reach the stone: then remove it with whatever is possible for you of instruments.
Then sew up the incision quickly and take care of it until it heals.
But if the thing falling into the ear is one of the grains which grow and swell, then administer its extraction.
But if it does not yield to exit, then take a thin, delicate spatula, according to this form.
And administer with it the excision of that kind of grain which falls into the ear, and do not do it when you think that the grain is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, until you put it into many pieces: then extract them with hooks, or with a fine forceps, or with suction, as we said, for its extraction is alleviated.
In the case of water entering the ear, the infirm person should first use sneezing with a handkerchief; and he should fill his ear with a little old linen; and he should lie on the ear in which the water is. If therefore it exits through that, it is good.
And if not: then take many stones, about the length of a finger, thin, smooth, and heat them slightly with fire, and let the infirm person insert one of them into the opening of his ear: and let him stand on one foot on that side, and strike with another stone upon the stone which is in the ear. Therefore let him not cease doing this with stone and stone, until all the water comes out.
And now the water comes out so that you take one from a piece of papyrus or a rush, and insert one end of it into the ear; and light the other end with fire, until most of it is burned. Then repeat with another rush, and do this many times, until all the water comes out: and draw it out with a tube as was said before with the stone.
But in the extraction of an animal entering it, if it is small, like a flea and similar things, treat it as I said in the division.
But if it is of large body, which is apparent to the senses: then manage its extraction with forceps and hooks: and its matter is easier than all things held in the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we said in the division, and in the treatise on distillations, and your treatment does not help: then it is necessary that you look at the ear in the sun. If therefore something of worms appears there, then extract it with forceps, or with fine hooks.
But if nothing appears there, then take a cannula, of which this is the form, narrow below, wide above: and insert the thin end into the ear according to the amount that the patient tolerates: then suck with it with strong suction. Do this many times, until all the worms come out.
But if they do not obey you to come out, then stop up the circumference of the cannula with wax, as I said in the case of the stone.
But if they do not come out with what we have said, then administer distillations which the ancients have already experienced in killing worms: and you will find that in the treatise on distillations.
And let your pouring out, by which you pour oils and medicines into the ear, be with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. As you see, make it of silver or bronze, narrow below, with a small hole, wide above.
Therefore let that which pushes what is in the concavity of the cannula be made of bronze, decently made. And if you wish, take a probe and wrap cotton around its end neatly; then pour oil, or juice, or whatever you wish of these medicines, into the cannula; and let it be in the ear; then insert the probe with the cotton from above, and press it with your hand with moderate pressure, until the oil is driven into the concavity of the hearing, and the patient feels it inside; and what is poured into the ear should have been warmed a little with fire; and beware lest that which is distilled into it be very cold; for medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter VII.
Concerning the care of the obstruction happening in the ear.
Sometimes certain infants emerge from the wombs of their mothers imperforate.
And it has also happened to some men that there is an obstruction in the passage of the ear from a wound, or from flesh growing there.
And this obstruction sometimes happens in the depth of the opening of the ear, which sight does not reach; and sometimes it happens near the exterior of the opening, and sight reaches it.
Therefore, that which happens in the depth of the ear, and which sight does not reach, is in most cases difficult to heal.
And in that which sight reaches, it is necessary that you put the ear of the sick person in the sun, and look into it: then if you see the apparent obstruction, open it with a thin spatula, whose form is as follows, let its extremity in which there is a certain small width, some part of which is sharp: and let the rest of the spatula be of two smooth sides: so that it does not injure the ear.
If therefore the obstruction is from flesh grown in the passage of the ear: then hold it with a thin hook, and cut it with strong ease, until all the flesh is removed.
But if the obstruction is in the depth of the ear: then take a thin, smooth probe, and heat it slightly with fire: then insert it into the passage of the ear.
If therefore you feel the obstruction with it, which the sick person finds with difficulty of hearing, try its opening with ease: and beware lest you injure the nerves of the ear.
Then place in the ear a pledget according to the size of the opening, which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment: do this for several days, until you are sure of the healing of the wound.
Or take a pledget, and dip it in rose water, and sprinkle on it powdered zegi, and use it.
But if you see the ear already festering: then it is necessary that you remove the pledget, and leave it with another pledget, which you will wrap in cerate which is made with rose oil, until the hot abscess settles: then treat it until it heals.
But if bleeding should occur: then immerse a sponge, or cloths in cold water, and place it over the ear: and use the remaining treatment, which stops the flow of blood, described in the treatise on distillations.
Concerning the treatment of the stye of the eyelids.
Chapter VIII.
These styes which occur in the eyelids of the eye, are sometimes moist, and sometimes dry.
Therefore it is necessary to grasp them with forceps: or with a hook, and to incise them with a spatula from their roots. Then place crushed vitriol on the site.
But if blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a thin lenticular cautery.
And to cauterize them is better after incision. For many times they return when something remains of their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned, and they do not return.
Concerning the treatment of the hailstone-like growths of the eyelid.
Chapter IX.
Very often something occurs in the eyelids which resembles hail in its strength and hardness, and because of this it is called hailstone: and it is a collection of thick moisture in the upper eyelid and the lower eyelid.
Therefore, the operation on them is that you should then consider if it is a hailstone extending into that which appears of the eyelid, and they move to every part, and its matter is easy.
Therefore make a fissure upon it lengthwise. Then scrape it from every side easily until it comes out. Then suspend it with a hook, and cut it off.
But if its excision is not prepared for you except after you penetrate the eyelid with a small incision, then that harms the patient in some way.
But if the fissure is large, then join it with a suture, and take care of it until it heals.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For ointment restores it and causes it to grow flesh.
And if the hailstone slopes towards the interior of the ear towards the inner surface, then invert the eyelid and suspend the hailstone with a hook; except that you need an incision, and press it from every side.
If the eyelid is lengthened by incision, that does no harm at all.
Then wash the eye after the incision of the hailstone with salt water, and treat the place with that which causes it to grow flesh, until the sick person is healed.
Concerning the treatment of the axirnath of the eyelids.
Chapter X.
Axirnath is fat which is generated in the membranes of the upper eyelid; and it happens mostly to children; and it weighs down their eyes, and catarrhs happen to them in the mornings, and they cannot look at the sunlight, because tears rush to them, and therefore you see them lying down on their faces always, and they sleep on them, and their eyelids are moist under their eyebrows, which swelling has now covered; and indeed the prominence of the axirnath is apparent to the eye, and when you press the place with your fingers, you feel the axirnath between them.
And the method of its operation is that the sick person should put his head in your bosom.
Then take a linen cloth, and prepare a pledget in it, and make from that pledget a circle according to the quantity which contains the axirnath on every side. Then place it over it, and press with your fingers on every side, so that the axirnath may be gathered in the middle of the circle.
Then make a cut in the middle of that moisture with a lancet, making incisions in width, and let the incision not be larger than that which is made in phlebotomy in depth.
Or it is necessary that you incise the whole skin until you arrive at the axirnath, for in many instances the axirnath proceeds from the incision according to the form of a piece of white fat.
Therefore extract it with the cloth which you have infiltrated between your fingers to the exterior: and you turn your hand to the right and left, until it is dispersed: and beware that you do not add to the incision, so that you do not arrive with the lancet to the eye, and injure it.
But if the axirnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the incision, add a little to the incision with ease, until the axirnath proceeds. Then extract it, as we said.
Then submerge cloths in vinegar and water, and place them on the place, and bind it with powders.
And some men grind salt, and put it in the hollow of the incision, so that what remains of that moisture may liquefy.
Then care for it until it heals.
But if it happens in a place with a hot abscess, then treat it with soothing plasters until it heals.
Concerning the ways of Ataxmir of the eye, that is, when the eyelid of the eye is softened, whence it is wrinkled as it ought not to be.
Chapter XI.
When superfluous hairs are born in the eyelids beyond the course of the natural hairs, and grow: then they harm the eye, and cause various illnesses, such as constant tears, and softening of the eyelids, and albugo, until it becomes a cause of destruction of the eye.
And Ataxmir of the eye is cured according to four methods. Or by cautery with fire. Or with a sharp medicine, according to what was said before in the chapter on cautery. Or Ataxmir is cured by incision and suture. Or by tubes, according to what I shall say.
It is necessary that you put the head of the sick person in your bosom. Then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If therefore it is turned, it is good. And if not: insert a needle into it, in which there is a thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate with the thread from above, and let that be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the margin of the eyelid, in which the hairs grow: and draw the thread upwards with the eyelid, and turn it with a probe.
Then make an incision in the inner part of the eyelid under the added hairs with a spatula, falling from the larger lacrimal duct to the smaller lacrimal duct.
Then remove the thread, and place under the eyelid a small cushion of cotton, or cloth: then mark above the eyelid with cautery, which is like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that the shape be made according to the size and elevation of the eyelid: because it is already diversified in men.
For among them is one who needs to have a large quantity cut from the eyelid, according to the quantity which is softened by the eyelid.
Then with a spatula incise upon the two lines which you marked, and begin from the larger lacrimal duct to the smaller lacrimal duct, and let there be one fissure near the natural hairs, as is the thickness of a ray.
Then introduce a hook into one of the two angles of the skin. Then flay it completely. Then unite with a suture the two lips, with needle and fine woolen thread: and wipe away the blood, and make adhere what superfluously protrudes from the threads over the two eyebrows, with certain sticking substances, if you wish to do it: and if not, do not worry.
Then leave the suture and threads for three or four days; afterwards, treat it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without a suture, and treat it with that which dries and constricts. For the eyelid is raised at the closing and uniting of the wound: but it will be better in the future.
The ancients taught this method of treatment, Ataxmir.
Nevertheless, there is concern for the patient, and it is from a good operation, and there is no fear in it.
And another method is also in Ataxmir: and it is to mark on the eyelid a figure which is like the figure of a myrtle leaf, as we have narrated.
Then raise the eyelid with three hooks, separate or joined together, according to this form which follows, then cut away what is superfluous with small scissors, according to this form, with an even cut.
But if it is not possible for you to hold the hook, and it is not equal: then take a needle on which there is thread, and insert it in the middle of the figure: and insert another thread near the larger tear duct, and a second thread near the smaller tear duct: and gather the threads between your fingers evenly.
Then raise your hand with an even lift: and raise the skin upon which the mark has been made, as we have narrated.
Then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture: and care for it until it is healed.
But if an abscess should occur at your incision, or your fissure: soothe that abscess with a cerate, and similar things, from soothing ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelids also, that their hairs are turned.
Therefore it is necessary that you use what we have said in incision: but suture is more skillful for both. Concerning the treatment of Ataxmir with canes.
And it is, that you should turn back the eyelid, and incise the fissure which is within, according to what I have related. Then skillfully make two canes, or two thin pieces of wood: whose length should be according to the length of the eyelid, and their width should be less than the width of a spatula: and you have already incised at the extremities of each from both sides where you hold the threads.
Then carefully apply each to that which superabounds from the eyelid of the eye, and bind the two canes from both sides with a strong binding, and leave it for several days. For the constricted skin will die, and will blacken, and will putrefy, until it falls off by itself.
If therefore it is delayed, then incise it with scissors. Then treat it until it is healed.
When therefore it is consolidated, the eyelid is raised: and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of two cannulas, which you will make according to this figure, and quantity in length and breadth.
Chapter XII.
Concerning the elevation of hairs pricking in the eye with a needle.
The operation in this is, that you take a fine needle, and insert into it a fine, smooth silk thread. Then bring together its ends, and knot each with a very fine knot: and let the length of the threads be one palm.
Then place in the Alinxotati another fine thread under it shortly, and knot its ends if you wish.
Then let the patient put his head in your bosom, and be near the sun so that the operation may be clear: because it is a subtle, or small, operation.
Then insert the needle with the Alinxotati into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly, until you pull it out above the natural hair.
Then pull the hair up above the Alinxotati: and insert that hair into the Alinxotati: if it is one, or two, or three, not more.
Then draw your hand with it, until the hair comes out with the Alinxotati in the eyelid with the natural hair.
But if the Alinxotati is pulled, and the hair does not come out with it: you will pull the Alinxotati downwards, with the thread which you have placed in it, until the Alinxotati comes out from the upper hole, and you see it with the eye's vision: then indeed you will pull the Alinxotati and the thread: and pull out both, and close the eye: and leave it closed for two days, or three, until the hair adheres to its place, and flesh grows over it.
But if the hair is short, then either join to it a long hair from the natural hairs, and lift it with it: or leave it, until it lengthens after a space of time, and then lift it.
Chapter XIII.
When this Alxatrati happens to the eye, it is called Leporina.
And this Alxatrati is either natural or accidental.
Accidental, therefore, arises from the consolidation of a wound, or incision, or cautery, and similar things.
And the method of operating on it is that you should cut that consolidation, and separate its lips, and place between them a pledget of linen, and bind it until it heals.
And it is not necessary that you should use in its treatment things which dry and constrict: for if you do that, the Alxatrati returns worse than it was: indeed use in it things which soften, such as fenugreek: and embrocate with water in which mallow has been boiled, linseed: and diachylon ointment, which has been liquefied with some oil: and envelop with it a pledget, and treat with it.
And the treasure of your treatment is that you should strive with all your skill that it should not consolidate according to the form which it had at first.
And this operation which we have described is nothing but a certain rectification of the Alxatrati, so that the shape of the place does not return completely to what it was.
Concerning the treatment of Alxatrati which occurs in the lower eyelid, and is a wrinkling or contraction upwards to the right and left.
Chapter XIV.
This Alxatrati which occurs below is that which is truly called Alxatrati. And it is natural, and it is accidental.
And accidental is caused by a wound, or fissure, or cautery, and similar things.
And the method of operation in it is that you take a needle in which there is a double thread, and you insert it into the flesh, and you make it penetrate from the left lacrimal duct to the right lacrimal duct, until the thread arrives at the two extremities of the flesh.
Then extend the flesh upwards with the needle, and incise it with a broad spatula. If therefore the shape of the eyelid returns as it ought, it is good.
And if not, take a radius, and place it in the fissure, and turn the eyelid with it: and make two fissures in the inner side of the eyelid, and let the extremities of the two fissures be from the two angles of the incision, until they meet and an angle is made from them: until when they are joined their figure becomes this figure, and it is the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh by the amount that is its sharp lower side, from that which follows the eyelid. Then join the separated parts; sew it with a wool thread.
Then treat them with what we have said, of softening medicines, and linen cloths, until they are healed.
And if an abscess occurs from a fissure, and suture, or cautery: then it is necessary that you make a flat fissure under the hairs of the eyelids, also according to what preceded. Then separate between the two lips with a linen cloth, according to what we have said.
And the sum of the discussion about the treatment of an abscess, when it is above, or below, is, that the operation proceeds in it, according to what is prepared from the form of the abscess.
For it itself is of many diversities in form: and a skilled craftsman prepares a remedy in whatever way is possible for him, until he restores the figure to its natural form, or close to it, and the patient himself, according to what is suitable for him from the operation and instruments in many arrangements.
Chapter XV.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva, or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation happens to many people, so that the upper eyelid is consolidated with the white of the eye, and prevents it from its natural functions.
Therefore it is necessary that you insert the end of a probe under the eyelid and raise it upwards: or extend it with a hook: then incise the adhesion with a thin spatula, which should not have the sharpness of cutting spatulas, but rather be somewhat blunt: so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and the spatula is removed and injures the eye with its sharpness: and let your incision be as if you are scraping away that adhesion easily, until the eyelid returns to its natural form, and the entire adhesion is healed.
Then pour salt water into the eye, wash it with water: or instill into it from a sieve made from ziniar dissolved in water.
Then separate the eyelid and eye with a linen cloth: and place on the eye wool infused with egg white: and after the third day use a sieve, and with solidifying agents, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the nail and the eminence of flesh of the tear ducts.
Chapter XVI.
Know that the nail is according to two methods. Or it is sinewy: and it is similar to hard, thin parchment. Or it is not sinewy, similar to congealed white moisture: which when iron touches it, or you try to seize it with a hook, is cut off, and the hook does not fix in it.
And both kinds do not begin except from the greater lachrymal [duct], until it goes gradually, until it covers the sight, and prevents light, and the movement of the eye.
And the method of operating in its incision is: that the sick person puts his head in your bosom. Then open his eyes, and lift the eyelid with your hand. Then collect the nail with a hook of little doubling, and extend it upwards. Then take a needle, and insert into it a hair from the hairs of a horse, or an ox, or a cow: and let the thread be strong, and double the end of the needle a little, and fix it in the middle of the nail: and penetrate it with the needle, and tie with thread the nail, and extend it upwards: and flay with the hair the side of the nail which follows the pupil, as if you were scraping it with the hair up to its end.
Then incise the remainder at the root of the lachrymal duct with a fine spatula, or with small scissors: and leave the natural flesh of the lachrymal duct, so that a constant flow of tears may not result from its incision.
And sometimes the nail is extended with a hook alone; or with thread, as we have said. Then scrape with a smooth spatula, of which this is the form.
And beware lest the spatula touch the corneal tunic, and a rupture occur in it, and the pupil be perforated.
Then after the incision, throw into the eye some crushed salt, or something made from ziniar, and leave it until another day. Then treat it with what is necessary, until it is healed.
But if the nail is not sinewy, and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor is a hook fixed in it, then this type of nail is often treated with medicines, and is healed as I have described in the division.
But if you wish to incise it, then open the eye of the patient, and take a thin, smooth spatula of weak sharpness, according to this form. And shave the nail from above with skill. But if you see that it is rarefied and broken, and the shaving impresses itself on it, then instill into the eye from your hour some sief made from ziniar, or from red sief, or crushed salt: and close the eye until another day.
Then repeat the operation on it until the whole of it is removed. Unless there is a hot abscess in the eye contrary to this: for then leave it, and treat the abscess until it is healed.
Then repeat the operation on it with a razor until it is healed.
But if the prominence of the lachrymal flesh causes great harm to the eye, suspend it with a hook, and incise part of it: and do not extend the incision, lest the flow of tears be affected.
Then instill into the lachrymal duct a red  sieif, or one made from verdigris, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of Alguardenic, and the flesh grown in the eye.
Chapter XVII.
Sometimes a red, composite flesh grows in the eyes of men, either superimposed, until it covers the pupil, or approaches, or constricts above the eyelids, and perhaps turns the eyelids outwards: and it resembles a balaustine rose.
And the operation in it also is, that the infirm person should place his head in your bosom. Then open his eyes, and collect all that flesh with hooks suitable for it: and hold it with one forceps, or instrument.
Then incise the flesh first, until its whole is finished with a scalpel. And beware of the eye, lest you injure it during the operation: and let your incision be that with which you incise it, either with a spatula, which we have described in the incision of the nail: or with small scissors, with which the asebel is collected, according to what its form will become after this chapter.
When therefore your incision is completed, and all the alguardenici is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt: or distil into it red powder, and things similar to it from corrosive medicines: and place upon the eye outside cotton with egg white: so that it may be protected from a hot abscess.
But if some of the alguardenici remains, and hot blood dominates you, and you fear a hot abscess: then leave the eye, and treat it with that which calms an abscess.
Then repeat the operation on it until it is healed.
And similarly do with the added flesh which occurs in the eye from this kind.
However, it is necessary that you abstain from treating any weak eye, from natural or accidental cause, by operating with iron: and do not put your hand to any of these operations until you see that the member is tolerating that operation.
Concerning the removal of a cataract from the eye.
Chapter XVIII.
Cataracts are red veins which are woven over the eye, and prevent the vision from performing its function: and weaken the eye with the passage of time.
Therefore it is necessary for you first to consider if the eye in which is the cataract is strong, and there is no illness in it other than the cataract: then remove its cataract.
And it is so that you instruct the patient to place his head in your bosom.
Then suspend those veins with one hook, or two according to your judgment: and let the hook be a thin double hook, according to this form, or let there be two hooks joined in one body, according to this form.
Then collect with fine scissors those veins skillfully, and wipe away the blood hour by hour, until you see those veins removed from the eye, and they are resolved: and beware lest you injure the eye with the ends of the spatula.
And let your operation be at midday before the sun, and be very firm in your operation, so that you do not cut except those veins.
And when you have finished, then distill into the eye green or red vitriol, so that by its sharpness it corrodes what remains of the sebum.
But if it is not possible for you to collect all of it in that hour, then apply a plaster to the eye which soothes the pain of the eye: and leave it for several days, until its pain is settled and it is secure from a hot abscess: then repeat on it in the same manner, until it is healed.
And this is the shape of the scissors.
Concerning the reduction of the Arisatus to the nose.
Chapter XIX.
Physicians call Arisatus a fistula, which you treat with a cautery, or with a sharp burning medicine, according to what its previous description has indicated.
And if it is not healed, then there is no other method in it: except that you incise above the abscess at its origin, and extract all the moisture that is in it and the pus, until the bone is exposed.
When therefore the bone is exposed, and you see corruption or blackness in it, then scrape it with an instrument, whose form is this.
And it is called a rasp.
Let it be made of tempered iron, and let its head be round like an awl, which is pointed with the point of a file, or with the point of an aliskilfer with a fine point: and place it upon the corrupted part of the bone.
Then roll it between your fingers, and you press your hand gently, until you know that that corruption has now been scraped away: and do that several times.
Then restore the place with drying,styptic medicines.
If therefore the place consolidates, and flesh grows in it, and the flow of pus is cut off: and it remains for forty days, and is not renewed, and does not fester, and no accident happens in it: then you know that it is now healed.
And if not, then there is no remedy but to reduce the fistula to the opening of the nose, according to this method.
And it is, that you uncover from the second bone with an iron, or with a sharp medicine: and when the bone is uncovered, then take a trephine in this manner, let its iron extremity be triangular: and let its wooden part be a pyramid tapering to the end, as you see.
Then place it upon the bone itself: and let it be near the lachrymal duct: and move your hand a little away from the eye. Then rotate your hand with the trephine, until it penetrates the bone, and the patient feels it: by this that he finds wind coming out of it, when he holds his hand over his nose.
Therefore then restore the place with drying styptic medicines, as I taught you.
When therefore flesh grows, and the place hardens: then the matter which was discharged outwardly, returns to the nose: therefore it is easier on the patient.
Concerning the reduction of a protrusion of the eye.
Chapter 20.
When the eye protrudes in its entirety, and no harm or diminution occurs in vision: then it is necessary that you loosen the patient; then you should bleed him; afterwards you should apply cupping glasses to the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then place on the eye an ointment which is made from acacia, aloe, olibanum, and sarcocolla. Then bind the eye from above with a plaster with many powders.
And the method of the powders is: that you take many soft cloths, and each cloth of them is doubled over fourfold; make many of them; then place them one upon another, according to the quantity which the eye needs; then bind over them with a hard cloth with a strong binding.
And repeat the cupping glasses with suction without scarification.
Then remove the cupping glasses, and leave the eye bound day and night.
But if the binding is again softened, then it is necessary that it be bound tightly: then remove the binding after a day and a night, as we said.
If therefore you see the eye has now returned to the tunic, it is good: and if not, then repeat the plaster, and the powders, and the binding, and the cupping glasses, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the uvea.
Chapter XXI.
When a rupture happens in the uvea tunic, then it protrudes and comes forth outside the eyelids like a grape seed, and the man's appearance is disfigured because of it, and you wish to incise it: then the operation in it is, according to what I relate: and it is, that you insert a needle into the root of the uvea from the lower to the upper part.
Then insert another needle in which is a double thread from the lachrymal side, and make it penetrate; and leave the first needle according to its position.
Then incise the place of the double thread, and tie with it the part of the uvea around the needle; and tighten it very much.
Then remove the needle, and place over the eye wool infused in egg white; and leave it until the thread falls with the uvea.
Then treat the eye with that which strengthens it, until the wound is healed.
And sometimes the uvea is constricted from every side with a circle of cloth, until the eye adds a great prominence.
Then let it be perforated with a fine spatula, which reaches to the depth of the eye, and let the white moisture run, and let the eye descend, and enter immediately; then tighten it until it heals.
Concerning the treatment of Alcomnati.
Chapter XXII.
This sickness which is called Alcomnati is nothing but pus which gathers in the eye, similar to descending water, and it is not itself.
And the method of operating on it is that you make the sick person sit on a prepared or straight seat.
Then take his head with your hands from both sides, and move it until you see the pus reach the lower parts of your eyes. Then it is fixed, and does not descend, and the light is resolved: and the sick person sees things as he saw: and if it does not descend lower, know that it is water.
But if the descent of the pus is not prepared with what we have said, then make the sick person sit between your hands. Then take a thin spatula, and cut with it above the corneal membrane a little at the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the crown, until the pus comes out.
When therefore it comes out, then instill into the eye warm water in which you have mixed honey: or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey: then it is treated with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of water which descends into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter XXIII.
We have already spoken of the types of water in the division, and what is suitable for perforation by means of a declaration and explanation, then take the same thing according to the health.
Then therefore it is necessary that you make the sick person sit between your hands squarely, opposite the light, near the sun: and bind his healthy eye, and bind it tightly: then the eyelid of his eye with your right hand, if the eye in which is the water, is the left eye: or with your left hand, if the eye is the right.
Then take with your right hand an almond, if the eye is the left; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right.
Then place the end of the almond near the cornea by the thickness of a ray in the very apparent whiteness of the smaller lachrymal eye: then push the almond with force: and you roll your hand with it, until it penetrates into the white of the eye: and you feel that the almond has already arrived at the empty thing.
And it is necessary that the measure of the journey of the almond to the depth be the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the end of the blackness: and that is the cornea of the eye. For you will see them in the very pupil by the vision of the eye, because of the clarity of the corneal tunic.
Then place the almond up to the place where the water is. Then press it downwards one time after another.
If therefore the water descends immediately, then the sick person sees that upon which he opens his eye immediately, and the almond in his eye.
Then rest a little. If therefore the water descends, then remove it a second time, except that the almond comes out.
When therefore it is fixed, and does not ascend: then extract the almond with ease: and you slowly roll your hand.
Then dissolve something of clear gem salt in water: and wash the eye inside with it. Then place outside the eye all the lint, or wool soaked in rose oil and egg white: and bind with it the healthy eye.
But we, and those who are of our time, place upon it crushed cumin with egg white.
But if the almond does not obey you to enter the eye because of its hardness, because it is from a man whose eye is very hard: then it is necessary that you take a spatula, which is called Alberid, whose form is this.
Therefore pierce with it only the conjunctiva, and do not put your whole hand in the hole: and it is only to make a subtle way for the almond to its place.
Then insert the almond, as we have said.
And at the completion of your work, let a properly made bed be prepared for the patient, in which he may sleep on his back in a dark room: and let him be forbidden all movements, and coughing: and give him food which soothes his nature: and let him not move his head to the right or left at all.
And let the bandage remain in its place until the third day.
Then unbind it in that dark room, and test his sight: and make him see things. Then reapply the bandage until the seventh day.
And it is not fitting that he do this at the hour of treatment, or immediately after incision; rather it is necessary that he avoid it; because water quickly rises through forceful gazing.
But if a warm abscess occurs, then it is necessary that you unbind the eye before the seventh [day]: and treat it with that which calms the abscess, until it is calmed; then clear his vision, and place upon his face an eyewash by which his sight may be governed for a few days under it, him being in that dark room.
Then let him go out from the house gradually, and converse in his own houses.
And know that in the cautery the student is not excused from vision sometimes: then indeed let him dare to perform the operation.
And now indeed there has come to us a certain one of those who are from Al-Andalus, who said that there was made in Al-Andalus a perforated tube, by which water is sucked.
Truly therefore I have not seen anyone in your land who has made that: nor have I read it in any of the books of the ancients: and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the forms of the kinds of tube, so that he who is ignorant of them may stand upon them.
Let them be made of bronze properly; and let their extremities be triangulated with this same thinness, and a sharp point.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the care of flesh grown in the nose.
Sometimes diverse fleshy growths are produced in the nose, of which some resemble a scorpion with many legs; and of these there is cancerous flesh, stony, of a dark color; and of these there is that which is soft flesh, not of a dark color.
Therefore, in that which of these fleshes is soft, not malignant, nor cancerous, it is necessary that you cause the infirm person to sit between your hands opposite the sun, and open his nostrils, and throw a hook into those fleshes; then draw them to the exterior, and incise what you have grasped of them with a thin, sharp spatula from one side, until you know that all the flesh is now removed.
But if something remains of that whose excision is not possible, then shave it with one of the thin instruments easily, until nothing of it remains. But if blood dominates you, or a hot abscess occurs, then oppose it by what is necessary; or if it is of malignant abscesses, then hasten to cauterize it until the blood is cut off and all the flesh is removed.
Then vinegar, and water, or wine, should be thrown into the nose after the incision. But if the nose is opened, and moisture proceeds from it to the palate, then know that he is now healthy.
If, however, the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then know that within it is flesh grown on the upper bones of the nose, to which the instrument did not reach with the incision.
Then it is necessary to insert a linen thread of some thickness, and tie many knots in it; and place between each knot the quantity of a finger, or less; and let the patient contrive to put one end of the thread into the nose with a probe, or with whatever is possible for him after making it similar to a cautery; and let him draw his breath until it reaches the cavity and comes out over his throat.
And indeed, boys often do something similar to this in schools; and it is an easy thing for anyone who wishes.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end which comes out over the nose, and the other which remains in the nose. Then take and remove the flesh with the knots that are on the thread. Do this until you know that the flesh is now with the knots of the thread.
Then pull out the thread: and after wiping away the blood, put a pledget soaked in Egyptian ointment into the nose: do this for three days, or more, until the ointment corrodes all that remains of the flesh.
Then, at the end of the process, place a lead cannula in the nose for a few days, until it heals.
If, however, it needs treatment that dries, use that.
To make a head-purge, with which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it of silver or bronze: it should be similar to a small lamp, open like a shell: and its channel likewise.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula, like a reed.
And the shell of the instrument for making a head-purge should be full, open; to which there should be a handle at its end, with which you hold it when you heat oil in it, or whatever you want from juices and liquids.
Chapter 25.
Often a polyp grows at the tip of the nose, and it becomes large, and increases with the days, until its appearance becomes ugly.
And because of that it is necessary that you cut it in the beginning of its appearance, and eradicate all of it. Then place on the location, either a cautery, or a burning medicine which stands in its place.
If therefore its incision is delayed, until it becomes large, then consider, if it is stony, hard, dark in color, and insensitive: then do not touch it with iron: because it is a cancerous abscess.
For many times I have seen, that someone incised such an abscess, and great harm resulted to him who had it.
And if the abscess is soft to the touch, not dark in color, and you see that incision is possible in all of it, then empty the diseased area, and incise it without fear, without dread: and treat the place with that which dries and constricts, until it is healed.
Chapter XXVI.
Concerning the suture of the nose, and lip, and ear.
Know that when a solution of continuity happens in one of these cartilages, the operation benefits little, except in certain men.
Therefore, when something of that sort happens to someone, you ought to consider if it is a fresh wound with its blood: and then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture: then treat it until it heals.
And if the lips of the solution of continuity are already separated, and each part has become healthy: then it is necessary that you scrape away all the part from its apparent skin, until it bleeds. Then bring together the two lips with a suture, and bind them, and powder over it dragons blood, and crushed olibanum: and place over the powders a piece of diachylon plaster, and some other of the consolidating plasters: and leave it bound for two or three days. Then unbind it, and change the medicine: and leave it until the threads are cut off by themselves. Then treat it with ointment until it heals.
The method of suture is, that you bring together the solution of continuity, either with needles, as we have recounted in the suture of the belly: or bring together the solution with thread, as I have made you know.
Chapter XXVII.
Concerning the extraction of knots from the lips.
Many men indeed often have hard abscesses in their inner lips similar to grains of barley: and some of them are smaller, and some larger.
Therefore it is necessary that you turn back the lip, and incise over each knot, and suspend it with a hook, and incise it on every side.
Then fill the place after the incision with crushed alum until the bleeding stops.
Then let it be washed with vinegar, and let the place be treated with something astringent, until the wounds are healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
Concerning the incision of flesh grown upon the gum.
Often flesh grows upon the gum, which the ancients called abuluss.
Therefore it is necessary that you suspend it with a hook; or hold it with forceps, and incise it at its root, and let the pus or blood flow.
Then place upon the place pounded egg white, or other styptic, drying powders.
But if that flesh returns after treatment, for it often returns, then incise it a second time, and cauterize it: for it does not return after cauterization.
Chapter XXIX.
Concerning the extraction of teeth with iron.
Sometimes rough, foul crusts gather on the surfaces of the teeth, on the inner and outer parts, and between the gums, and sometimes they blacken, and become yellow, and green, until corruption from this reaches the gum, and the teeth become foul through it.
Therefore it is necessary that you make the sick person sit between your hands, and his head in your lap, and scrape the molar and tooth, in which the crusts and things like sand appear to you, until nothing of them remains, and similarly do with the blackness, and greenness, and yellowness, and others, until they are cleansed.
If therefore that which is in them is removed from the beginning of the scraping, it is good; and if not, then repeat the scraping on them another day, a second, and a third, until at last your intention is achieved in that which you wish.
And know that a tooth needs scrapers of diverse forms, many shapes, according to what is prepared for your operation; therefore, because the scraper with which a tooth is scraped from the lower part is different from the scraper with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to other forms.
And these forms of scrapers should be prepared beforehand, which you see.
Chapter 30.
Concerning the extraction of aching teeth.
It is necessary that you treat a tooth from its pain with all skill, and delay in its eradication. For there is nothing which supplies its place when it is eradicated: since it is a sublime substance: until when there is no excuse for its eradication, then it is necessary when the infirm person is compelled to eradicate it, so that the aching tooth is steadied and made certain for you.
For often pain deceives the infirm person, and he thinks that it is in a sound tooth and eradicates it: then the pain does not depart, until the infirm tooth is eradicated.
For we have already seen that from the operation of phlebotomists.
Therefore, when the aching tooth itself is made certain for you, then it is necessary that you scarify around the tooth with a spatula, in which there is something of virtue, until the gum is loosened from every part: then move it with your fingers, or with fine forceps at first gradually, until you shake it.
Then place large forceps on it in a good position, and the head of the infirm person between your knees: which you have now held well, so that it does not move: then pull the tooth straight, so that you do not break it.
If therefore it does not come out, then take one of those instruments, and insert it under it on every side easily, and attempt its movement, as you did at first.
But if the tooth is perforated, or corroded: then it is necessary that you fill that hole with cloth: and bind it with the end of a thin rod, so that it is not broken at the time of your constriction upon it with forceps.
And it is necessary that you act exquisitely with scarification around the gum on every side abundantly.
And guard your strength, so that you do not break it, and a part of it remains, and returns upon the infirm person from it a malignancy which is greater than his first pain.
And beware, lest you do that which ignorant phlebotomists do in their haste, and boldness over its eradication, beyond what we have recounted.
For very often they cause to happen to men great infirmities, which are more difficult to break, and its roots all remain, or some of them: or that they extract it with certain bones of the jaw: as we have many times testified to that.
Then let it be rinsed after its eradication with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
But if a flow of blood happens from the places (for it often happens) then grind something of zegi, and fill the place with it: and if not, cauterize it if zegi does not benefit you.
The shapes of the fine forceps with which the tooth is first moved, let them be of long ends, short handles, thick, so that they do not bend when pressure is applied with them to the tooth: let them be like you see of thick handles, until when you squeeze with them, they do not yield themselves, nor bend: of short ends, and let them be made of good iron, or of steel decently made, of straight ends: and at their ends let there be teeth, of which some enter into some, and grip with decent, strong constriction.
And sometimes the ends are made like the shape of the mouth of a bird which is called a stork, and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter XXXI.
Concerning the eradication of teeth, and the extraction of fractured jawbones.
When, in the eradication of a tooth, something of it remains broken, then it is necessary that you place a cautery with butter on the place for one or two days, until the place is softened: then insert into it a searcher, or forceps: the ends of which resemble the mouth of a bird which is called a stork. This then is the shape of the forceps.
Let their ends be made like a file on the inside; or like aliskilfegi.
If therefore it does not obey you to come out with these forceps, then it is necessary that you cauterize above the root, and uncover all the flesh, and insert the instrument which is called a small lever, of which this is the form.
Let the end be short, somewhat thick; and let it not be brittle, so that it may not break.
If therefore the root comes out by means of that, it is good: and if not, it is necessary to help with these other instruments whose forms are these.
The shape of the first is triangular at the end, in which there is a certain thickness.
The shape of the end is a thin triangle.
And with other instruments and tools which preceded in the matter of teeth.
Also let there be help with this instrument which is similar to a large hook, of this form, with a twisted triangular end, in which there is some thickness, so that it is not broken: and let it not be soaked.
And know that the instruments for teeth are many: and similarly the remaining instruments are not counted, and a skilled craftsman, subtle in his art, finds for himself an instrument, according to what the operation and the illnesses themselves signify to him.
Because there are illnesses for which the ancients did not describe an instrument, because of the diversity of their kinds.
But if a bone is broken from the jaw, or from other bones of the mouth, or rots: then investigate it in its place with that which is suitable to it from the instruments and forceps which I mentioned in the extraction of roots, and help with a probe, whose form is this.
Let there be some gradual thickness in it, so that the bone may be tightened or held with it, and not escape and move, until the bone comes out: and restore the place with medicines suitable to it.
But if there is a small rottenness in the bone, then shave it from its rottenness, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter XXXII.
Concerning the sawing of teeth prominent above others.
When teeth project not according to their natural course, an ugly shape is produced by it; and especially when this happens in women and girls, namely, those who are for sale.
Therefore it is necessary that you consider. For if a tooth is grown behind another tooth, and is not located, or its sawing or filing is possible, then eradicate it: and if it is joined with another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, of which this is the form.
It is similar to a small beak, and is made of tempered iron, of a very sharp extremity.
And let your incision, by which you cut it, be in many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that another of the teeth is not moved.
But if it is prominent, exposed to its filing: then file it with a file of tempered iron, of which this is the form.
Let the whole be of tempered iron, and its handle of very small points, like a file with which needles are made: with which the tooth may be filed little by little in many days easily, so that the tooth is not moved, and falls out: then smooth it, finally scrape it with some scrapers.
But if it is a tooth, from which a part is broken, and injures the tongue: then it is necessary that you file it also, until the roughness of that fracture is removed, and is made even: and is smoothed, and does not injure the tongue, nor corrupt the speech.
Chapter XXXIII.
Concerning the fastening of teeth with gold threads.
When a shaking and movement happens to the front teeth from a blow or a fall: and the sick person cannot bite on what he eats so that it does not fall: and you have treated them with astringent medicines, and the treatment has not availed: then the method is that they be fastened with gold thread: because silver turns green, and rots after days: but gold is never changed.
And let the thread be medium in thinness and thickness according to the amount that it takes between the teeth.
And the manner of fastening is that you take a thread, and insert a double of it between two sound teeth; then weave with the two ends of the thread between the moved teeth, whether it be one or more, until you arrive with the weaving to a sound tooth of the other part. Then repeat the weaving to the part from which you began: and tighten your hand with subtlety and wisdom, until it is not at all removed: and let your constriction with which you tighten the thread, be at the roots of the teeth, so that it does not escape.
Then cut the superfluous ends of the thread with scissors: and join both, and twist them together: and hide them between the sound tooth and the moved tooth, so that it does not injure the tongue: then let it remain so tight as it is.
But if it is loosened, or cut, tighten them with another thread, and it will stand with it in this way the whole time.
And this is the form of the teeth, and the figure of the fastening of two sound teeth, and two moved teeth, as you see.
And sometimes it happens that one or two teeth return to their places after the fall of both, and are retained as we have described, and remain: and this is only done by a skillful, prudent, and experienced craftsman.
And sometimes something from the bones of animals is fitted: and it is made like the shape of a tooth, and is placed in the place from which the tooth is strengthened and tightened, as we have said: and it remains, and serves for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Sometimes this ligament which happens under the tongue is either natural, with which a man is born, or accidental, from a wound which has already healed.
And the operation in it is that you open the mouth of the sick person at his head in your lap, and raise his tongue: then incise that nervous ligament across, until the tongue is released from its restraint.
But if in it there shall be something of hardness and knotting, and it shall be from the healing of a wound: then throw into it a hook, and split it across, until you incise the ligament, and the knotting is loosed.
And beware, lest there be a fissure in the depth of the flesh, and you incise an artery there, and a flow of blood happens.
Then let the place of incision be washed with rosewater, or with vinegar, or cold water.
Then place under the tongue a small linen cloth, which the sick person should hold throughout the night: so that it does not heal together again.
But if a flow of blood happens, then place pounded alum on the place. But if the blood overcomes you, then cauterize the place with a sluggish cautery, which is suitable for it: then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
Sometimes an abscess occurs under the tongue which resembles a small frog, preventing the tongue from its natural function; and perhaps it becomes so large that it fills the mouth.
And the operation in it is that you open the mouth of the patient in front of the sun: and consider the abscess.
If therefore you see it of a dark or black color, hard, to which the patient feels no sensation: then do not touch it, because it is a cancer.
And if it inclines to whiteness, in which there is moisture, then throw in it a hook, and incise it with a fine spatula, and free it from every part. But if blood overcomes you at the time of your operation, then put on it crushed alum until the bleeding stops; then return to your operation, until you remove it completely.
Then wash it with vinegar and salt: and treat it with the remaining treatment suitable for it, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of an abscess of the two tonsils.
Chapter XXXVI.
Sometimes abscesses occur between the throat glands, that is, glands which resemble the glands which occur externally: and they are called the two tonsils: which when you treat with what we have said in the division, and they are not healed, then consider.
If therefore the abscess is hard, dark in color, and has little feeling: then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is red in color, and its root is thick: then do not touch it even with iron, fearing a flow of blood, but rather leave it until it matures. And then either you will pierce it, or it will break by itself.
And if it is white in color, round, and its root is thin: then it must be incised.
And the operation in it is that you consider before its operation if its hot abscess has quieted completely, or is diminished by some diminution: then therefore make the sick person sit before the sun, and let his head be in your bosom: and open his mouth, and let a servant take [something] between his hands, and press his tongue downwards with an instrument, of which this is the form.
Let it be made of silver or bronze, which is as thin as a knife.
Therefore, when you press the tongue with it, and the abscess is shown to you, and your sight falls upon it: then take a hook, and fix it in the tonsil, and draw it outwards as much as you can, except that you draw with it something from the tunics.
Then incise it with an instrument, of which this is the form which follows. It is similar to scissors: however, its ends should be curved, so that the opening of each of them is directly opposite the other sharply: therefore take care that it is made of good iron, or imbued with alfuled.
But if this instrument is not present, then incise it with a spatula, of which this is the form. Let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other.
And after you incise one tonsil, incise the other according to this same kind of incision.
Then after the incision let the sick person gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
