But if a flow of blood happens to him, let him gargle with water in which pomegranate rinds have been boiled: or myrtle leaves, and things similar to them from among styptics, until the flow of blood is stopped: then cure him, until he is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses are produced in the throat, besides the two tonsils. Therefore incise them according to what I said in the incision of the two tonsils equally.
And I indeed was already treating a woman from an abscess which had formed between her throat, which was pulling towards blackness, little sensation, which had almost closed her throat: and the woman was breathing through a narrow passage, and it had already prevented her from eating, and drinking water: and she had already come to death, if she had remained one day, or two days.
And the abscess had risen so much that it had produced two branches, until they came out above the two openings of the nose.
Therefore I hastened, and fixed a hook in one of them. Then I pulled it, and a large portion was drawn out from it.
Then I cut it off where I grasped it from the opening of the nose.
Then I did the same to that which protruded from the other opening of the nose.
Then I opened its mouth, and pressed its tongue; and afterwards I fixed a hook in the abscess; then I cut off part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it: therefore the woman's throat was cleared, and she immediately began to drink water, then she took some food.
Therefore I did not cease to incise that abscess many times for a long time: and the abscess kept coming in the place of what I incised, until it was excessively prolonged and with it that.
Therefore I devised, and cauterized the abscess between the throat.
Therefore it stopped from increasing; then I went away, and I did not know what God did with her after me.
Concerning the incision of an abscess of the uvula.
Chapter XXXVII.
When catarrh descends to the uvula, and becomes an abscess, and is long: then it is called perpendicular; and if it is thick and round in the lower part, then it is called a grape.
When it is treated with what we have said in the division, and the treatment does not help, and you see that the warm abscess has subsided from it, and it is thin: then it is necessary to incise it.
And what is gathered from it, round, and not long, and is bloody, or brown in color, or black, and has no feeling: then it is necessary to avoid its incision. For in it there is fear for the patient.
Therefore, when you see it according to the manner I have described, from its whiteness and length, you should make the patient sit before the sun: and press his tongue with the instrument, the description of which has preceded: then fix a hook in the uvula, and pull it downwards: and incise it with one of the two instruments which I have described in the incision of the two tonsils.
And it is necessary that you not incise from it, except that which is added to the natural limit without addition.
For if you incise more of it, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then after the incision, use what we have described in the incision of the two tonsils: and treat it until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by the incision: then it is necessary that you use skill in cauterizing it without fear and terror.
And the method of cauterization in it is not, except with a sharp instrument; and it is, that you place the patient's head in your bosom. Then press his tongue with the instrument that we have mentioned. Then take of the water which I told you in the chapter on cauterization, and mix with it some quicklime not slaked, and put it neither thick nor thin, and fill with it the concavity of this instrument, of which this is the form.
Let there be at its extremity in which the medicine is placed a concavity, like the concavity of a spoon's bowl.
And place the instrument with the medicine upon the uvula itself: and let the patient lie upon his side, so that the saliva may run from his mouth from the inside of the medicine, so that nothing from it may descend to his throat, and harm him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you shall keep it upon the uvula for the amount of half an hour, until you see it already blackened, and the biting of the medicine calmed.
And if you wish to take a pledget, and wrap it upon the extremity of a probe, and soak the pledget with medicine, and insert the probe with the pledget into the cannula from above, until the pledget adheres to the uvula.
Do this many times, until you obtain what you wish of the cauterization of the uvula. Then leave it: for it itself dries and falls after three days, or four.
But if you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it: and after cauterization wipe around the uvula with a pledget soaked in butter, and cleanse with it what is around it of the medicine: and let it be rinsed with cold water.
And let it be treated externally with lotions, and internally with gargles, until it is healed.
Therefore this method of treatment is healthier than incision and further from fear.
And sometimes the uvula is also cured with something finer than cautery and incision: for it is cured with fumigation, in this way. Take calamint, and hyssop, and origanum, and rue, and sage, and chamomile, and southernwood, and similar herbs: and mix them all, or some of them, in a pot, and let them be submerged with vinegar, and boil: and let the pot be properly luted with clay: and let there be a hole in the middle of the pot, over which a concave instrument is placed in this way.
Let it be made of silver or bronze: and let the end, in which there is a pomegranate, be inserted into the mouth of the sick person, until the vapor ascends to the uvula through the tube, until the uvula is much darkened.
Then repeat over it many times, until it dries.
And beware, lest you do this cure at the beginning of the arrival of the abscess. For it often adds to the abscess: and it is not fitting that you do it except at the decline of its warm abscess.
But if this instrument is not present to you, then take a tube, and place at its end an eggshell: so that the mouth of the sick person is not burned: because the eggshell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth: and this is of good cure with health.
Concerning the extraction of thorns and bones from the throat.
Chapter XXXVIII.
Often a bone, or fishbone, or something else sticks in the throat.
Therefore it is necessary that you extract what of them is apparent upon which the sight falls, after you press the tongue with an instrument towards the sun, and it is made clear to you what is in the throat.
And what does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, it is necessary that you make the sick person vomit before the food is digested in the stomach. For perhaps the attached thing will come out through vomiting.
Or let the sick person swallow a piece of turnip, or a root of lettuce, or swallow a piece of dry bread: or let him take a piece of soft dry sea sponge: and tie it to a thread, then let him swallow it. When therefore it arrives at the place where the spines, or bone, are, and is extracted, then it comes out.
If therefore it does not come out with what we have said: then use an instrument of lead according to this form.
Let it be somewhat thicker than a radius, and at its extremity let there be a curve, which he may easily insert into his throat: and let him have his head raised upwards.
And let him beware of touching his epiglottis, lest a cough should befall him, and drive the bone or spine with it, or let the physician insert it with his hand.
But that the patient himself insert it, is better: because of his knowledge of the place of the attached thing: and let him push downwards, or draw his hand upwards with the instrument, all of it according to the quantity which is prepared for him, until it comes out.
Concerning the extraction of a leech.
Chapter 39.
When a leech is medicated with what I have mentioned in the division of treatment with medicines, and it does not help, then consider in the patient's throat before the sun, or in the sun after you have pressed his tongue with the instrument which I have described to you.
If therefore your sight falls upon a leech, extract it with a small hook, or with fine tweezers decently.
But if it is not possible with these: then take a concave cannula, and insert it into the patient's throat as far as the throat: then insert into the concavity of the cannula a piece of iron heated with fire. Do this many times: and let the patient abstain from water the whole day.
Then let him take a glass full of cold water, and open his mouth in it: and let him rinse his mouth with it, and not swallow any of it: and let him move the water one hour after another with his hand. For the leech immediately falls when it feels the water.
But if it does not come out with what we have narrated, then fumigate the patient with bugs, or with asafoetida with the instrument which I narrated in the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this many times, because it itself will fall.
And the method of operation in fumigation is that you take a pot in which there are live coals with fire: and the pot is covered with a cover, in the middle of which is a hole: and the end of the instrument is placed in that hole.
Then throw in the fumigation, and let the patient put his mouth on the end of the tube, and close his mouth so that the fumigation does not escape, until you know that the fumigation has now reached the leech. For the leech will immediately fall.
But if it does not fall, then repeat the fumigation many times.
And let the patient endure thirst, and eat salt, and garlic, and not drink water.
Therefore it is necessary that it come out with this regimen.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the leech is extracted from the throat, when it falls upon the uvula.
It is similar to forceps, as you see: except that it has this curve which is inserted into the throat. And its extremity is similar to the beak of a bird which is called a stork, in which there is a roughness like a file: when it grasps something, it does not let go of it at all.
Concerning the opening of abscesses, and their incision, and treatment in general.
Chapter 40.
There are many kinds of abscesses, of many types, according to what comes to mind of them, namely one and one in this book: and they differ in their perforation, and incision of them in two ways.
One of which is from the species of abscess in itself, and that which it contains of humors.
And the second method is because of the places in which it occurs in the body.
Because an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the anus: and an abscess occurring in a fleshy place is different from an abscess occurring in the joints: and each of them has its own judgment from the operation.
And among abscesses there are some which it is not necessary to perforate except after the maturation of the pus in them, and its completion: and there are some which it is necessary to perforate while they are raw, and not matured according to completion.
Such as abscesses which are near the joints.
Because when an abscess occurs near a joint, and its being prolonged until that which is around it putrefies, perhaps the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted: wherefore it is a more proximate cause of the contraction of that member.
Or there is an abscess near a principal member: because if you delay its perforation until it matures, it will harm either it, or through it the principal member.
Or it is near the anus, therefore perforate it immature: and it is not necessary to perforate a raw abscess, of incomplete digestion, except that which is near the anus, lest it putrefy deeply, and extend to the interior of the anus, and become a fistula, or become in its extremity that which is not healed.
It is necessary that you know the time of perforation of an abscess, which is matured according to completion: and it is with the sedation of pain, recession of fever, and diminution of redness, and pulsation, and acuteness of the head of the abscess: and the other signs.
And it is necessary that you make the same perforation in the lower place of the abscess, and of more vehement prominence: and let the perforation go in the length of the body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands and feet, and places of muscles, and tendons, and nerves, and arteries, and lastly in all equal places, in which there is no reflection.
But in those which are reflected, proceed with the perforation according to that member.
When however the abscess is in fleshy places: then it is better that you delay its perforation, until its digestion is confirmed, as we said according to completion.
Because if you perforate it before that, the course of the virus from it is prolonged, and there is much dirt and filth, and perhaps its lips and its depth are hardened.
And some abscesses are perforated according to the breadth of the body in necessity: or according to that which the member requires.
And it is necessary that you administer in small abscesses a small perforation, and in large abscesses a large perforation: or many incisions according to the likeness of the magnitude of the abscess.
And there are some abscesses in which it is necessary that the skin is hollowed out: and incised when it has already become like a cloth, and has become to the point that it is already dead: like that which happens in many, like boils and buboes.
And some of them are those which are cut with a three-angled incision. And some of them are those from which is incised like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Like the abscess of the groin.
And some of them are those in which a round incision and a lunate incision are administered: and similar to them from among the incisions.
That, however, which does not have a head, such as are soft superficial abscesses, it is necessary that you perforate it with a superficial, or only a flat, perforation.
And it is necessary, when an abscess is large, and has already collected much pus, and you perforate it, that you do not hurry and extract all the pus at that time: rather, extract from it a part of it. Then obstruct the abscess until another day. Then extract something of the pus again.
Do this many times according to a gradual process, until it comes out: especially if the patient is weak in strength, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old, decrepit person; for the animal spirit is often dissolved from the body with the exit of pus: and perhaps the patient dies, and you do not perceive it.
Therefore beware of this chapter with great caution.
And after your perforation, by which you perforate these abscesses, it is necessary that you cleanse the wound: and consider. If therefore the wound of the abscess is small, or the incision is flat, then apply linen cloths, or old tow. And if the abscess is large, and the fissures of the perforation are many, then it is necessary that you insert a linen cloth into each fissure, until they meet each other.
And if in the abscess you have cut away a part of the skin, or have hollowed it out around: then it is necessary that you fill it with old tow, or as when cloth is woven, or is shaved with linen lint without moisture: and bind it tightly until the third day.
Then remove it, and treat it with what is necessary of ointment, until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs during your operation, then apply cold water and vinegar, after a cloth is soaked in them: and apply it to the place of bleeding many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then it is necessary that you administer the regimen, and treatment, and powders, which I have described in many places of this our book, and from the division.
But if it is in winter time, and the place of the abscess is of many nerves: then it is necessary that you infuse small pillows with warm wine and oil: and place them on the places.
And if it is in summer, and the places are fleshy: then it is necessary that you apply small pillows soaked in water and oil, or in wine and oil, and all of it cold. Until when it will be the third day, as we have said, then it is necessary that you open the abscess, and cleanse it, and administer in its treatment that which is suitable for it, until it is healed.
This therefore is what is necessary from the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses universally.
According to the way of distinction, however, I have already spoken of every abscess, in what way is the path to its treatment individually in chapters.
Chapter XLI.
Concerning the incision of abscesses which occur in the skin of the head.
Small abscesses occur in the skin of the head, and they are of the kinds of Nodes, which contain tunics, which are sacs for them: as if they themselves were the wattles of a hen.
And their kinds are many.
Some of them are fatty; and some of them contain a moisture similar to mud; and some of them contain a moisture similar to phlegm and porridge; and similar to them; and some of them are hard, stony.
And in the incision of all of them, and their extraction, there is no fear, as long as an artery does not oppose their incision.
And the operation in their incision is that you should explore them with an instrument, whose form comes after this, which is called an Intromissum, until you know what it contains.
For if it is that which contains moisture, then incise it with a longitudinal incision, a superficial incision: according to this form.
And begin with an incision from line b to line g. When therefore the moisture is evacuated, then scrape the cyst which contains that moisture, and do not leave anything of it at all.
For very often it returns, if anything of it remains.
Then dip a pledget in Egyptian ointment, if it is present; and if not, in water and salt; and fill the wound with it, and leave it until another day. For it will corrode what remains of the cyst.
Then repeat the application of a pledget with Egyptian ointment a second and a third time, and if you need it, until you are confident that nothing of the cyst remains; then treat the wound with ointment until it heals.
But if the abscess contains a fatty node, then incise it in this manner, making a cruciate incision; and draw the hooks between the lips of the incision, and scrape it from every part; and exert your skill in extracting the membrane which contains it.
But if an artery opposes you, then do what I shall relate to you later.
And similarly act in an abscess, if it is stony, by the same incision and treatment, according to what we have said.
And the incision on a stony abscess is easier, because it contains little blood and moisture. And sometimes there are some of these abscesses which are in the head in certain men, with which there is entirely no moisture.
That is: because I incised an abscess on the head of an old woman, and I found an abscess like a solid and hard, rough, white stone, which could not be broken; and if anyone were struck with it by throwing, it would wound him.
In other abscesses however occurring in the head, besides these namely abscesses which occur in the heads of children, and near the roots of the ears, the incision of all of them should be a superficial incision.
Or place their perforation always from their lower part, so that the flow of pus is relieved downwards.
Then treat them with what is suitable for them from treatment.
Concerning the incision of the Strophulae in the neck.
Chapter XLII.
Often these abscesses occur in the neck, and under the armpits, and in the groin; and sometimes they occur in the rest of the body.
And concerning those which occur in the neck, there is one, and many; and they are generated from one another; and every strophula among them is within its own tunic, just as is in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have narrated.
And the kinds of these strophulae are many. And among them are stony ones; and among them are those which contain humors; and among them are malignant ones, which do not obey treatment.
Therefore, in that one among them which you see of good disposition to the touch, whose apparent color is close to that of the skin, and which moves in every direction, and is not attached to the nerves of the neck, nor to the jugular vein, nor to an artery, nor is deep: you ought to cut it with a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body along a line, b, to line, g, and flay it from every side: and extend the two lips of the skin with a hook, or with many hooks, if you need that, as we said in the abscesses of the head, and extract it gradually.
And it is important to be cautious, lest a vein or nerve be cut.
And let the scalpel not be very sharp: lest it injure your hand with the incision, or disturb the patient, and you cut what is not necessary.
But if you cut a vein or an artery, or something prevents you from operating, then place on the wound crushed alum, or some powders which staunch blood, and bind the wound, and leave it until the acuteness of the abscess subsides, and the wound softens: or it begins and wants to fester.
For the blood is stanched. Therefore return to your operation, until you complete it.
Then investigate with your index finger if something remains of the little pustules or other small things; therefore incise them and cleanse them.
But if there is a large vein at the root of the pustule: then it is necessary that you not incise that pustule from its root; indeed it is necessary that you tie it with strong doubled thread, and leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old lint, which you have already submerged in Egyptian ointment. Then take care of it.
If the whole pustule is incised, then it is necessary that you bring together the two lips of the wound; and stitch it up immediately, after you see that absolutely no excess remains.
But if you see that excess remains because of the size of the pustule, then it is necessary that you strive to incise its root and extract it: and administer the suture which we have described.
And those pustules which contain fluids, perforate them with a superficial perforation: so that the place of their maturity may appear to you: and place the perforation from that which follows lower in the body, as we have said.
Then after the perforation administer a pledget with ointment, and similar things, so that it corrodes what remains of the corruption, until when you see the wound to be now cleansed: then take care of it with flesh-generating ointment, until it is healed.
Chapter 43.
Concerning the incision of the epiglottis from an abscess occurring within the throat.
The ancients have spoken of this incision in the epiglottis, and I have not seen anyone in our land who has done it: and this is the writing of their account.
However, in those having synanche, the incision of the epiglottis should be avoided: since no benefit is gained by it; because all the veins and the lung are weak.
In those, however, who have a hot abscess in the mouth, or throat, or in both tonsils, when there is no illness in the windpipe, it is necessary to use incision of the epiglottis, to avoid the destruction which occurs from suffocation.
Therefore, when we wish to do it, we should incise the epiglottis below the three or four circles of the epiglottis, with a small incision across, in that which is between two circles, to an extent that the incision is in the membrane, not in the cartilage: and this indeed is a suitable place for the incision, since it is free of flesh, and the blood vessels are far from it.
But if the physician is timid, then it is necessary that he extend the skin of the throat with a hook. Then let him cut the skin until he reaches the windpipe; let him avoid the blood vessel, if he sees any of them.
Then let him cut the membrane which we have described: and the incision of the windpipe is indicated by the phlegm which comes out of it, with that which is broken, and by the loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Therefore, when the time in which suffocation is feared is removed, bring together the two lips of the wound in the skin, and sew it alone without the cartilage.
Then administer medicines which cause flesh to grow, until it is healed.
Says Albucasis. The sum of this discourse which we have narrated is an exposition only when they see that some of these abscesses has already obstructed the throat of the sick person: and the sick person is close to death, and his breathing is to be cut off: they proceed to cut the epiglottis, so that the sick person may breathe through the place of the wound with a certain respiration, and be saved from death: and for this reason they have instructed to leave the wound open, until the malignancy of the sickness is consumed, and its malignancy is for three days, and as if by itself: then therefore they instructed to sew the wound, and to cure it, until it is healed.
And that which I saw with my own eyes is that a maidservant took a knife, and put it into her throat, and cut with it a part of the windpipe.
And I was called to her cure, and I found her bellowing, like the bellowing of the dying.
I uncovered therefore the wound, and I found the blood which had come out from the wound to be little. Therefore I was confident that she had not cut a vein, nor the gullet: and wind was coming out from the wound.
I hastened therefore, and sewed up the wound, and I treated it, until it was healed: and nothing happened to the maidservant, except hoarseness in her voice: and she returned after days to a better condition.
Hence therefore I say: that in the cutting of the epiglottis there is no fear.
Concerning the incision of a throat hernia.
Chapter 44.
This abscess, named a throat hernia, is a large abscess, according to the color of the body: and it is found much in women.
And it is according to two ways: for it is either natural or accidental.
In the natural one certainly there is no pus.
The accidental one however is according to two ways: of which one is similar to fatty nodes. And the other way is similar to the abscess which happens from the knotting of an artery. And in it there is fear, and it is not necessary that presumption be made to it with iron, except in that which is small: and you prove it, and investigate it with a probe. If therefore you find it similar to a fatty node, and it is not connected with any veins: then cut it, as nodes are cut: and extract it with what contains it from the cyst, if it is in a cyst: and if not, then explore the whole of it. Then treat the place with what is necessary of treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter XLV.
Concerning the incision upon the types of nodes.
There are many kinds of nodes, and I have already described all their kinds in the division: and it is necessary that I relate here the difference between a node and an excrescence, since it causes doubt.
I say therefore, that when an excrescence is hot, and feverish, and with violent pains, until the boiling over of superfluity is settled, and putrefaction is complete: then indeed the fever and heat are quiet.
With a node however there is no heat, nor fever, nor pain, and it contains them in a  kystis sifachin, which is its own sac, and is according to the colour of the body: and its beginning is like a chickpea, and it becomes like a batecha, both larger and smaller.
And they are of two kinds: either fatty: or containing moisture. And the colours of the moisture are many, as I said in the division.
It is necessary therefore, when you arrive at the treatment of a node, that you examine it, and investigate it first with an instrument which is called an Intromissum, according to what its form will be in the chapter which is after this.
And the method of investigating abscesses and all Nodes is, that you take this instrument, and insert it into the moistest place which you find in the abscess: and you revolve your fingers with it gradually, until you know that the instrument has now penetrated the skin. Then move your hand according to the measure of the size of the abscess.
Then withdraw the Intromissum, and consider that which comes out in its track.
If therefore a running moisture comes out, whatever color it may be: then cut it with a superficial incision, according to what I said in the other abscesses.
And if no moisture comes out in the track of the Intromissum, then know that it is fatty: therefore cut upon it with an incision having the form of a cross, according to this form, as I taught you: and suspend it with hooks, and flay the skin from every part with ease: and keep the kistin if you can over it, so that you may extract it whole with the Node.
But if the kistis bursts during the operation, and it is not possible to extract it whole, for this often happens, then extract it piecemeal, until nothing of it remains.
For if a little or much of it remains, the Node returns all the more.
But if it overcomes you, and a little remains of it: then fill the wound at your completion with certain sharp corrosive powders: and bind the wound, and place upon it what sits upon a warm abscess, and treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Therefore if the Node is large, then sew its two lips, and treat it with that which incarnates.
But if you have a pulsating or non-pulsating vein opposite: then hasten, and fill the place with crushed zegi, and leave it bound for two or three days, until the wound putrefies, and the boiling of the blood rests: then return to incising what remains of the Node.
Chapter XLVI.
Concerning the instruments for incision and perforation.
Of these are the shapes of the intromittents, and they are of three kinds.
For of them there is a large one, and of them there is a medium one, and of them there is a small one.
Let them be made of alfulud iron with square ends decently, so that they hasten entry into the abscess.
And these are the shapes of the explorators, and namely, that is, perforating: and one of them is called beris, that is, perforating: and they are of three kinds.
For of them there is a large one, and a medium one, and a small one.
They are suitable for investigating by means of them abscesses, and exits, and fistulas, and buboes from what is between them of bones.
And others are made round, smooth, soft, like large needles with which chairs are sewn, namely of citrine, or of bronze, or iron, or silver.
And better are those which are made of citrine.
And probes are also made of black lead, which are suitable so that by means of them fistulas may be explored, whose concavity has reflections, so that they may be reflected with their softness along with those reflections.
And they are again of three kinds. Because among them are long ones, medium ones, and short ones, according to the size of the fistula and its stricture.
The shape of hooks, and they are of many kinds.
Because among them are simple ones, namely those which have one curve: and they are of three kinds as you see, large, medium, and small.
And among them are blind hooks: and they are of three kinds.
And among them are hooks having two curves, and they are of three kinds: and among them are hooks having three curves, and they are of three kinds: and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the shapes of scalpels, with which nodes and abscesses are cut and scraped: and they are of three kinds: for among them is a large, a medium, and a small one.
Their ends with which the cutting is done are sharp, the other ends are not sharp: and they are not placed in this way, except so that with them help is given in scraping nodes for fear of cutting a vein or nerve: and so that the sick person may be healed by them, and find some tranquility from the burning which he finds in the scraping of an abscess.
They are made of bronze, and are similar to a spatula with which alcohol is made: and at the broad end is a hidden spatula-shaped point: and it runs into the interior and to the exterior when you wish, as you see.
The shape of the spatulas which are hidden between the fingers in the perforation of abscesses: and the sick do not perceive them: and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
The shapes of the cupping glasses with which the flow of blood is cut off: and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
Let them be made of bronze or brass, round, somewhat long, as you see: and let them be thin.
And it is necessary that these kinds of cupping glasses be with you, large and small: so that you may cut off blood with speed by means of them when necessary, and then when medicine is not present to you.
However, do not use them in cutting off blood in every place of the body, and do not use them except in fleshy places: such as the thighs of the leg, and the hip, and the thighs of the arms, and the breasts, and the belly, and the groin, and similar to them, from fleshy, moist thighs.
And sometimes small instruments are made from them, similar to the husks of nuts, according to their form: with which blood is cut off when it flows from the place of phlebotomy, or at the incision of a vein, or artery: and sometimes they are made round according to this form.
Chapter XLVII.
Concerning the care of the breasts of men which are similar to the breasts of women.
Sometimes the breasts of some men are inflated upon the completion of sleep (that is, when they begin to be polluted in sleep) until they are similar to the breasts of women: and they remain foul and abscessed.
Therefore, he who abhors that, it is necessary that he cut above the breasts with a lunate incision, according to this form from line b to line g. Then flay all the fat. Afterwards fill the wound with incarnative medicine. Then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture, and care for it until it is healed.
But if the nipple declines to the lower parts, and softens because of its size, as happens to women: then it is necessary that it be incised on its two upper sides with two incisions similar to the lunar figure, each of which is connected with the other at the ends of each, until there is a larger line containing a smaller one, according to this form from line b to line g. Afterwards flay the skin which is between the two lips, and remove the fat, and administer what we have said of suture and powders, which is necessary for the treatment, until it heals.
But if you do not complete the incision of what you ought to incise, because of the patient's restlessness, or because of the flow of blood: then it is necessary that you fill the wound with a pledget soaked in corrosive ointment, and leave it until it corrodes what remains of the fat. Then care for it until it heals.
Concerning the perforation of an abscess which occurs under the armpit.
Chapter XLVIII.
Those abscesses which occur under the armpit are of the hard kinds of strumae, which contain cysts, and of those which contain humours.
Therefore those which contain humours ought to be incised with a lunate incision, according to this form, from line b. to line g.  When therefore all that is in the abscess of pus is evacuated, then fill it with old lint, and leave it until another day.
Then let the lint be removed, and let it be treated with a suitable ointment for it, until it is healed.
But if the abscess be of the kind of nodes of strumae, then incise upon it, as we said in the section on strumae equally.
But if the wound extends so that it discharges pus, then apply a cautery to it, according to what we have said before.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the incision of an abscess which occurs because of an artery and vein.
When an artery is ruptured, and the skin which is above it is consolidated, then an abscess frequently results from this; and similarly it happens to a vein also, that an abscess and swelling occur in it.
And the signs by which it is known that the abscess and swelling are because of an artery and vein are: because, when the abscess is because of an artery, it is long, gathered deep in the body: and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel as if there is a kind of creaking in it.
And that which is because of a vein, is round on the surface of the body.
And incision upon this abscess is a mistake, and especially that which is under the armpits, and groins, and neck, and in many places of the body: and it is very large. Therefore it is necessary that its treatment with iron be avoided, and that which of them is also in the extremities, or in the head, it is necessary that it be avoided.
Therefore, upon that which of them is from the swelling of the opening of an artery, make in the skin an incision lengthwise. Then open the incision with hooks, afterwards the artery: and free it from the tunics, until it is uncovered.
Then insert under it a needle, and make it penetrate to the other side, and tie the artery with double thread in two places, according to what I taught you in the extraction of the two arteries which are in the temples.
Then puncture the place which is between the two ligatures with a spatula, until all the blood which is in it comes out, and the abscess is resolved.
Then administer treatment which generates pus, until the ligatures fall off: afterwards treat it with ointments suitable for it, until it is healed.
But if there is an abscess because of a section of a vein, then it is necessary that you hold with your hand as much as you can of the abscess with the skin. Then insert a needle under the place which you held with your hand, and make it penetrate: and let there be in it double thread, until you extract it from the other side. Then tie the abscess with a good ligature: according to what I said to you in the ligature of the grape, on two sides with two ends of thread.
But if you fear that the thread will be pulled out, then insert another needle with another thread under the whole abscess at the incision of the needle: press, and tighten your threads in four places.
Then cut the abscess in its middle, until when that which is in it comes out: then incise the superfluity of the skin, and leave that which is bound of it.
Then place upon it a small pillow which you have submerged in wine and oil. Then administer the treatment, which is done with linen cloths and ointments, until it is healed.
Chapter L.
Concerning the abscess which happens from the twisting of the nerves.
Just as an abscess happens in an artery and vein, similarly an abscess happens in the nerves: which happens in them through percussion, or happens from excessive labor, and similar things; and it happens most in the places of the joints, or the heel, and in every place where the joints move: and it is a serious abscess.
Its color is similar to the rest of the body, and it is in most dispositions without pain: and when it is squeezed with force, the sick person feels in it something like numbness.
And it is not an abscess gathered deep in the body, but rather it is under the skin, and it moves to every part, and does not go forward, or before, nor backward to the posterior.
Therefore, what is among them in the joints, ought not to be invaded with iron. Perhaps contraction will happen.
But in that which is among them in the head or on the forehead, incise the skin with a spatula. If therefore the abscess is small, then grasp it with forceps and cut it away from the root.
And if it is large, then suspend it with a hook and flay it: then remove it and close the wound with suture: and treat it until it heals.
Chapter 51.
Concerning the incision of an abscess which occurs in the abdomen.
It happens to some men many times in their bellies, and in the rest of their bodies,  thelil, which are called Fungals, because of their similarity to fungi.
Whose root is thin, and their head is thick, and their lips are inverted. And some of them are small; and some of them become very large.
And indeed I myself have seen a man, and in his belly were two thelil, which were more similar to fungi than to other things: they were without difference, white, with a small root, and their lips were inverted, and they were split, and moisture always flowed from them.
Therefore I cut them out, and I found one weighed about eighteen ounces and the other about six ounces.  The procedure in cutting them out is that you should consider then if the patient is moist, and the color of the thelil is white, moist, with a thin root: then cut it out with a broad spatula, and have cauteries in the fire at hand.
For very often much blood is expelled during its excision; therefore, if the blood overcomes you, then cauterize it quickly.
But if you see the patient is faint and afraid of incision with iron, then take a properly made lead thread, and tie the thelil with it: in the manner we have described, and leave it for two days.
Then add to the lead ligature. Therefore do not cease tightening the lead, whenever it presses on the thelil, until it is cut off and falls off by itself without effort.
But if the thelil has a thick root, then there is something to fear and dread in its excision; especially if it is in the abdomen.
However, it is possible to incise half of it. Then cauterize it, so that it does not return.
Then treat it until the wound is healed.
And beware lest you presume to incise a thelil which is brown in color, insensitive, and foul in appearance: for it is a cancerous abscess; and I will deal with the remembrance of Cancer after this.
Chapter 52.
Concerning the treatment of an umbilical hernia.
An umbilical hernia arises from many causes. Or from fissures of the peritoneum, which is over the abdomen; and from it protrudes fat, or intestine, according to what happens in other ruptures. Or from blood flowing from a vein or artery, as has been stated before. Or from wind constricted there.
But if it is because of a fissure of the peritoneum and the protrusion of fat, then its color is similar to the color of the body; and it is soft, without pain; and it appears in various places. And if it is because of the protrusion of the intestine, then its location, with what we have narrated, is of more varied difference. Therefore, when you press it with your fingers, it is hidden; then it returns, and perhaps there is a sound with it; and it is greatly increased at the entrance of the bath, and with vehement labor.
But if it is because of moisture, it is soft; then it is not hidden when you press it with your hand, nor constricted, nor is it increased.
But if it is because of blood, then with these signs an abscess appears tending toward blackness.
But if it is because of newly formed flesh, then it is a serious, large, hard abscess, and is fixed according to a single quantity.
But if it is because of wind, then its touch is soft.
And the operation in that is that you should then consider if the protrusion of the umbilicus is because of the blood of an artery or vein, or wind; then you must refrain from its treatment. For in that there is fear and dread, as I taught you in the chapter in which I spoke of abscesses due to an artery and vein.
But if the protrusion of the umbilicus is because of the intestine or a hernia, then you must instruct the patient to hold his breath and stand upright and extended. Then mark with a cautery around the whole umbilicus. Afterwards instruct him to lie on his back between your hands. Then encircle with a broad spatula around the umbilicus over the place you marked with the cautery. Then extend the middle of the abscess upwards with a large hook. Then tie it around firmly with thread, or with a strong silk cord; and let the knot of the ligature be secure.
Then open the middle of the abscess extended above the ligature. If therefore you find it already caught by the ligature, then pull it out carefully, and insert your index finger into it and push the intestine into the interior of the abdomen; and if you find a polyp, then seize it with a hook, and cut away its excess.
But if an artery or vein is opposed to you, then surround it abundantly. Then return to your operation. And take two needles, and thread them with two strong threads; and insert the two needles into the circumference which you made around the abscess in the manner of a cross, which you make penetrate.
Then tighten the abscess in four places over the needles; and if you wish, remove the needles, and leave the place until the extended flesh putrefies and falls off by itself; or incise it when it becomes thin and putrefies.
Then treat it with whatever ointments are necessary until it is healed.
But if the prominence of the navel is from raw flesh in it, or from moisture, then it is necessary that you hollow out the abscess, as we said. And extract the blood, or moisture, which you find in it.
Then treat it with what solidifies the place.
Chapter 53.
Concerning the treatment of cancer.
We have already spoken in the division of the kinds of cancer, and how the way to its cure is with medicines: and caution is to be observed with its treatment with iron, so that it is not ulcerated: and we have spoken of cancer generated in the womb, and caution concerning its treatment.
And the ancients said that sometimes cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible: such as cancer which is in the breasts, or in the hip, and similar to them, from the members situated so as to extract the whole of it: especially when it is beginning small.
When, however, it is old and is large, it is not necessary to approach it.
For I have not been able to cure anyone of it: nor have I seen another before me who has succeeded in it.
And the operation in it, when it is situated as we have said, is that you should precede, and loosen the diseased part from the black bile sometimes. Then you should bleed it if you find it with manifest repletion.
Then prepare the patient with a preparation in which he is situated with the operation.
Then throw hooks suitable for it into the cancer. Afterwards hollow it out from every part with the skin, according to exploration, until nothing remains of its roots: and let the blood run, and do not cut it quickly, but rather press the places, and wipe away all the thick blood with your hand, or with whatever is possible there of instruments.
But if the flow of thick blood from the incision of an artery or vein opposes you, then cauterize the vein until the blood is stopped. Then care for it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of dropsy.
Chapter 54. We have already narrated in the division the types of dropsy, and how the collection of water occurs: and the signs of each type, and its treatment with medicines.
And that which is treated with iron is only a type of ascites alone: and it is not approached with iron in the case of tympanites: nor in the case of hyposarca at all: for that is deadly.
When you have already treated this type of dropsy, tympanites, with medicines, and your treatment has not helped: then consider. For if the patient has already suffered weakness, or has another illness besides dropsy: such as if he has a cough, or diarrhea, and things like that: then beware of treating him with iron: because there is fear of that.
But if you see a patient of exuberant strength, and there is no illness with him besides dropsy alone: and he is neither a child nor an old man: then the method of operation in him is that you raise the standing patient between your hands, and a servant behind him presses his belly with his hand, driving the water to the lower part of the abdomen.
Then take a spiny spatula, according to this form. Let it be of two sharp points, long, the sharp end like a spatula. However, let there be a certain shortness in it gradually, so that you do not pass through with it in the operation as far as the intestine, and injure it.
Then consider. For if the generation of dropsy is from the part of the intestines, then it is necessary that you lengthen with the incision from the navel the quantity of three fingers, to the lower parts directly above the pubis.
But if the generation of dropsy is because of a liver ailment, then let your incision be to the left of the navel by three fingers.
And if its generation is because of the spleen, then let the incision be on the right side: and it is not necessary also that the incision be on the side on which the infirm person wants to lie, so that superfluities do not run to that weak place.
Then perforate the whole skin with an instrument. Then insert into that incision, and raise your hand with a spatula between the skin and the fat, as if you are flaying it: and let the quantity which is flayed be the quantity of a nail, or thereabouts.
Then perforate the fat, until the spatula reaches the empty place: and it is the place of the water. Then extract it, and insert into the hole an instrument, whose form is this. And it is similar to a tube: it is made of silver, or bronze, or brass, smooth, polished: which in its lower part has a small hole, and on its sides are three holes, two on one side, and one on the other: and its end is incised, according to the manner of the incision of a reed, as you see: at its upper end is a ring.
For when the instrument reaches the water, then it immediately descends upon the instrument, therefore evacuate from the water in an hour a moderate quantity.
Because if you evacuate from it more than is necessary, in an hour perhaps the sick man dies through the dissolution of his animal spirit: or syncope befalls him which approaches death.
Truly evacuate from it according to the quantity of his strength, and according to what the conditions of the sick man signify to you from the strength of his pulse, and from the goodness of it.
Then withdraw the instrument, and retain the water.
Which is, because it is immediately retained because of the skin which retains the hole which is above the sifac: of which I have spoken, that you may perforate it according to that method.
Then repeat the instrument on the next day, if you see the sick man tolerate it: and also draw out from the water a small quantity.
Do this for several days without fear, and guard yourself from error: until there remains of the water only a little.
But if you fear for the patient, and leave a large amount of water, then treat him with burial in warm sand, and much sweating in a bath and sun, and let him endure thirst: and treat him with drying medicines, until he is healed: and cauterize over the stomach, and liver, and spleen, after the extraction of the water: according to what I have related in what precedes.
Concerning the care of infants who are born with their urinary places not perforated.
Chapter 55.
When an infant comes forth from the womb of its mother, and the orifice of its penis is not perforated, or the opening is narrow, or not in its proper place.
Therefore it is necessary to help with its perforation immediately upon birth with a very fine spatula, according to this method.
Then place a thin lead nail in the opening and bind it: and keep it there for three or four days.
When therefore he wishes to urinate, let it be removed, and he will urinate. Then replace it.
And if you do not place lead, it is possible that the urine which flows over the place will not allow it to close.
But he whose opening is narrow, is treated with lead, as we have said, for many days, until it is widened.
But those in whom the opening is not in its proper place, are injured. That is because in those who are born, the opening is at the end of the penis: wherefore he cannot urinate forwards, until he raises his penis with his hand upwards. Nor is there generation for him, because the semen cannot reach the womb in a straight line: and it is a very foul disease.
And the method of operation in this case is that the patient is laid on his back. Then extend the head of his penis with your left hand with a strong extension, and incise the head of the penis from the place of the glans, with a sharp knife or scalpel: like the incision of a reed, or like you shave something, so that its middle becomes similar to the head of the penis: and so that the opening falls in the middle as is necessary.
And beware in your operation of a flow of blood. For that often happens. Therefore oppose to it that which stops blood, and treat the wound until it heals.
Concerning Albucasis, what happens in the foreskin and head of the penis, and corruption, blackness, and adhesion.
Chapter 56.
These things often happen to the penis according to Albucasis, and there is a protrusion of foul flesh.
And of it, one is malignant, and another is not malignant.
Therefore, the non-malignant one should be suspended with a thin hook, and cut away until you cleanse it entirely. Then place upon it lint soaked in Egyptian ointment. Then treat it afterwards with diafinicon ointment until it is healed.
But if the Albucasis is malignant, of foul color, then it is necessary to apply a cautery to it after its incision and shaving.
But if the Albucasis is in the foreskin of an uncircumcised captive, and part of the Albucasis is between the foreskin and part of it outside: then it is necessary to remove the Albucasis which is inside first, until when it heals, then treat the outside: because when you treat it simultaneously, the foreskin is not secured from being perforated.
And now blackness and corruption also occur in the testicles and foreskin. Therefore, it is necessary that you hollow out all that which is already blackened and that which wants to be corrupted, or is already corrupted.
Then spread over it afterwards with honey which is already mixed with the flour of pomegranate rinds and of crushed, sifted herbs.
Then treat it with the remaining treatment until it is healed.
But if a flow of blood occurs, then apply a cautery with a lunar cautery, according to this form.
For a cautery is helpful for two conditions at once, namely for a flow of blood, and a wound when it is corrupted.
But if the head of the penis is corroded and the whole thing is destroyed in an hour: then it is necessary that you insert into the meatus of the penis a small tube of lead, so that the sick man may urinate over it.
In the adhesion, however, of the foreskin with the head of the penis, and this adhesion indeed does not happen except in one whose foreskin is healthy, and circumcision is not required on it: and sometimes adhesion happens because of a wound, or abscess: therefore it is necessary that you scrape it with a blunt spatula, until the ligament is loosened, and the head of the penis is freed from every part.
But if it is difficult to distinguish them precisely, then it is necessary that you scrape something from the head of the penis, and from the foreskin. Which is because when the foreskin is thin, perhaps it is perforated because of its thinness quickly.
Then separate the foreskin and the head of the penis with a thin linen cloth, which has already been moistened in cold water, so that they do not adhere again.
Then it is treated with astringent wine, until it is consolidated.
Chapter 57.
Concerning the cleansing of infants, and the treatment of accidents happening to them.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity, like other wounds; nevertheless, since it is from our operation, our will, and its use is properly in boys, it is necessary that we mark in it a better operation, and a lighter way leading to health.
Therefore I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books: because they do not use it in their laws, and it is only what we have acquired through experience.
That is, because I have found the majority of artisans and phlebotomists using cleansing, circumcising with a razor and with scissors; and they use cautery, and binding with thread, and incision with a nail.
And I indeed have already tested all these methods, and I have not found better than cleansing with scissors and binding with thread.
Because in cleansing with a razor, the skin often escapes. Because the foreskin has two layers: therefore perhaps the upper skin is incised, and the lower layer remains; therefore it is forced to an incision to incise the other, and future pain. And with the cleansing which is done by cautery, that is, a finger-stall, which tailors use, the incision of the extremity of the virile member is not secured: because perhaps it enters its hole.
In cleansing moreover with a fingernail, perhaps the iron escapes, and corrupts your operation: and indeed the skin of a boy is short in nature, and very often they are born such that they do not need cleansing: and I have already seen that.
The cleansing moreover with scissors, and ligature with thread, revealed a good experiment to me: because scissors are proportional in incision: therefore, because the sharpness which is above, is like the sharpness which is below. When therefore you press your hand, two sharpnesses are proportioned, and they incise according to one operation in one time: and a compression is made in a similar figure, containing the skin of the penis from every part, with which no error falls at all.
And the method of the proper operation is, that you cause to be circumcised, a boy especially, if he is from those who understand a little, that you do not do, except that you tie a thread around his penis only: and you leave him until another day.
Then make him rejoice and be glad in every way that is possible for you, from that which his intelligence receives. Then make him stand between your hands of prepared stature, and let him not be sitting: and hide the scissors in your sleeve, or under your foot, lest the boy see them at all, nor anything of the instruments.
Then insert your hand to his penis, and inflate the skin, and draw it upwards, until the head of the penis emerges. Then cleanse it from the filth that has gathered in it.
Then tie the marked place with a double thread. Afterwards tie a second ligature a little lower down. Then hold with your index finger and thumb the place of the lower ligature with a good grip, and incise between the two ligatures. Then raise the skin quickly upwards, and draw out the head of the rod. Then let the blood flow gradually (for it is better) and put powder on top: and over the powder a cloth in which the yolk of a hard-boiled egg has been spread, beaten with good fresh rose oil: and leave it on until the next day: and treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Now the shape of the forceps for cleansing is this.
Let the two ends be blunt, and let them meet: lest a branch of a nail protrude in them: and let the length of the two sharpened parts be equal to the length of the two handles equally.
An error however occurring in cleansing is: because perhaps the whole inner skin, or a part of it, escapes at the incision.
Therefore it is necessary that you extend it from your hour with your fingernail before suppuration of the place: and incise it evenly.
But if you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then draw it out with a hook, and incise it.
If however three days pass, and what is under the rod remains inflated, suppurated: then leave it until the warm abscess settles, and flay it easily, and incise it as is prepared for you: and beware of the head of the rod. For that will not harm it.
Therefore treat it with that which consolidates a wound from the powders, which we have recounted in the treatise on powders: and if it is incised from the skin beyond measure, and extends to the upper parts, that also does not harm it.
Therefore treat it with what we have said, until it is healed.
Chapter 58.
Concerning the extraction of urine retained in the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder occurs from an obstruction caused by a stone, or congealed blood, or pus, or a growth of flesh, and similar things to those which we have mentioned in the divisions of treatment.
And if the urine is not discharged, and you see that its retention is due to a stone which is now in the neck of the bladder: then it is necessary that the patient be bent over on his knees. Then let his feet be placed upon his back, and let him extend his belly from the upper part of his back, and let the patient hold his breath as much as possible. The neck of the bladder is thus forced, for the expulsion of the stone to the rear: wherefore the urine is discharged.
But if it is not discharged by what we have said, and the matter is urgent for the patient: then it is necessary that you administer its extraction with an instrument, which is called a syringe, of which this is the form: Let it be of silver, and let it be slender, smooth, concave like the quill of a bird's feather in the slenderness of a ray, long in the likeness of one and a half palms: to which there should be a slender tube at its head.
And the method of drawing off the urine by means of it is that you take a doubled thread, and tie wool, or lint, to its end with a good ligature: and introduce the end of the thread into the lower part of the syringe: and cut off with scissors if anything of the wool is superfluous, but introduce it into the tube forcefully, like an auger.
Then anoint the syringe with oil, or with butter, and the white of an egg: and make the patient sit upon a seat, and moisten the bladder, and its neck with moist oils, and olive oil, and warm water.
Then introduce the syringe into the neck gently, until it reaches the root of the neck.
Then bend it upwards from the umbilical region.
Then push the syringe inside, until it reaches the bladder, and the patient feels it has now reached the empty thing: and this is not done in this order, except because the passage, into which it goes, is one which has a tortuous course.
Then extend the thread with wool gradually. For the urine will follow the wool.
Then withdraw it, and make the urine run through the urinary passage; and repeat with the syringe, and do not cease doing this, until the bladder is emptied, and the patient finds relief.
Concerning the quality of bladder irrigation with a Clepsydra.
Chapter LIX.
When an ulcer occurs in the bladder, or blood congeals in it, or pus is constricted in it, and it is necessary to instill waters and medicines into it: then let it be done with an instrument which is called a Clepsydra, and this is its form.
For it is deaf, that is, closed. And in it are three openings, two on one side, and one on the other, as you see.
And the concave place, in which is that which impels, should be according to the quantity which fills it without addition, until when something is drawn with it, the moisture is drawn: and when it is impelled, it is expelled with it to a distance: according to what the clepsydra does, by which naphtha, that is, Greek fire, is projected in the battles of Mars.
Therefore, when you wish to project moisture into the bladder, insert the end of the clepsydra into the moisture, and draw with it that which impels upwards, for the moisture is drawn into the concavity of the clepsydra.
Then insert its end into the tube, as we have described in the syringe. Then impel the moisture with that which impels it; for that moisture arrives at the bladder immediately, so that the infirm person feels it.
The form of a slender clyster by which the bladder is clysterized.
Let it be made of silver or brass: its head should be higher, similar to a small  alcama, and below it a sinus into which a ligature falls.
Then take a ram's bladder and put into it the moisture with which you wish to clysterize the bladder. Then tie it between the two sinuses with a strong ligature of double thread, and warm that moisture a little over the fire.
Then insert the end of the clyster into the rod, afterwards squeeze your hand over the bladder with the moisture with a proper constriction, until the patient feels that the moisture has already reached the bladder.
But if you do not have a bladder at hand, then take a piece of parchment and make a circle from it, and pierce it all around its circumference. Then insert a strong thread into those holes, as they are rolled. Then gather the circle, as the alsofratu is gathered; after you place in it what you wish of moistures, oils, and waters.
Then tie it to the instrument: and do with it as you did with the bladder by pressing with your hand, until the moisture reaches the bladder.
Chapter 60.
Concerning the extraction of a stone.
Now we have spoken in the division of the kinds of stone, and its cure with medicines; and I have spoken of the difference between a stone generated in the kidneys and bladder, and the signs of all this; and I have spoken of the stone in which operation with the hand and incision falls; and it is a stone generated in the bladder properly; and that which is fixed in the urethra; and I am narrating this with manifest exposition and brevity.
Therefore I say that a stone generated in the bladder happens most to boys.
And among its signs is that urine exits from the bladder similar to water in its thinness; and white sand appears in it, and the sick person rubs his penis, and labors with it, and often it hangs down. Then it is erected, and the anus comes out with it in many of them.
And the healing of children is easy, until they reach fourteen years. And it is difficult in old men. But in young men it is moderate, in that which is between that; and of him whose stone is larger, the cure is easier; and if small, the treatment is the opposite of that.
Therefore, when we come to the treatment, it is necessary first that we cleanse the patient with a clyster, which draws out all the dung that is in his intestines; for it sometimes prevents the finding of the stone on examination.
Then let the patient be taken with his feet up, and shaken, and moved downwards: so that the stone descends to the bottom of the bladder, or rises from a high place sometimes.
Then make him sit between your prepared hands, and his hands shall be under his hips, so that the bladder may be wholly sloping downwards.
Then examine him, and touch him externally.
If therefore you feel the stone in the space, then hasten at once with the incision upon it.
But if it does not fall under your touch at all, then it is necessary that you wipe the index finger with oil of the left hand, if the patient is a boy: or the middle finger if he is a full-grown youth: and insert it into his anus, and search for the stone, until it stands under your finger: and turn it gradually towards the neck of the bladder.
Then press upon it with your finger, and impel it outwards towards the place, of which you wish to make an incision: and order the assistant to press the bladder with his hand, and order another assistant to draw up the testicles with his right hand: and with his other hand to draw away the skin which is under the testicles in the part from the place where is the incision.
Then insert the cutting spatula, whose form is this. And make an incision in that which is between the anus and the testicles, and not in the middle, to the side of the left buttock: and let the incision be upon the stone, and your finger be in the anus: and let the incision be transverse, so that the incision be wide externally, and narrow internally, according to the amount that the exit of the stone from it may be no larger.
For perhaps the finger, which is in the anus, compresses the stone at the incision, and it comes out without difficulty.
And know that of a stone which has angles and edges, therefore its exit is made difficult because of that.
And of that which is smooth, similar to a gland, and round: and its exit is made easy.
Therefore to that which has angles and edges, add to the fissure.
If therefore it does not exit thus, then it is necessary that you exert force upon it: or that you bind it with a suitable bandage, whose extremity is like the moon, which binds upon the stone, and does not slip from it: or that you introduce beneath it a subtle instrument, of curved extremity.
But if you cannot [do this] upon it, then enlarge the opening a little. But if something of blood overcomes you, cut it away with a scalpel.
If however the stone is more than one, then push the largest first toward the opening of the bladder. Then incise upon it. Afterwards push the small one after that: and similarly do if there are more than two.
But if it is very large, then it is ignorance to cut upon it with a large incision. Because one of two things happens to the patient, either that he dies, or that continuous urinary dripping occurs to him; because of this,  because the place does not completely consolidate.
Truly administer its expulsion, until it comes out; or devise its fracture with forceps, until you extract it piecemeal.
Therefore, when you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with frankincense, and aloes, and dragon's blood: and bind it, and place upon it a cloth infused with oil and wine, or rose oil, and cold water: so that the hot abscess may be seated.
Then lay the patient on his back, and do not loosen the bandage until the third day. When therefore you loosen it, moisten the place with water and much oil.
Then treat it with diafinicon ointment and basilicon ointment until it is healed.
But if a hot abscess occurs in the wound, and corrosions, and things like that: as if blood congeals in the bladder, and urine is prevented from passing from it: and this is known from the passing of blood with the urine. Therefore insert your finger into the wound, and draw out that blood. For if it remains, it provokes corruption of the bladder and its putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt: and apply to every kind of wound what is appropriate for its treatment, until it is healed.
And it is necessary during the hours of the entire treatment to bind the two hips together, and bring them close, because of the medicines that are placed on the area.
But if the stone is small, and is in the passage of the penis, and is fixed in it, and the exit of urine is prevented: then treat it with what I relate before you arrive at incision. For many times you will be excused from incision by this treatment, for I have already proven it.
And it is so that you take a perforator of iron, whose form is this, with a triangular point, sharp, fixed in wood.
Then take a thread, and tie with it the rod below the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then introduce the perforating iron into the penis easily, until the perforator arrives at the stone itself: and turn the perforator with your hand in the stone gradually: and attempt its perforation, until you make it penetrate on the other side.
For the urine is immediately released.
Then press your hand upon that which remains of the stone from the outer part of the penis. For it is broken, and exits with the urine, and the patient is healed.
But if this treatment is not prepared for you, because of something which prevents it: then tie a thread under the stone, and another thread above the stone. Then cut above the stone in the penis itself between the two ligatures. Then remove it. Afterwards loosen the ligature, and clean the congealed blood which forms in the wound.
And the ligature of the thread under the stone is not necessary, except that it may not return to the bladder: and the other ligature above, except that when the thread is loosened after the removal of the stone, the skin may return to its place, and cover the wound: and for this reason it is necessary for you, when you tie the upper thread, to raise the skin upwards, so that it may return to its place upon your completion, and cover the wound, as we have said.
Chapter LXI.
Concerning the extraction of a woman's stone.
A stone is rarely generated in women.
But if a stone should happen to one of them, then the cure is difficult, and is forbidden for many reasons.
One of them is that the woman is perhaps a virgin.
And a second is, because you do not find a woman who will reveal herself to a physician, when she is chaste, or has a husband.
A third is, because you do not find a woman well-skilled in this art, especially operating with the hand.
And a fourth is the distance from the location of the stone: wherefore it needs a deep incision, and in that there is fear.
But if necessity compels to it, then it is necessary that you take a woman physician, well-skilled: and few are found.
If however you deprive her of [a woman physician], then seek a chaste, skillful physician, and present a well-taught midwife skilled in women's matters, or a woman who has some understanding in this art: therefore make her present, and especially instruct her to do all that you order her from the examination over the stone.
First what is, that she should look if the woman is a virgin: then it is necessary that she insert a finger into her anus, and seek the stone.
If therefore she finds it, let her compress it under her finger, and then instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the vulva of the weak woman, and inquire about the stone, after she places her left hand upon the bladder, and compresses it with a good compression.
If therefore she finds the stone, then it is necessary that she gradually move it from the opening of the bladder to the lower parts with the utmost of her strength, until she arrives with it to the root of the hip.
Then let her cut upon it at the juncture of the middle of the vulva at the root of the hip, from whichever part it is prepared for her, and she feels the stone in that part: and her finger should not be removed from the stone, compressing under it: and let the incision be small at first. Then let her insert a probe upon that incision.
Then let her insert a probe upon that incision. If therefore she feels the stone, then let her add to the incision according to a proportionate amount so that the stone exits from it.
And know that the types of stones are many. For among these there is a small one, and a large one, a smooth one and a rough one, a long one, and a round one having branches.
Know therefore its types, so that it may be signified to you by this concerning that which you wish.
If therefore blood overcomes you, then add in the place pounded alum, and hold it one hour, until the blood is stopped.
Then return to your operation, until the stone comes out: and do so that you prepare with you of the instruments, which I mentioned in the extraction of the stone in men, that you may be aided by them in your operation.
But if the flow of blood overcomes you, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from the artery which has been incised: then place powder upon the place, and bind it with a suitable bandage: and do not change that: and leave the stone, and do not extract it. Perhaps for the weak woman will die. Then treat the wound.
Therefore when the sharpness of the blood is settled after days, and the place is putrefied, then return to your operation, until the stone comes out.
Chapter 62.
Concerning the incision for watery hernia.
A watery hernia is nothing but a collection of moisture in the white sheath which is under the skin of the scrotum containing the testicle; and it is called a sheath.
And sometimes it is in its own membrane, which nature has prepared for it in part of the testicle, until it is putrefied; which is another testicle, and is the skin of the scrotum between the white sheath which we have mentioned; and this does not happen except rarely.
And indeed the generation of this hernia is from weakness which happens to the testicles; wherefore this matter is poured out to them. And sometimes it happens from a blow upon the testicles.
And this moisture has many colors. For either its color inclines to yellow, or it is red like blood, or fecal, black, or watery white, and this is what is most common.
And the signs by which it is known where the collection of water is, are these. For if it is in the white sifac which we have spoken of, then the abscess is roundish, somewhat long like the shape of an egg: and the testicle does not appear, because the moisture contains it on every side.
And if the moisture is in its own tunic, then the abscess is round with a part of an egg, and because of this a person thinks that it is another egg.
And if the moisture is between the skin of the testicles and the white sifac, then it falls under the sense.
When therefore you wish knowledge of the color of the moisture, then test the color of the moisture in the abscess with the introduced square, whose form has preceded. According to that therefore which comes forth in the track of the introduced [instrument], judge concerning that which is between [the surfaces].
When therefore we come to treatment with iron, then it is necessary that you order the patient, that he be bled if it is possible for him, and you see his body full.
Then let him be laid on his back upon something a little high, and place many cloths under him.
Then sit you on his left, and instruct the assistant, that he sit on his right, who shall extend his rod to one of the two sides of the scrotum's skin, and to the part of the flank of the belly.
Then take a broad spatula, and cut the skin of the scrotum in the middle lengthwise, as far as near the pubis: and let the incision be made according to the straightness of a line equidistant which divides the skin of the scrotum, until it reaches the white membrane enclosing [the testicle]: and flay it and observe, or beware of this, that you do not cut it: and let your flaying, with which you flay it, be from the part which hangs with the testicle more: and subtly search with the flaying according to the amount that is possible for you.
Then perforate the membrane full of water with a wide perforation, and draw out all the water.
Then separate between the two lips of the incision with hooks, and extend the membrane to the higher parts, and do not touch the skin enclosing the testicle: and incise the membrane however it is possible for you to incise it, either with its totality, or piecemeal, especially its thin side. For if you do not exquisitely search, in its incision there is no security, lest the water return.
But if the testicle proceeds outwardly from its skin during your operation: then when you have completed the incision of the membrane, replace it. Then bring together the two lips of the skin of the testicles with a suture.
Then treat it like other wounds, until it is healed.
But if you find the testicle corrupted by blackness or something else, then it is necessary that you ligate the vessels which are its suspensory ligaments, fearing the flow of blood.
Then cut away the testicle with its suspensory ligament and remove the testicle; afterwards treat it as we have said.
But if water has collected in two parts, then know that there are two fistulas; therefore cut the other part as you did the other equally.
And if it is equal to you, so that there is one operation, then do it; then wipe away the blood. And insert into the incisions wool soaked in oil, or in rose oil; and place outside another wool which you have soaked in wine and oil, and spread it over the two testicles and the scrotum; and place doubled cloths on top, and these are compresses; and bind them on top with a bandage which is six fingers wide according to this form.
Then two cloths are taken, and sewn, and filled with carded wool, according to this likeness; and let the ends by which it is compressed be made of tow or wet wool; and let them be fastened with a bandage around the loins; and one end should reach above the anus to the back, and the second should reach to the groin; and two ends below to the two hips, and two other ends to the two groins, and let the whole bandage be fastened around the loins.
