And sometimes this fistula is treated surgically with a cautery in place of incision with an iron; and it is that you take a thin, knife-like cautery, and incise the skin of the testicles with it; and it is heated, as we have related, until when white pus containing water is revealed: then take a cautery, according to this form.
It is similar to a Greek letter.
Then perforate that pus with it, and it is heated, until all the moisture comes out.
Then extend the pus with hooks, and scrape it with a thin, heated cautery, and incise it as much as possible for you, until you eradicate all of it.
And beware of the egg, lest the fire touch it.
And know that this operation is safer, and further from blood flow, and better than the operation with incision.
But if a hot abscess, or blood flow, or something else causes you difficulty during the incision, or during cauterization: then it is necessary that you treat what causes you difficulty with the treatment that is appropriate for it, according to what has been said before.
If, however, the patient is timid and does not expect this operation because of its horror, then administer perforation as I relate it: and it is.
When it is ascertained by you through what we have said about the signs, that the fistula is watery, then it is necessary that you make the patient sit on a raised seat.
Then perforate the abscess in its lower part with a wide spatula; and place the perforation according to the length of the body, and let the opening be wide, until all the water runs out.
Then place a pledget over the place, and bind it, and leave it so that the remaining water may run out; then treat it until the wound is healed.
But if there is opposition to the egress of water at the time when you perforate it, and this is only because a white membrane makes opposition in the orifice of the wound and prevents the exit of the water, then it is necessary that you introduce a feather into the wound, or an instrument with which the water of dropsical patients is extracted, the form of which has preceded; or add a little in the operation of the wound.
And know that the water returns and gathers after six months, or thereabouts. Therefore, when it gathers, then let it be perforated in the same manner, and the sick person will pass his days.
Chapter LXIII.
Concerning the treatment of fleshy fistulas.
Know that in the incision upon this fistula, there is fear leading to destruction in many conditions. Therefore, it seems to me that its dismissal is to be saved from it.
And I am recalling the operation on it, and the appearance of the abscess.
Therefore I say that many abscesses occur in the bodies from which the testicles are composed; and this happens from many causes, namely.
Or from an excess of something sharp or acrid, which is poured out to the testicles. Or from a blow.
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body, and no pain is found in it: and it is a serious abscess.
And perhaps it is a stony abscess, whose color is dark, and there is no sensation in it.
And sometimes it happens from a knotting and swelling of the arteries, as its mention has already preceded: or from a swelling of the veins.
Therefore, that which is from a swelling of the arteries is recognized by the disposition of the abscess when you compress it with your fingers. And it is not necessary that you touch it completely.
That which happens from a swelling of the veins, however, does not disperse anything from it upon compression with the fingers.
And now it is possible that an incision be made upon this type and upon the remaining flesh: and it is so that you cut the skin of the testicles. Then extend the testicle upward, and extract it with the white sheath; and free the spermatic cord from the vessels, and ligate the vessels; and incise the spermatic cord after you free it from every part of the parts of the testicle.
If therefore the testicle is consolidated with those newly formed tissues, then it is necessary that you pull the testicle and incise it.
And if there is adhesion between some part of the sheath, or in that which is between the vessels, then it is necessary that you free all that adhesion and incise it with a circular incision.
But if there is a growth of flesh in the place of adhesion which is behind, then it is necessary that you incise all of it and extract the testicle, as we have said.
Therefore, when your operation is completed, then fill the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and wine; then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter LXIV.
Concerning the treatment of the fistula which is with a vine.
A fistula is a swelling enveloped in a certain wrapping, similar to a bunch of grapes, with softening of the testicles; and it makes movement, exercise, and walking difficult for the patient.
And in the treatment of this illness there is fear, because of the recollection of what preceded it.
Truly it is necessary that we speak of the operation in it according to what the first ones did.
I say therefore that it is necessary that you make the patient sit on a raised seat. Then push the suspensory of the testicles downwards. Then hold the skin of the testicles with your fingers, with the vessels which are near the penis: and let another assistant hold them, and extend them with a strong extension.
Then incise with a broad, sharp spatula with a transverse incision in the direction of the vessels, until the vessels are revealed.
Then flay from every side, as we told you in the extraction of the arteries which are in the temples.
Afterwards, fix in them a needle in which there is a double thread, and tie it in the first place where the veins meet: and tie it again in the last of them. Then cut them in the middle with a straight incision according to the length of the body, and extract what is gathered from foul, corrupt humors.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds, in which pus is generated, so that the vessels which nourish one testicle fall away. For there is no harm through it.
But if a vein affects all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles be extracted with the vessels: lest the egg be deprived of nourishment, because of the incision, and waste away, or dry up, and there be no help with them.
Chapter 65.
Concerning the treatment of intestinal fistula.
The outcome of this fistula is from a section which occurs in the sifach extended over the abdomen in the groin region from the mirach of the abdomen.
Therefore the intestines descend from that rupture to one of the testicles. And this rupture is either from the sifach, or from its extension.
And these two types occur from many causes: either from a blow, or a jump, or a shout, or lifting a heavy object, and similar things.
And the sign, when it is from the extension of the sifach. Is that it happens gradually over a long time, and does not happen suddenly: and it is an equal abscess towards the deep; therefore, because the sifach compresses the intestine.
And its sign when it is from a section of the sifach, because it causes a vehement pain suddenly from its beginning: and it is a diverse abscess, appearing under the skin nearby: and that is because of the exit of the intestine, and its course to the exterior of the sifach.
And sometimes it exits with the intestine zirbum: and this is named intestinal Ramex, and zirbia: and sometimes it happens with flatulence.
And sometimes the stool runs into the intestine, and is retained there: and with it is the destruction of the infirm; because it causes a bad pain, and especially when it is pressed.
And in the treatment of the species of this illness with iron there is fear: wherefore it is necessary that the case be avoided in it.
And the method of operation is, that you order the infirm, that he may return the intestine with his hand to the interior of its concavity, if it obeys the return. Then he is laid supine upon his neck between your hands: and raise his legs.
Then extend the skin which follows the skin to the upper parts, and cut the whole skin of the testicles lengthwise. Then fix hooks in the two lips of the incision, according to what you need to open the incision with them: and let the incision be of such a size that, if possible, the egg may come out of it.
Then flay the siphak, and what is under the skin of the testicles, until when the white siphak is uncovered, hard on every side: then insert your index finger into that which follows the testicle, into that which is between the white siphak, which is under the skin of the egg, and the second siphak, and absolutely loosen with it the adhesion which is in the posterior part of the egg.
Then move with the right hand towards the interior the skin of the testicles: and with this extend the white siphak upwards, with the left hand: and lift the egg with the siphak to the part of the incision: and instruct the assistant to extend the egg upwards: and you loosen the adhesion which is behind, the solution being complete.
And search with your fingers, that there may be nothing there of the intestine involved in the hard white siphak. But if you find anything of it, then push it down to the belly.
Then take a needle, in which is a thick thread, made of linen from ten threads: and insert it near the last siphak, that is, of the tunic, which is under the skin of the testicles which follows the incision: then cut the ends which project from the thread, until there are four threads. Then arrange some of them over others with a figure which is in the manner of a cross: and tie with them the alsifach, of which we spoke, because it is under the skin of the testicles with a strong ligature, until it cannot send anything to them from any of the vessels which nourish them: so that a hot abscess may not occur thence. And place again a second ligature outside the first ligature, less than two fingers distant from it. And after these two ligatures leave of the siphak, which is under the skin of the testicles, an amount the size of a finger: and incise all the rest according to the roundness, and remove from it the egg.
Then cut in the lower part of the skin of the testicles an incision, from which blood and pus flows: as we have related in what has been said before.
Then apply wool soaked in oil, and place it in the wound: and apply the ligature which we have described. And sometimes cauterize the white siphak which we have mentioned, after its incision fearing the flow of blood. For this often happens. And leave the ligature until it falls off by itself.
But if its falling off is delayed, then let it be moistened with warm water: and place upon it that which we have mentioned, until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with the remaining treatment of wounds, until it is healed.
Chapter 66.
Concerning a cupping instrument with a tube.
In this cupping instrument I have not seen anyone who dared to operate on it with iron.
And the first ones said that it is done as we have narrated in the cupping instrument which is with a tube. That is, to tie the vessels after the incision upon it with ease below; then cut in the middle, and finally treat it with that which opens it, until the vessels fall away: and treat the wound, according to what we have said, until it heals.
Chapter 67.
Concerning the rupture which occurs in the groins.
When a rupture occurs in the groins, as we have said, and the place protrudes, and nothing from the intestines descends to the testicles: and if it descends, it is little, and returns at all hours: truly if time is prolonged with it, a fissure is added in the sifach, until the intestines descend into the sifach.
And this happens from the extension of the sifach, which is in the groins, as we have said. That is, because the sifach is extended: then it softens, and the place becomes inflamed, and protrudes.
And its treatment is with a cautery, as its account preceded.
And sometimes it is treated with iron, according to this method: and it is, that the sick man lies on his back between your hands. Then cut the place of the rupture of the abscesses and the protruding part, with a cut across, to the amount of three fingers.
Then constrict the alsifak and what is under the skin, until when the white sifach is uncovered, which is under the skin that follows it.
Then take a radius, and place it on the place protruding from the sifach, and press it to the depth of the belly.
Then sew the two prominent places on the end of the radius from the sifach, and connect one of them with the other with a suture; then remove the end of the radius.
And do not cut the alsifach completely, nor stretch the egg, nor anything else, as I made you know in the treatment of intestinal fistulas.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds. Therefore, when you cut the threads, clean it, and restore the wound until it is consolidated.
For the sisach contracts, and no prominence is added to the rupture.
And cautery is better in this place, because it is closer to health.
Chapter 68.
Concerning the softening of the skin of the testicles.
Often the skin of the testicles is softened in some men, on account of which the flesh which is between them is softened, and its appearance is made foul.
Therefore it is necessary for him who is asked in its treatment, that he make the sick man lie on his back, and that he incise all the skin which is softened upon the natural skin.
Then let him bring together the two lips with a suture: and if you wish, sew first the superfluity of the softened skin with three sutures, or four, and make the suture firm.
Then incise between the sutures.
Then treat it with the treatment of other wounds, until it is healed, and the threads fall out.
Chapter 69.
Concerning Castration.
Know that castration is forbidden in our law: and because of that it would be permitted to me not to mention it in this my book: and its mention is not necessary except for two reasons.
One of which is that it should be in the knowledge of the physician when he is questioned about it: and that he should know the treatment of him to whom it happens.
And the other reason is that we often need the castration of certain animals, for our benefit thereby, such as rams, he-goats, and cats, and similar animals.
I say therefore that castration is done in two ways. Or by attrition. Or by fissure and incision.
Now in that which is done by attrition, the method of operation is: that we make the animal sit in warm water until its testicles are softened and relaxed, and hang down; then rub them with your hand until they are dissolved and do not appear to the touch.
But in castration by fissure and incision, it is necessary that you hold the animal, and press the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament, and cut above each testicle with one incision, until both testicles appear; then incise them after you have skinned each, and do not leave on them of the *sifak* anything except the thin *sifak* which is over the vessels.
And this method of castration is better than that which is done by attrition; because attrition perhaps leaves something of the testicles, wherefore the animal desires coitus.
Then care for the wound until it heals.
Chapter LXX.
Concerning the care of a hermaphrodite in two ways.
A hermaphrodite from men is according to two ways. One of them is because there appears in that which follows the space, or in the skin of the testicles, in that which is between the two testicles, a figure like the vulva of a woman, in which there are hairs: and sometimes urine flows from that which is in the skin of the testicles.
In women, however, there is one type, and it is the vulva of a woman above the pubis like very small male testicles, protruding outwardly: of which one is like the penis of a man, and two like two testicles.
And the treatment of the three types, two from men, and one type from women, is: because it is necessary that you cut away the added flesh, until the impression of them disappears. Then treat them with the treatment of other wounds.
In the second type of men, however, from which urine exits, which is in the skin of the testicles, there is no operation, nor is there any treatment at all.
Chapter LXXI.
Concerning the incision of the frenulum and the protruding flesh in the vulvas of women.
The frenulum is perhaps added to a natural thing, until it is deformed and its appearance becomes ugly; and sometimes it is magnified in some women so much that it expands like in men, and reaches even to coitus.
Therefore, you ought to hold the excess of the frenulum with your hand or with a hook and incise it; and do not be excessive in the incision, especially in the depth of the root, so that a flow of blood does not occur.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds until it is healed.
The flesh, however, which arises is flesh which is born in the orifice of the womb, until it fills it; and perhaps it extends to the exterior according to the likeness of a tail; and because of this some early authorities call the ailment a tailed one. Therefore it is necessary that we incise it just as we incise the frenulum, and treat it until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of an imperforate vulva, which is the non-perforated vulva of a woman.
Chapter LXXII.
Imperforate vulva is when the vulva of a woman is not perforated, or when the opening is small. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental [imperforate vulva] is from a preceding illness; and indeed it is formed either from newly grown flesh, or from thin membrane, or from thick membrane; and it occurs either in the depth of the uterus, or on its sides, or in its upper part, or its lower part; and it prevents intercourse and impregnation, and childbirth, and perhaps prevents menstruation.
And its recognition is made by the observation of a midwife, if the obstruction is apparently near.
But if it is not apparent, then seek it with your fingers, or with a probe.
But if the obstruction is from a thin membrane, and it is near the two labia, then hurry, and break it.
And it is [necessary] that you place small cushions on the two labia.
Then place the index fingers of your two hands together, and let the woman be on her back with her legs spread. Then extend the two labia with strength, until the thin membrane is broken, and the obstruction is opened.
Then take wool and soak it in oil, and place it on the area; and let the woman cohabit every day, so that the area does not heal in another way.
But if the membrane is thick, then it is necessary that you cut it with a broad spatula similar to a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is of grown flesh, then suspend it with hooks and incise it: and have with you medicines that stop the flow of blood without biting, such as acacia, and dragons blood, and frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then administer a wide lead cannula, so that the wound does not heal quickly, keep it in place for several days: and administer a pledget of dry linen, then treat it with the remaining treatment until it heals.
And sometimes the birth of other flesh occurs in the womb: therefore it is necessary that they be incised in this way: provided it is not a cancerous abscess; for a cancerous abscess which is in the womb, one should never use iron against it.
Chapter LXXIII.
Concerning the treatment of hemorrhoids, Athehelil, and Bothor rubea in the vulvas of women.
Hemorrhoids indeed are an opening of the orifices of veins, until much blood flows constantly from them.
When therefore hemorrhoids become old, they become thelil: and I have already spoken in the division of their species, and their signs: and I shall here say which of them receive treatment, and which do not.
I say therefore that hemorrhoids which are in the orifice of the womb, and do not appear to the senses: then there is no treatment for them with iron.
But those of them which are in the orifice of the womb upon which the senses fall, are those which are cured.
Therefore it is necessary that you introduce the woman into a cold house. Then extend the hemorrhoids with scissors, or with a rough cloth, and cut them from their roots.
Then pulverize upon them at the flow of blood from one of the incisive powders without mastication: such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense, and similar things to them.
Then let him push her feet against the wall for an hour. Afterwards, place her in cold water.
But if the flow of blood persists, then make her sit in a decoction of sumac, and pomegranate bark, and gallnuts, and similar things.
Then take barley flour, and mix it with honey and vinegar, and make a plaster with it upon her back.
If therefore blood is cut off with it, it is good: and if not, apply cupping glasses to the back and breasts, without scarification.
Then take wool, and immerse it in the juice of taraxacum, or the juice of borrage, or the juice of the branches of alaula, that is, aglanter, with Pontic wine, and apply it to the place.
When therefore the abscess is quiet, then treat the place with ointments, until it is healed.
Albarth however is red, similar to the heads of alhaasse, and is of harsh appearance.
It is necessary therefore that you incise what appears of it, according to what I said in thelil equally: and treat it, until it is healed.
Chapter 74.
Concerning the perforation of the exit which happens in the womb.
Many kinds of abscesses happen in the womb, such as cancer, and a stony abscess, and ulcers, and adubelati, and alekilati, that is, herpes, aestiomemus, and fistulas, and hemorrhoids, and fissures, and thelil, and a hot abscess.
And I indeed already said all these illnesses and their kinds, and their signs, and their cures in the division: it is necessary that I say in this treatise about the hot abscess which happens in the womb, when it is of the abscesses which collect pus, and you wish to know how its opening is done with iron.
Therefore it is necessary that you consider. Then if the pain of the abscess in its beginning is acute with its percussion, and inflammation, and fever: and you see it redden: if feeling falls upon it, then do not hurry with its perforation: and treat it with that which helps with maturation, until when the acuteness of the symptoms ceases.
Then it is necessary that you make the woman sit on a seat which slopes down two seats, and she should recline, or be laid on her back, and draw her feet to the lower part of her belly: and let her hips be spread, and let her put her arms under her knees, and let both be bound with a suitable ligature to that.
Then let the midwife sit on the right side, and administer the instrument by which the orifice of the womb is opened.
And it is necessary when you wish to administer this instrument, that you fix with a ray the depth of the woman's womb: so that that which enters from the instrument is no more than the depth of the womb, and harms the weak woman.
But if the instrument is longer than the depth, then it is necessary that you place small cushions on the covering of the depth of the womb: so that the entry of the whole instrument into the depth of the womb may be prevented: and it is necessary that he place the handle, which runs in the instrument, from the upper side: and that he hold the instrument, and that the assistant rotate the handle, until the depth of the womb is opened.
When therefore the exit appears, and is touched by hand, and is soft and smooth, then it is necessary that you cut it at its head with a broad spatula.
When therefore the pus is evacuated, then it is necessary that you place in the wound a linen cloth submerged in rose oil: or green oil, in which there is a certain styptic quality: and place the linen cloth, and extract the section in the depth of the womb.
And place externally on the womb, and on the pubis, clean wool submerged in water in which mallows have been boiled.
Then let it be treated after two days with an ointment which is suitable for it, until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and wound are washed, so that it is clysterized with water and honey: then with water in which the root of lily or long birthwort has been boiled: then it returns to the ointment.
But if the wound is hidden internally in the womb, then it is necessary that you refrain from its treatment with iron, rather treat it with that which we have said in the division.
Chapter 77.
On the teaching of midwives, how live embryos, having emerged in a non-natural form, are to be treated.
It is necessary for the midwife to know first the form of a natural birth.
Therefore, among its signs is that when you see a woman being compressed downwards, and she desires to breathe the air, and that which is upon her of the birth is eased, and the exit of the embryo hastens: then know that this birth is according to the natural form, and its exit is head first, and the afterbirth with it: or suspended, or hanging from the navel.
Therefore, when you see these signs, then it is necessary that her belly be compressed, so that the embryo descends quickly.
For when it descends head first, the afterbirth descends with it, and it is cleansed from those superfluities by a good cleansing.
But that which is besides this birth, is unnatural, and not laudable.
Because sometimes the fetus comes forth upon its feet, and its body comes forth before its head, and its feet, and one of its hands, or one of its feet: or its head and its hands come forth: or its belly comes forth: and perhaps it is turned upon its neck, and similar things among the unlaudable forms.
Therefore it is necessary for the midwife to be prudent, skillful in all these things, and to beware of error and deception: and I am showing every form of them, and what is the character in it, and it is signified by that, and she should attend to it.
When the fetus comes forth on its head in its natural manner, and that becomes vehement upon the woman, and the birth becomes difficult for her, and you see her strength already weakened: then make her sit upon a seat, or let women hold her: and fumigate her vulva with a decoction of fenugreek, and with warm oils.
Then let the midwife insert a small spatula between her fingers, and let her break the secundine with it, and cut it with her nail, until that which is in it of moisture flows out: and press the woman's belly, until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then it is necessary that the woman be given a clyster of fenugreek mucilage with sesame oil.
Then instruct her, after the clyster, that she be compressed; and make her sneeze with a confection, and hold her mouth and nose for one hour. For the fetus will come forth immediately.
But if two hands of the fetus come forth first, then it is necessary that you gently reduce them little by little. But if it does not obey in entering, then place the woman on a seat and raise her feet upwards; then shake the seat on the ground; and let the woman be held so that she does not fall on shaking.
Therefore if the hands do not come forth, and the fetus dies, then incise them and draw out the remainder of the fetus; and tie its hand with a cloth, and draw it out evenly: for it will come forth.
The exit of the embryo on its feet. When the fetus comes forth on its feet, then it is necessary to push them upwards; then turn the fetus little by little; afterwards straighten it.
Then let him take one of its feet, and extend it little by little.
When therefore they come forth as far as the hips, then instruct him that he be compressed, and make him sneeze with sneezing powders; for he will come forth, if God wills.
And if he does not come forth with what we have narrated, then gently return the fetus until you place it according to its natural form. For then it will come forth with an easy exit.
But if it is prevented with all that we have narrated: then take of marshmallow mucilage, and sesame oil, and dissolved gum: and pound all in a mortar with a good pounding: then smear the woman's vulva and the lower part of her belly with it. Then make her sit in warm water until it reaches her hypochondria.
Therefore, when you see that her lower parts are now warmed, then make a pessary for her of myrrh: and insert it. When therefore the pessary has remained for one hour, then make her sit on a seat. Then make her sneeze: and pinch her mouth and nose: and press the lower part of her belly with gentle pressure. For the fetus will come out immediately.
The exit of the fetus [is] over the knees and hands. When it comes out in this manner, then he will succeed in introducing his hands gradually. Then the woman will be laid back on her neck, and her feet will hang downwards: and she herself will be lying on the bed, and will clasp her hands. Then press on her belly gradually. Then remove what is under her feet from the pillows, until she is prepared in the lower part. But if the fetus does not come out, then take hold of its feet, and move them with a strong movement. Then press what is above the hypochondria gradually, until the fetus ascends to the upper parts. Then let him insert his hands and gradually straighten the fetus; and instruct the woman to bear down, until the fetus comes out.
The exit of the fetus hanging laterally from the hands. His hand will be used in the reduction. But if he cannot reduce it, then raise the woman and make her walk.
But if she cannot walk, then make her lie on the bed: and shake her legs with a strong shaking.
Then administer the liniment which you have prepared from mucilages.
But if labor seizes her, then make her sit on a seat. Then strive to reduce the hand, and adjust the fetus according to its natural form: and make the woman sneeze, and instruct her to strain herself until it comes out.
The fetus emerges headfirst, with its arms outstretched, and its face towards its mother's back. The midwife ought to hold the hand of the fetus, then gradually rotate it, and adjust it easily.
When therefore it is adjusted, then place upon her vulva that which we have described of oil and mucilages.
Then instruct her to move gradually. When therefore you have done that, then have her sit on a seat, and instruct her to lean to her right side. Then instruct her to bear down; and make her sneeze with sneezing powders.
The exit of the prepared fetus is on the side. When it descends to the orifice of the womb in this manner with the afterbirth, then tear the afterbirth with your nail until it is emptied of moisture.
If therefore it hangs over the face of the fetus, and its neck, then cut it from the navel: so that it is not retained, and dies.
Then push the fetus inwards, until you place it as is necessary: and instruct the woman to bear down several times: for it will then come out easily.
The exit of twins, or of many fetuses. Know that twins are often born: and sometimes three, and four are born. However, that is unusual, and rarely happens.
Five however is unnatural, and they do not live at all: and one afterbirth contains them, however many they are: and they are separated by a membrane, distinguishing between them, attached to the navel of each of them.
And sometimes they emerge as one fetus emerges by a natural, easy exit; and they emerge by a blameworthy exit, as we have related.
But if some of them are difficult at birth, and arrive at that which we have spoken of from unnatural forms, then treat them as we have related: and let the midwife be skillful, and let her act with speed and slowness, and let her contrive according to every form with that which is prepared for her by skill leading to health.
Concerning that which is formed in the womb from embryos, and is aborted.
Sometimes one, and two, and three, and four, and five, and six, and seven, and more than ten are formed in the womb: and it has already been verified by me, that a woman aborting emitted seven, and another fifteen, all formed.
Which was done by the measurement of the glorious wise man.
Therefore all this ought to be in the knowledge of the midwife. For perhaps a similar case will come to her: therefore let her oppose it with that which is necessary.
Chapter LXXVI.
Concerning the extraction of a dead fetus.
When you treat a dead fetus with what I have mentioned in the division of medication, and it does not come out, and you are forced to use an operation with the hand, then it is necessary that you consider.
If therefore the woman is of sound constitution, and is not afflicted with any of the accidents from which loss is feared for her: then it is necessary that the woman be laid on her back on the bed, and her head be inclined lower, and her legs raised; and a nurse should hold her from both sides, or she should be tied to the bed, so that her body is not pulled during the extraction of the fetus.
Then moisten the mouth of the womb with moist oils with mallow, fenugreek, and linseed mucilage.
Then let the midwife anoint her hand with these oils and mucilages: then let her insert it into the womb easily. Then let her seek with it the place in which to fix hooks on the fetus, and the suitable places for that.
Consider then if the fetus is prepared to descend head first: then fix hooks in its eyes, or in its neck, or in its mouth, or in its palate, or under its chin, or in its clavicle, or in places near the ribs, and under the hypochondria.
But if the descent is on its feet, then in its pubis and middle ribs.
And it is necessary that she hold the hook with her right hand: and place its curve in that which is between the fingers of her left hand: and insert it with her hand easily, and fix the hook in one of those places which we have mentioned, until it reaches the empty thing.
Then let her fix in opposition to it another hook, or a third, if needed: so that the traction is equal, and does not lean to one side.
Then let it be extended with an equal extension, and let the extension not be only according to straightness, but rather let it be moved to every part, so that its exit is eased: just as is done with a tooth at its extraction.
And it is necessary that that which is between them be softened for the extension. But if a part of it is retained, then it is necessary that the midwife anoint some fingers with oil, and insert them from the side, so that she may revolve with them that which is retained.
If therefore a part of the fetus comes out, then let the hooks be changed to another place which is higher: do this until the whole fetus comes out.
If therefore a hand comes out before the others, and it is not possible to reduce it because of its constriction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it. Then pull it out until, when it comes out entirely, you cut it either at the joint of the sole, or at the elbow; and thus you must do in the other hand, and in the two feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or constriction happens to it in its exit, or water is collected in its head, then it is necessary that he insert a thorny spatula between the fingers, and split the head with it, and draw out the water; or break it with the instrument which is called Almisdac, whose form comes with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter which is after this.
Similarly, let him do if the fetus is of a large-headed nature, then it is necessary that you cut it with algengematu; or pull it out with misdach, as we said. Then pull out the bones with forceps.
If the head comes out, and is constricted at the clavicle, then let it be split, until the moisture which is in the chest is poured out.
But if it is not complicated, nor does it yield to exit, then cut the head piecemeal, according to what is possible for you.
If there is an abscess in the lower part of the belly, or there is dropsy in it, then it is necessary that you pierce it, until the moisture runs out of it.
If however the fetus is on its feet, then its extraction is easy, and its direction to the mouth of the womb is light.
But if it is constricted at the belly or chest, then it is necessary that you extract it with a cloth which you have wrapped around your hand; and split the belly or chest until that which is in them runs out.
But if the remaining limbs are removed, and the head remains, then let the midwife insert her left hand. Therefore if the mouth of the womb is open, then let her insert her hand into the depth of the womb, and with it seek the head, and draw it with her fingers to the mouth of the womb; then let her insert into it one hook, or two hooks, and draw it out with them.
But if the mouth of the womb is constricted, because of a hot abscess which has happened to it, then it is not necessary that one should labor over it; rather it is necessary then to administer the effusion of humid, oily things, and administer plasters; and let her sit in waters which soften and moisten.
But if the fetus is on its side, then it is possible that it be straightened; then let that which we said in the case of a living fetus be administered.
But if that is not possible, then let it be cut piecemeal, and extracted.
And it is necessary that you not leave anything at all of the afterbirth inside.
Therefore, when you have completed your operation, then administer what we said concerning hot abscesses which happen in the wombs.
But if a flow of blood happens, then let her descend into styptic waters, and administer the remaining treatment.
And I indeed already saw a woman, in whose belly a fetus had died; then she was impregnated over it a second time, afterwards another fetus also died. Therefore, after a long time an abscess happened to her in her navel, and it swelled until it opened: and it began to emit pus.
Therefore I was called to her cure, and I treated her for a long time: and the wound was not consolidated.
Therefore I placed upon it some plasters of strong attraction: and a bone came out from the place. Then several days passed for her, and another bone came out. Therefore I wondered at this, since there is no bone in the belly.
Therefore I thought that they were from the bones of a dead fetus.
Therefore I investigated the wound, and extracted many bones, the woman being in better condition: and now she lived thus for a long time, and emitted little pus from the place.
And I did not establish this unusual thing here, except because there is knowledge in it, and help for that which the skillful physician administers with his hand in the treatment.
Chapter LXXVII.
Concerning the forms of instruments necessary in the extraction of a fetus.
The form of the whirl by which the womb is opened.
Here below is the form of a forceps, by which books are straightened, which has equally two whirls at the two ends of the two pieces of wood. However, this whirl ought to be more slender than the forceps; and it ought to be made either of ebony or boxwood.
And let the width of each piece of wood be about two fingers; and the edge of each of the two is about one finger, and the length of each of them is a palm and a half; and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two additions of the same kind of wood, which are now fixed in both, whose length is half a palm and a little more; and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mouth of the womb, so that it is opened with them when you revolve the two whirls.
Another form similar to that, more slender, and lighter.
Let it be made of ebony or boxwood, according to the likeness of forceps. However, at its extremity let there be two additions, as you see here above: and the length of each addition is about a palm: and the width of each is two digits.
Therefore when you wish to open the womb with it, then make the woman sit on the bed, with her legs hanging down, open what is between her legs. Then insert these two joined additions into the womb: and you are holding the lower extremity of the instrument between her hips. Then open your hand with the instrument, as you do with forceps, equally according to the amount that you wish of the opening of the mouth of the womb, until the midwife does what she wants.
The shape of another speculum which the ancients called the impelling shape by which the fetus is impelled.
The shape of the Almisdac, with which a large head is crushed: and it is similar to forceps, which have teeth at the end, as you see.
And sometimes it is oblong, like forceps according to this shape, as you see, which has teeth like saw teeth, by which it is cut and crushed.
The shape of a hook having one point.
The shape of a hook having two points.
The shape of a spatula having two ends for cutting off the head.
The shape of two broad spatulas also.
All these instruments are practiced, and the more their types are multiplied and prepared by the craftsman, the quicker he is at his work, and the more highly regarded by men, on account of their quantity.
Therefore you should not despise any of these instruments, but let them be prepared for you. For their necessity is inexcusable.
Chapter LXXVIII.
Concerning the extraction of the secundine.
When the secundine is retained after childbirth, then you ought to instruct the woman to hold her breath. Then make her sneeze with a sternutatory; and press your hand upon her mouth and nostrils. If therefore it comes out by this, it is good.
If not, then take a pot, and pierce a hole in the lid; and put into it herbs which open the mouth of the womb, such as calamus, rue, and dill, and chamomile, and seseli, and cassia lignea, and centaury: take all these herbs, or some of them, and immerse them with water, and put them over the fire.
Then place a reed tube over the hole in the lid of the pot, and the other end into the mouth of the womb; and let her hold it until the vapor reaches the cavity of the womb.
Then make her sneeze, as we said. For the afterbirth will come out easily.
But if it remains after this treatment, and does not come out: then instruct the midwife to immerse her hand in sesame oil, or mallow mucilage. Then let her insert it into the vulva, and search for the afterbirth with it. When therefore she has found it, then let her take it, and extend it gradually.
But if it is attached deep in the womb, then let her insert her hand, as we have related, until when she has found the afterbirth, and extract it gradually according to the straightness, so that the womb does not fall during forceful pulling: indeed it is necessary that it be moved with ease to the right and left sides: then let the amount of pulling be added. For then it obeys, and is preserved, and escapes from adhesion.
But if the mouth of the womb is constricted, as we have already related: treat it with sneezing, and with medication from a pot with herbs.
But if it does not come out with all that we have related, then beware lest you tire yourself over it in repeated extraction.
Truly it is necessary that what comes out to the mouth of the womb from it, be tied to the hip of the woman. Then give her a clyster with tetrapharmacon ointment. For it will putrefy it after days, and it will dissolve, and it will come out.
However, when it putrefies, an evil odor rises from it to the stomach and head, and it harms the weak woman. Therefore it is necessary that suitable fumigations be administered to it.
Some of the ancients, however, have already experienced the fumigation of nasturtium and dried figs.
The form of the instrument by which a woman is fumigated in the retention of the secundines and menses.
Let it be made of glass similar to an alcazar, or of brass: and let the wide end be placed over the fire: and let the fumigation be placed upon the coals: let her hold it until that fumigation is consumed: and repeat another.
Chapter LXXIX.
Concerning the cure of an imperforate anus.
Many boys are born, and their anuses are imperforate, whom a subtle film has already obstructed. Therefore it is necessary that the midwife perforate that film with her finger: and if not, let her perforate it with a sharp spatula: and let her beware of the muscle, lest she touch it.
Then place wool soaked in wine and oil upon it. Then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
But if you fear that it will become obstructed, then place a small lead tube in the opening for many days, and remove it when the boy wishes to defecate.
And now obstruction of the anus happens also from the healing of a wound, or an abscess. Therefore it is necessary that that consolidation be cut, then treated with what we said of treatment and lead.
Chapter Eighty.
Concerning the treatment of fistulas which occur in the lower part.
Fistulas which occur in the lower part are a knotting and a thickening which occur near the anus externally, or in the space of one part; and it is one fistula, or more.
When therefore that knotting becomes old, it opens, and a watery white moisture or thin pus flows from it.
And sometimes some of these fistulas are penetrating. Therefore penetrating ones are recognized by that which comes out with the excretion and flatulence, when the patient uses excretion; and perhaps worms come out of them.
And there are some of these fistulas, when they are penetrating in the space, even to the bladder, and even to the urinary passage. And some of them penetrate to the hip joint and to the coccyx.
However, that by which a fistula penetrating to the anus is distinguished from one that is not penetrating, is that you insert your index finger into the anus and insert a thin probe into the fistula of air or iron when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in it, then insert into it a thin lead probe, or a horsehair, until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
But if you do not feel it completely, nor does anything come out of the opening from an excretion, nor flatulence, nor a worm as I said: then know that it is not penetrating; therefore hasten to the cure.
And when the fistula penetrates to the bladder or to the urinary meatus: then its sign is the exit of urine from it, and the prevention of its consolidation in this place with medicines.
If, however, it penetrates to the hip joint or to the hip bone: then its sign is that the probe reaches there, if there is no tortuosity in it to the bone; and the finding of pain in the circumference of the part, and that pus always comes out of it; and that medication is not effective in it, nor does it consolidate with ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistulas there is absolutely no cure: and their treatment is labor and vanity for the foolish physician upon whom it is performed.
However, in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing is hoped for with the saw, according to what I relate to you, and I have already proved it: and it is so that the patient lies on his back between your hands; and raise his legs above his hips, and his hips be inclined toward his belly. Then introduce a lead or bronze probe, if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know where the probe reaches.
But if the patient feels it around the anus, then you must insert your index finger into the anus.
Therefore, if you feel the probe has already penetrated by itself, detected by your finger, besides what you feel between your finger and the fistula or flesh: then you know for certain that it is penetrating: therefore do not work on it, since there is no cure for it, as we have said.
However, it has been said that in some men the flesh heals, and of the medication by which help is hoped for it, is to heat a fine cautery according to the size of the fistula, as preceded, and insert it burning into the fistula until it reaches around the anus. Then repeat this twice or thrice, until you know that all those added filtered flesh, which are like the quill of a birds feather, are now cauterized.
Then treat it with linen cloths wrapped in butter, until those cauterized fleshes come out; then treat it with ointments that generate and consolidate flesh.
If therefore it heals, it is good.
And if not, then it never heals with any other treatment.
And if a probe emerges, and does not reach your finger, which is in the anus, and there is between it and the probe a thick covering of flesh or fat, and you see a fistula in that which follows the surface of the skin, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you proceed cutting with the probe which is in the fistula, until the incision reaches where the end of the probe reaches, and the probe is freed and falls out.
Then cleanse those filtered fleshes, which resemble the quill of a bird's feather, so that nothing of them remains.
But if blood overcomes you, and separates you from your operation so that you cannot cut those fleshes, then what is better and of quicker help is to cauterize it with fire or with a sharp medicine.
Because cauterization with fire gathers good qualities, the burning of those added fleshes, and the cutting off of blood, and the drying of humors.
Then treat it with linen cloths wrapped in butter, or with crushed cabbage leaves, until the place emits pus, and those burnt fleshes come out in the pus.
Then treat the wound with consolidating ointments, which generate hard flesh: and there are medicines in which there is astringency and drying.
For when they are restored, then it is healed: and you do not fear a recurrence.
But if a fistula has already reached the depth of the anus, and is elongated from the superfluity of the body: then insert your finger into the anus, and explore with it.
If therefore you feel a probe, and between you and it there is a covering of fat or flesh, and it is close to penetration: then there is no operation in that, except according to the way of confidence and hope.
That is, because one of three methods is administered to it: either to cauterize it, as we said: or to excise it, until you obtain its concavity, so that it is possible for you to insert lint, and treatment from nearby. And do not proceed far with the incision, lest you cut the muscle containing the anus, and there happens to the patient an involuntary discharge of excrement. Then treat it with what we have said.
For perhaps it will heal: or you will lengthen that covering, and cleanse it after you have cut it almost to the anus, as we said, either with a probe or with another instrument of sharp point. Then cleanse those filtered tissues which are in the incision, as far as you can with exploration.
Then attempt the restoration of the whole wound with a suture, and the upper orifice where it is consolidated: and a fistula remains open in the inner part of the anus, and it will be easier on the patient.
And sometimes a fistula is sutured in this way: and it is, when you insert a probe into the fistula: and it is in the side of the anus around the surface of the body with the skin and margin of the anus: then take a probe with a perforated end, which is like an aleskef needle, that is, of those who prepare hides, according to this form. And insert into it a thread twisted from five threads, and around it. Then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula, until it reaches its depth.
If therefore it penetrates into the margin of the anus inwardly nearby, then pull the thread out of that opening: so that you insert your finger into the anus, and pull out the end of the thread, and bring the two ends together: and tighten each, and leave it for one day, or two days: and the more the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well, until those fleshes which are between the two ends of the thread are cut, and fall away: then treat the wound, until it is consolidated, and healed.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating: then make it penetrate in whatever way is prepared for you, unless it is very deep. For it is not in your power to do that because of the muscle, so that you do not cut it.
Then do with it as we have said, until it is healed.
The shape of a spiny spatula with which a fistula is split, whose curvature is very sharp, and the other part is not sharp, so that it does not cut what is not necessary, as you see.
Chapter LXXXI.
Concerning the suturing of hemorrhoids, from which blood flows from their incision: and the treatment of fissures.
There are hemorrhoids according to two methods. For they are either within the anus, similar to bladders of coals, or plums; or as if they themselves were grape seeds. And of them there are small ones, and large ones: and blood flows from them always, and it is one, and there are many. And they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those which are outside the anus, are for the most part few in moisture, from which flows a yellowish water, or little blood with a constant flow: and they are according to the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which is within the anus is that you order the patient to strain; and it is pressed out until it emerges from the anus, and the athelil appear to you. Therefore, also suspend it with hooks; or hold them with your nail. Then incise them at their roots.
But if the hooks do not hold them, because of their moisture and softening: then take them with a rough cloth, or with a pledget of woolen fabric, and draw them out with your fingers. Then incise them, and sprinkle upon them after incision certain hot powders, that they may stand in place of a cautery. Or cauterize them according to what preceded in the chapter on cauterization. Then treat them with the remaining treatment, until they are healed.
But if the anus does not obey evacuation, then administer an enema to the patient in which there is a small amount of irritant, so that what is in the anus may be washed out with it, and it will obey evacuation quickly, when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are external to the anus, then their matter is light, easy: and it is that you take them with your fingernail, or take them with a hook, and incise them. Then treat them with what we said, until they are healed.
And whoever abhors incision with iron, then it is necessary that you administer their suture according to this method. Take a wound thread, and insert it into a needle. Then draw the hemorrhoid upwards, and make it penetrate with the needle at its root on the other side: and wrap the two ends of the thread, in the lower part of the needle, and let them be lateral: and tighten the hemorrhoid with a strong constriction. Then knot the thread, and remove the needle.
Do this with all the hemorrhoids, and leave one of them: nor should you sew it, so that an excess of blood may flow from it.
Then place over the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil, or cerate, and place it over the anus: and instruct the patient to rest. Then leave it until it falls off.
When therefore the hemorrhoids fall off, treat them with ointments, and the rest of the treatment, until they are healed.
Concerning the treatment of fissures. For very often they happen from the dryness of the stool, and the constriction of nature.
When therefore they become old, and medicine does not work in them: then it is necessary that you scrape them with the point of a spatula, or with your fingernail, until they become moist, and the upper layer which prevents them from healing is removed from them. Then treat them until they heal as is necessary.
But if they do not heal: then repeat the scraping with more force than the first time, until they bleed much, and are raw; then treat them until they are healed.
Concerning the treatment of inverted and non-inverted almirmar, and dry athelil, and formica.
Chapter LXXXII.
Know that almirmar is nothing but a round nodule, the color of the body, similar to the head of a nail; and it occurs throughout the body, especially in the lower parts of the feet and toes; and pain results from it during walking.
Therefore it is necessary that what is around the nail be cut, and held with forceps, or with a hook, and eradicated from its root.
Then treat it; and if you wish, cauterize it according to what preceded in its chapter.
From dry athelil, however, there is a small, rough, filtered, round eminence on the surface of the body. And they often occur on the hands of children.
And its treatment is that you extend the athelil and incise it: or tie it with a silk thread: or with hair, until it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize it according to what was stated beforehand with fire, or with a sharp medicine: and now inverted athelil, and non-inverted, are eradicated with the instrument which I shall describe.
An ant, however, is also a small, filtered, thick eminence on the surface of the body, going very deep: and when cold finds it, something like the bite of an ant is felt in it, and it happens in the whole body: and it occurs most in the hands.
And its treatment is that you take a small tube from a goose quill, or a strong vulture quill: and place that tube over the ant, until the circle of the tube contains some of it. Then roll your hand with the tube until you cut what is around the ant: and place it deep in the flesh, and eradicate the ant from its root.
And if you wish, do it with a bronze or iron tube, according to this method.
Let the top of the tube be drawn upwards to a rolled-up blunt point, so that its grip and rotation on the finger may be eased.
And if you wish, cauterize the ant as was previously stated in the cauterization of Athelil in the chapter on cautery.
Chapter 83.
Concerning the forms of instruments which are used for clysters in diseases of the anus, and for the loosening of the bowels, and for colic.
Sometimes a clyster is made of silver, or of brass, or of cast or hammered bronze.
And sometimes this instrument is made small and large, according to those in whom it is administered.
Therefore that which is administered in the treatment of small children should be small: and those whose anuses are weak or painful, so also their clysters.
A large clyster.
Its tube, that is, the upper end to which the bag is tied, should be wide in this manner: and let it have a distinction so that the bag may be tied as you see, and its lower extremity which is inserted into the anus, should be blunt, that is, not open, very smooth, tapering to a point: and on one side of it let there be two holes, and on the other side one hole.
And let the size of the hole be according to the thickness of the fingers, and a little thicker, and let the bag in which the medicines are placed be made of an animal's bladder: or of sheepskin, which should be in the form of a small asofratus: the root of which is one palm.
And let it be perforated with many holes as it is rolled up, and let the space which is between each hole be the thickness of a finger. Then insert into those holes a strong thread of raw thread, which is made of ten threads, or thereabouts, and gather with it the bag like an asofratus. When therefore the bag is placed in it, let it be tied at the head of the clyster between two distinctions with a strong constriction. Then clysterize with it.
And this is the form of the circle of the bags here depicted, and of the holes.
Moreover, the manner of clysterizing with it is this: that you fill the bag or bladder with medicine which is warmed according to the amount the infirm person can tolerate; and beware lest it be very hot or very cold.
Then let the infirm person be laid on his back, and let him raise his legs upwards; and place under him an inthaan, that is, a leather on which people lie, or a linen cloth.
Then anoint the end of the clyster with oil, or with egg white, or with fenugreek mucilage, or the like. Then introduce it into the anus gently. Then let the attendant take the bag with both hands together, and squeeze it forcefully until he has emptied all that is in the clyster into the intestine.
Then withdraw the clyster, and let the infirm person raise his legs to the wall.
And if the medicine causes him to defecate, then let him hold it back as much as he can. And if he sleeps with it the whole night, it is more effective in helping.
But if you give him a clyster for ailments of looseness of the bowels, and his evacuation is rapid: then repeat the clyster until he is healed.
But if there are hemorrhoids or harmful abscesses in the anus, then let the clyster be very fine, gentle, of the kind used for clysterizing children.
And this is the form of a fine clyster over which the skin is tightly drawn.
Chapter LXXXIV.
I have already spoken in the division concerning the treatment of wounds which was appropriate to the book, and I will here speak of their treatment by hand and with medicines according to the complete method.
Therefore I say that wounds are diversified according to that by which wounds are made, and according to the place upon which the wound falls.
Therefore the things by which wounds are made are very often such as the throw of a stone, or the cut of a sword or knife, or a blow with a lance or stick or arrow, and similar things from many objects.
Moreover, wounds which are made according to the locations of the body are such as a wound which falls upon the head, or neck, or chest, or belly, or liver: and similar parts.
And I am narrating the treatments of certain wounds, so that I may put that reasoning and rule upon the remaining wounds.
And I shall begin with simple head wounds: because the discussion of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
I say therefore. When a simple wound happens in the head, and there is no fracture of the bone: consider then, if it is from the throwing of a stone, or similar things, and it has now broken only the skin: and it is a large wound, and you fear concerning the bad outcome of a hot abscess: then hasten, and immediately bleed the cephalic vein: and do not delay it: and draw from him blood according to the quantity of his strength: if nothing is prohibiting phlebotomy, especially if the blood which comes forth from the wound is little. And let the bloodletting be on the opposite side of the wounded part.
And let the bloodletting be on the opposite side of the wounded part. And let the patient beware of repletion, and wine, and coarse foods.
But if something prohibits bloodletting, then let him lessen [his intake] from food, and let him not approach meats, nor wines.
And put on the wound, if a hot abscess happens to it, a compress soaked in rose oil alone, or with wine in which there is astringency.
But if you are secure from a hot abscess, then put on the wound, if it is fresh with its blood, and the air has not changed it, the powder which I shall relate shortly: and if the air has already changed it, then put on it certain ointments, until it is healed.
But if the wound is large, or is from the incision of a sword, and similar things: and its lips do not come together by themselves, then bring them together with suture, according to what I shall relate in the wounds of the belly concerning their suture.
But if the wound is already bared from the bone, and hangs down, and there is no retention of it except in a small suspensory: then incise it. Then treat the wound with medicines, and restore in it hard flesh in place of skin, and instruct the patient to be nourished with foods in which there is thickness, such as are heads [of grain] and boiled wheat, until it is like pottage: and similar things.
But if you find putrefaction in the skin, and it does not adhere to the bone: then incise all the putrefied part, and throw it away: then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood flows: and it is not stopped by medicines, then investigate the artery. If therefore you find it uncut, then cut it with a Spatula, or tie it. But if necessity demands when that which we have said does not help, then cauterize it until the bleeding stops.
But if these wounds are small, simple, then their treatment is easy, light: so that you may sprinkle on them this powder: because it consolidates them before the air changes them.
Method of incarnative powder. Take one part frankincense, and two parts dragon's blood, and three parts of unslaked lime: let the whole be thoroughly ground, and sifted: and let the wound be filled with it, and bound tightly, until it adheres well to it, and coagulates with the blood.
Then bind it over with lint, and leave it, and do not loosen it while it remains, so that it may not fester. For the medicine is not removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes the lime alone does this, when you do not have frankincense and dragon's blood at hand; and dragon's blood also performs the same operation in many large wounds, when it is appropriate to apply it and bind it, and the wounds are fresh with their own blood, which the air has not altered.
But if the air has already altered them in some way, especially in summer, then the application of this powder is not intended: therefore treat it with what we have said, so that you apply certain digestive ointments to it; or apply poultices of barley flour, made with water and honey, until they discharge pus. Then treat them with the remaining treatment, until they are healed.
But if a fracture of the bone occurs with the wound, and it is small, then extract it with forceps: and I will now describe the treatment of head fractures in what is to come.
Concerning wounds of the neck.
If, however, a wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference in its treatment and that of a head wound when it is simple.
But if a nerve or artery of the neck has been cut, then if it is a nerve, there is nothing to be done except to avoid treating it with something that causes strong astringency, such as ziniar and zegi, and similar things. Because they harm nerves and contract them.
And do not put anything cold on the wound at all, because the substance of the nerves is cold, and its continuity is with the brain, which is the most superior of the members; and let that which heals it be from mild medicines, such as lime washed with fresh water infused several times with oil, or with rose oil, and tutty, when it is also washed with fresh water; and the other mineral stones, in this manner, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer a suture; or draw its lips together with powders. Then treat it until it heals.
But if the wound is deep, or a sinus occurs in its lower part, in which pus is already collected, then perforate it in the lowest place in it.
But if an artery has been cut in the wound, and a flow of its blood has resulted, then separate, or cut it, and tie it; or cauterize it, if necessity demands it.
But if the vertebrae of the throat, or the whole, have been injured, and the al-w have been saved, then bring together the edges of the skin with a suture over the tube of the throat, and do not touch the throat, but level it, and restore it to its natural shape. Then bind it with a suitable bandage, and leave it for several days. Then treat it with ointments which possess astringency and drying properties, until it is healed.
But if the wound has made an impression on the bone, then examine it: then if there are fragments of bone in it, remove all of them, or as many as you can: and what is not prepared, leave it, until the wound rots, and is separated and its discharge is relieved.
But if the wound is fresh, then sprinkle upon it the powder which I have described. But if bone is seen in the wound, and a fistula forms, then treat it with that whose mention came in its chapter.
Concerning a wound in the shoulder blades.
If there is a wound from a lance or a knife and you see its depth, then consider. Therefore if air escapes from the wound when the sick person breathes, then know that the wound is mortal.
But if it is not deep and is fresh with its blood, then do not at first apply powder to it, nor bind it tightly, lest the blood be retained in its depth and return to the heart and kill the sick person; but apply an attractive ointment to it, and dissolve it twice a day.
If, however, you do not have ointment, then place old linen in the opening of the wound, so that it may absorb what comes out of it in the form of moisture; and cause the sick person to sleep on the wound, so that what collects in it may flow out.
If, however, three days or more have passed since the wounding, and no spasm or bad tremor of the heart or tightness of breath has affected the sick person, and you see his other conditions to be good, then know that the wound is safe. Therefore treat it with linen cloths and the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
But if its healing is delayed, and it always opens up again, then know that a fistula has now formed. Therefore treat it according to its chapter.
And if the wound is from a sword or knife cut, and is simple on the surface of the chest or back: then treat it with what preceded concerning suture, if it is large; or with powder, if it is small.
But if it has already made an impression on the bone, and fragments have fallen from it: then probe the wound, and hasten to extract those fragments, if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then leave them until the wound putrefies, for their extraction is then easier.
The judgment in the treatment of other wounds occurring in other members is the judgment of what we have said.
Concerning wounds of the abdomen, and the protrusion of the intestines, and their exit.
The rupture which happens to the belly is great, moderate, and small.
Therefore, from a large one sometimes an intestine, or a number of intestines, comes forth.
Wherefore their replacement and their suture is more strenuous and difficult.
And a small rupture also becomes difficult in another way. Therefore it follows that their reduction is more difficult, if you do not hasten in their replacement to their place immediately: because it is inflated, opened, and thickened: wherefore its entry becomes difficult.
Therefore, on account of this, a middle rupture is better: because with it the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is difficult in these two kinds.
Know that the suture of the abdomen is according to four methods.
Two common sutures are suitable for the suture of the abdomen, and for the suture of the other wounds of the body: and two other sutures are properly shared with the other wounds. Nevertheless, they are more proper with the suture of the abdomen: and I relate to you each one with explanation and clarification.
Therefore I say, that when a wound is small, and something of the intestine comes out of it, and it becomes difficult to reduce it: then that is because of one of two methods. Either because of the smallness of the disruption, as we said: or because there is inflation in the intestine because of the coldness of the air.
When therefore it is so, then it is necessary that you warm it, so that you submerge a sponge, or a cloth moistened in tepid water alone. Or boil in water squill, and cyperus, and spica, and embrocate the intestine with it, until the inflation is resolved.
Sometimes wine in which there is astringency does this: and it is better than water alone in resolving inflation.
When therefore you see the inflation already resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which mallow, or althea has been boiled. For by it its entry is eased with a small thing.
But if its return is excused after this cure, then incise a little in the rupture, with the instrument with which hemorrhoids are cut.
And the first cure is better when it is prepared, than incision: and constraint to incise is not done after necessity and failure of the instrument.
Let one part of it be which is twisted, sharp, and another part of it not sharp, and let the thin end not be with the thinness of a spatula, but rather be a little blunter: and it is that which is similar to a cautery, as you see.
Therefore when the wound is enlarged, and the intestine enters, then it is necessary that its reduction be according to its natural form in its own proper place, if it is possible over that: because it is better.
When however the rupture is large in the lower part of the belly: then it is necessary that you make the sick person lie on his back, and place his legs higher than his head. And if it is in the upper part of the belly, then place his head and chest higher than his lower parts.
And similarly if there is a rupture in one of the two parts of the belly, then place your intention and your consideration always in this, that the part in which is the wound be higher than the other part.
And so it is necessary that you administer it in large wounds and in medium-sized wounds.
In small wounds however, place it according to what is prepared for you.
Then cause a pious assistant to be present between your hands, who holds the whole rupture with his hand: and bring together its lips. Then reveal from it to him who follows the suture something after something: and this is the method of the common suture of one.
And it is that you take a needle, or a number of needles according to the quantity of the size of the wound. Then leave from the extremity of the rupture the quantity of the ring finger, that is, of the physician: and fix one needle except that you insert into it thread at two extremities of the skin, with two extremities of the sifac which is under the skin from the insides, until you cause it to penetrate from the other part, and you have now brought together two extremities of the skin, and two extremities of the sifac: and four rows are made.
Then bind with double thread around the needle several times from the two parts together, until the two lips of the wound are brought together with a decent binding.
Then leave the thickness of a finger also, and fix in them another needle: afterwards tie it with thread, as you did with the first needle. Therefore do not cease doing that with as many needles as you need, until you complete the binding of the whole wound.
And let the needles be medium between thickness and thinness: because thin needles cut the flesh very quickly: and thick ones are difficult to enter the skin: therefore it is necessary that they be medium in thinness and thickness.
And it is not necessary that the needle be fixed in two extremities of the skin that are very close: so that the flesh is not cut quickly, and the wound is opened before its consolidation, and they are not also lengthened with the suture, so that the wound is not prevented from consolidation.
Then cut off the ends of the needles, so that they do not injure the patient in his sleep: and place on them little cushions of cloth on every side, which hold the ends of the needles: and leave them, until you know that the wound is now consolidated.
And indeed this kind of suture with needles is thus more suitable in small wounds. Because one needle, or two, or about that many, suffice in their suture.
The second suture, however, is common, so that you bring together with the suture four extremities: two extremities of the skin, and two extremities of the fascia at one time, with a needle on which is wound a thread equal in fineness and thickness.
Then when you have penetrated with the needle these four extremities, return the needle from the side from which you began the same, so that the drawn thread falls from the upper part of the wound: so that the suture is like the suture of a cloth by which cloths are bound, and place between each suture the quantity of the thickness of the little finger.
And this suture is known to men.
And with this suture I sewed a wound which had happened to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and the disruption of the wound was greater than one palm: and there protruded from the intestines about two palms of the mid-intestine, and the disruption was in the middle of the belly.
Therefore I reduced it after its intestine had stood outside the wound for twenty-four hours.
Therefore the wound was consolidated in the space of about fifteen days: and I treated it, and he was healed: and he lived after that for many years, behaving in his usual way.
And the physicians had judged that he would not be healed.
And it was by a miracle that I did not treat it with ointment. Because I was in a place where no ointment, nor any medicines, were to be found. Therefore I placed old linen on the wound twice a day, and diligently washed it with honey-water, until it was healed.
But I shall describe two kinds of suture proper to the subject.
And it is, that you begin with a sharp suture: and you insert the needle from the outer to the inner part.
When therefore the needle penetrates the skin, and the muscle running according to the length and breadth of the whole body: leave the end sifac on the side into which you inserted the needle, and make it penetrate to the other end from the inner part to the outer at the other end mirach.
When therefore you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate accordingly in the same mirach end from the outer part to the inner: and leave the sifac end which is on this side, and make the needle penetrate to the other end from the inner part to the outer: and make it penetrate with your penetration with which you make it penetrate in the sifac end mirach, which is in its part, until it penetrates the whole of it.
Then begin also from this same side, and sew it with the end which is from sifac on the other side, and draw the needle out of the skin which it penetrated.
Then return it into that skin, and sew the sifac end which is on the other side with this mirach end: and draw it out of the skin which is on its part, and do that once, and again: and do that turn by turn, until you sew the whole exterior according to that example.
And the sum of this suture is that you sew the suture of leatherworkers as they sew hides: so that you sew sifac once from one part with one end of the skin: and that you leave it another time from another part, until you complete it.
The second kind of proper suture, which Galen mentioned.
This speech of his is narrated, so that you sew it according to the likeness with which certain physicians sew, so that they bring together each part to its own naturally corresponding part: and they apply the end of the sifac to its other end, and the end of the mirach to its other end. And that is done as I tell you.
It is necessary that you fix the needle in the end of the mirach nearest to you on the outer side, and make it penetrate to the inner side in that alone: and leave the two ends of the sifac. Then turn the needle, and make it penetrate from the outer side to the inner side in the two ends of the sifac, namely on both sides.
Then turn both, also make them penetrate from the inside to the outside, into the two other ends of the mirach, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is better than the common easy suture, and it is the suture whose mention preceded.
This is therefore also Galen's statement.
And some said. The suture of the abdomen is straightened with two needles. That is, to insert one thread into both of them: and begin with the insertion of the needle, from that which is with you, and make it penetrate to the other side, according to the suture of those who sew hides equally.
And know that when the rupture is in the middle of the abdomen, then its suture is more difficult than in other places of the abdomen.
The judgment, however, of the medication of a wound, is the judgment of other wounds.
That is, because when you obtain a fresh wound with its blood, before the air alters it, and you reduce the intestine, and its own [substance], and fit it: then place upon it incarnative powder.
But if the air has already altered the wound: then place upon it certain ointments which are present to you, until they produce pus: and the threads fall out, and the sifac and mirach consolidate. Then treat it as is the treatment of other wounds, until it is healed.
