But if medicines are not present to you, then place upon it immediately, from the time it begins to emit pus, old cotum: and change it twice a day, as I taught you, until it is healed.
For you do not need other medication in most instances, when the wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound communicates with the principal members in the lesion, then it is necessary that you submerge soft wool in oil of moderate warmth, or in rose oil: and place it around the areas which are between the groins, and under the armpits.
But if it feels pain, or prickings, or putrefaction in its intestines (for this often happens) then clysterize it with astringent, black, warm wine: especially if putrefaction has already arrived in the intestine, and has become an exit penetrating to its concavity.
And know that that which is from the large intestine is of easier healing: and that which is from the small intestine is of more difficult healing.
The intestine moreover which is named Jejunum, does not receive healing from a wound which happens in it completely: and that is because of its multitude, which is from veins, and the size of them, and because of the subtlety of its body, and the proximity of its nature to the nerves.
But if that which proceeds from the wound is an intestine, and you acquire it fresh: reduce it according to your reduction, by which you reduce intestines equally.
If however a space has passed by, and it has already become green, or black: then it is necessary that you bind it with thread above the place which is blackened from it, so that a flow of blood does not happen. For in an intestine are veins and arteries.
Then cut that which is beyond that ligature, and place two ends of thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside it: so that the attraction of it, and the extraction of it in the case of the intestine, and the emission of pus of the wound, may be alleviated upon you.
Concerning a wound which happens in the intestine.
When a rupture happens in the intestine, and it is small: then it is possible that it may be restored in some men: therefore, because I saw one who was wounded in his belly with a blow of a lance, and the wound was from the right of the stomach, and the wound was old: and a fistula was made, and excretion, and flatulence came out from it.
Therefore I applied a treatment, until I was not confident in its healing; nor did I cease to refine it, until it was healed, and the place was consolidated.
After I saw the place already consolidated, I feared for the patient, that some bad accident might happen to the patient in his belly: and no bad accident happened to him from that. And he remained in better conditions, eating and drinking, and copulating, and he entered a bath, and he exercised in his diets.
And now some experienced people have said that when a wound happens in the intestine, and it is small: then it is necessary that it be tied according to this method. And it is, that you take ants with large heads. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, and place one of those ants which have an open mouth over the two lips of the wound. When therefore it seizes upon it, and tightens its mouth, cut off its head, for it adheres, and is not loosened.
Then place another ant near the first, and do not cease doing this with ant after ant according to the quantity of the whole wound.
Then reduce it, and sew the wound. For those heads remain attached in the intestine, until the intestine is nourished and healed, and no harm at all happens to the patient.
And now it is possible that the intestine may also be tied with a fine thread: which has been wiped from the animal's intestine, attached to it after it is inserted into the needle.
And it is so that you take the end of this thread from the intestine, and it is much thinned. Then tie a fine linen thread wrapped around its end. Then insert that thread into a needle, and sew the intestine with it. Then return it into the rupture.
And this method of suture with ants and intestine, is only according to a way of trust and hope.
Know that a wound which is large, wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines: then there is no remedy except thinning, nor is its healing at all.
Concerning the treatment of Zuchamen and fistulas.
Know that every wound or abscess when it grows old and becomes ancient, and becomes an ulcer, and does not consolidate, and always discharges pus, and is not incised: then it is universally called a fistula, wherever it may be in the body, and we call it Zuchamen.
And a fistula indeed is, according to the truth, a filtered, hard, white knot; with which there is no pain, which has the concavity of a bird's feather; and because of that some call it Risati, that is, a feather.
And it is at certain times moist, which discharges pus. And perhaps the moisture is divided at certain times. And sometimes this moisture is abundant, and sometimes it is scanty; and it is thick, and it is thin.
And I already premised in the division, then when I spoke of wounds, that every ulcer which is not healed, nor does flesh grow in it, is not that except because of one of nine causes.
Of which one is, either because of a deficiency of blood in the body: or because of its bad quality: or because between it and its edges there is hard flesh, preventing the growth of flesh: or because there is much dirt and filth: or because the ulcer itself is putrid, and the matter which is discharged from it is of bad quality: or because the medicine is unsuitable in its treatment: or because a corruption falling in the region is a kind of pestilence, or through a property of the region, as happens in Caesaraugusta: in which the digestion of illnesses is difficult, and the healing of abscesses by nature is delayed there: or because in it there is one bone, or a number of bones.
And I have already shown all these causes and their signs, and their treatments with medicines in the division: and it is necessary here that I make you know the treatment of Fistula and Zuken, which is done by way of operation with the hand.
Know therefore, that fistula occurs in all the members of the body. For among them are fistulas which reach to large veins and arteries, or nerves, or fat: or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to one of the ribs: or to one of the vertebrae of the back, or to one of the composite joints of a composition: such as the hand and the foot: and among them are those which go to the proximity of a principal member, and similar things.
Therefore, which of these fistulas is according to this manner, is of the illnesses difficult of healing, and does not receive treatment, and the occupation with it is labor and ignorance.
But if there is some fistula whose depth does not reach these places which I have mentioned: and you hope, and trust in its cure, and wish to arrive at the knowledge of it, then take a probe of bronze, or iron, if the fistula extends in a straight line, and explore it with it.
But if there is tortuosity in the fistula, then investigate it with a probe of thin lead. Because lead, with the softness of its body, is eased, and is bent around the tortuosity.
But if the fistula has many orifices, it is not possible for you to explore it with a probe. Therefore, inject a clyster into one of its orifices.
For the moisture with which you inject it, goes towards the other orifices, and flows out from them.
Then diligently inquire, in whatever way is possible for you, so that you may know whether there is a bone or nerve there, and whether the fistula's concavity is far or near; or whether it is a single fistula which has many orifices, and consider all that with the utmost of your strength; with your questioning, by which you question the patient, and by finding pain upon compression with your hand upon the place, and similar signs and causes happening to an abscess.
When therefore you have considered all these things truly, then proceed to treatment confidently: and it is that you should then consider if the fistula is near, apparent, or in a safe place far from a joint, or from a nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the places which I have told you about: then cut the fistula as has been previously described in my account, and remove what is in it of pus, and corrupt flesh, which grows in its orifice from flesh or thelul, and the like: and treat it until it is healed.
But if the fistula is of great depth, and is straight, then it is necessary that you cut it deeply as much as is possible for you. Then cleanse it of all its corrupt flesh.
Then apply linen cloths wrapped in sharp medicaments; and insert them up to the concavity of the fistula which you have reached with the iron: do this with it several times, until the sharp medicament corrodes all that remains in the concavity of the fistula from the corruption.
Then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh, until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by that, then cauterize it as we have described.
If therefore the cause of the fistula is bone, and this is ascertained by you: then investigate it, and cut it as I have told you: if nothing prevents you, such as a vein, or nerve, or principal member, as we have said.
If therefore bone is discovered, and there is something of corruption and blackness in it: then scrape it until all its corruption is removed. Then treat it with that which causes it to grow flesh, until it is healed.
But if it is not healed, and emits pus as it did before: then know that you have not reached the complete eradication of the corruption.
Therefore, investigate it again, and carefully perform its scraping and cleansing with your utmost strength.
Then restore it. If therefore it heals, it is good; and if not, then know that that corruption is in a distant depth of the body, which you have not reached. Therefore you have no skill in it, except to leave it to nature.
But if a small bone is corrupted, and it is possible for you, draw it out with fine forceps which are suitable for it.
But if there are many bones, then carefully perform the extraction of all of them, and do not leave any of them behind with your strength.
If, however, something of the humors opposes you, and there is no fear or mistake in incising them, then incise those humors and flesh, if they are there, and free the bones.
If, however, you are not prepared to remove the bones at that time, then place something on the wound that will cause it to putrefy: and leave it until that which is around those bones of flesh and humors putrefies.
And keep the wound so that it does not heal. And let the incision that you made in your operation be constricted. Rather, if you fear, place on it a lint dipped in crushed sulfur with oil: or lint dipped in Egyptian ointment, or some green ointments.
For when those humors putrefy, and the bones are separated from them, their removal and extraction are eased.
But if it is one large bone, like a bone of the leg, or a hip bone, and similar to those, and that which is corrupted from it is a large part: then scrape it with the utmost care, until that blackness and corruption is removed. Then restore the wound.
But if that which is corrupted in it is a large part, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to saw it, and to cut it all the way to the place where the corruption has reached: then treat it until it consolidates.
And I relate to you, a case which happened to a man in his leg: so that you may place it as an example and aid in your operation.
There was a man, young in age, about thirty years old, who suffered pain in his leg, from a cause which was stirred up within him in his body, that is, from within, until the materials reached the leg, and it became a large abscess: and there was no external cause.
Therefore, time passed with him with the error of physicians, until the abscess was opened, and much material gathered in it. And its treatment was delayed until the leg became fistulated, and many openings were made in it: all of which emitted pus and the humors of the body.
Therefore, a multitude of physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no one skilled in the art of surgery, until I was called, and I saw his leg, and the materials which flowed from those openings in great quantity: indeed the whole body of the man was now wasted, and his color was yellow.
Therefore I inserted a probe into one of the openings, and the probe reached the bone.
Then I examined all the openings: and I found them to reach each other from all parts of the leg.
Therefore I probed, and cut above one of those openings, until I uncovered a part of the bone: and I found it corrupted: which was now eroded, and blackened, and putrefied, and perforated, until it had penetrated to the marrow.
Therefore I sawed what was revealed to me, and prepared [it] from the corrupted bone.
And I considered that there was nothing in the bone except that corruption, which I cut away and sawed. And I had now eradicated it.
Then I applied a restoration to the wound with consolidative medicines for a long time. And it did not consolidate.
Then I attempted, and uncovered the bone a second time above the first uncovering: and I found continuous corruption with the bone: and I sawed what appeared to me also from that corruption.
Then I attempted its restoration. And it was not restored.
Therefore I uncovered it again, and did not cease cutting the bone piecemeal, and attempting its restoration: and it was not restored, until I cut about a handbreadth from the bone, and extracted it with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicines, and it consolidated quickly, and healed: and this iteration in its operation, and section of it, was not necessary, except because of the disposition of the weakness of the infirm, and the smallness of his tolerance, and my fear for him of death: because there happened to him at all hours from the superfluity of evacuation bad fainting spells.
Therefore he healed with a complete healing: and firm flesh grew in the place of the bone, and a good disposition was made in his body, and his strength returned: and he conversed in his dispositions: and it did him no contrary harm at all in walking.
But if a prominent bone in the body is already broken: then it is necessary that you saw it, according to this manner: and it is: that you take a ligament, and bind it to the extremity of the prominent bone: and instruct someone, who extends it upwards: and place another ligament of thicker wool than the first ligament. Then bind it over the flesh which is under the bone: and extend its extremity, so that you pull the flesh downwards: and you uncover the flesh over the place which you wish to saw, so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
Place a piece of wood, or a board, decently under the bone below: because when you do that, it is not prevented that it cuts corrupt flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be a little over the corrupt place, so that there is not corruption in the concavity of the bone: and it does not appear in its appearance to the senses, and compel to sawing in its place another.
But if the bone is corrupt, and is not prominent, but rather a part of it is continuous with a part: and the corruption is in its middle, or in a part of it: then uncover the flesh from all parts entirely. Then place wood below.
Afterwards saw it from the first part, where the corruption is, until the sawing is continued from the other part: and let the sawing be according to the length from the corruption a little, as we said.
But if the corruption is in the joint, cut the corrupt joint itself, and saw the bone where it is continuous from the other part.
But if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no remedy in it except abrasion.
But if the corruption is in the metacarpus of the hand, or in the metatarsus of the foot: then the matter is very difficult. Truly it is necessary that you inquire how the corruption appears to you. And abrade it, and cleanse it according to whatever arrangement is possible for you, and with whatever instrument is rectified for you: when it does not oppose you, vein, or nerve.
And know that cutting instruments and saws for cutting these bones are many, according to the position of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness, and their subtlety, and their magnitude, and their smallness, and their hardness, and their rarity. Therefore it is necessary that you prepare for every kind of operation an instrument suitable for that operation.
And know that these operations signify to you the kind of instrument you need, when you have long study. And the knowledge of the methods of this art, a testimony of the methods of illnesses: for then you will find by yourself that which is suitable for it from the instruments for every infirmity.
And I am confirming for you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments, which you will place as examples upon which you will cut, and the test by which you will try them on others.
The shape of another saw.
The shape of a joint rasp. Let the head of this rasp be according to the shape of the head of a star-headed nail; and let its points be according to the shape of the points of an elishethfeg.
And it is not suitable except to rub with it the heads of joints, when they are corrupted, or a bone that is wide and large.
The shape of a larger rasp.
The shape of a wide rasp.
The shape of a rasp in which there is a concavity.
The shape of another, fine rasp.
The shape of another rasp with a curved end.
The shape of a small, thin, slender rasp.
This is similar to a radius, and the end is like a saw; it is shown in many places for the sake of bones.
The shape of a rasp which is suitable for rasping that which has been perforated from bones.
Its end is triangular, of sharp points.
It is made of Indian wood. And similarly it is necessary that all those things which we have spoken of be made from rasps, and incisors, and saws.
Concerning the incision of extremities, and the sawing of bones.
Extremities putrefy, either from an external or an internal cause.
Therefore, when you have treated that corruption with medicinal cures, and the cure does not help, and you see the corruption cutting into the limb, which nothing repels: then it is necessary that you incise that limb until you reach the extent of the corruption, so that the patient may be saved from death, or from an evil which is greater than the loss of the limb.
And the sign that this happens is that the limb blackens until it putrefies: as if fire had burned it, or it putrefies after the blackness: until that putrefaction is incised to where it follows that limb, and creeps into the whole body.
Therefore hasten with its incision, and similarly if the cause of the corruption is from the striking of certain poisonous things: such as a sea scorpion, and a viper, and a whelk, and similar things.
Therefore if the corruption or the blow is in the extremity of a finger, then incise the finger, and do not give space to the corruption, that it may proceed to the rest of the hand.
And similarly if it happens in the hand, then incise it from the root of the fore-arm, and do not give space to the corruption, until it takes hold in the fore-arm.
And if it invades it, then incise the arm at the elbow in its joint.
But if the corruption passes through, and you see it around the elbow: then in that is the death of the infirm. And to administer beyond that from treatment, is a quantity of virtue.
And similarly do with the foot, when corruption invades a toe, then incise it at one of the joints: and if it takes hold in the instep, then incise the whole foot. But if it ascends to the knee, then incise the leg at the knee joint.
But if the corruption has already reached above the knee, then there is no skill in it, except dismissal, and leaving the infirm to death.
And the method of incising a limb, or sawing it, is to bind a ligature below that which you wish to incise: and bind another ligature above the place. And let one assistant extend the lower ligature downwards; and let another assistant extend the upper ligature upwards. And you separate the flesh which is between the two ligatures with a broad spatula, until all the flesh is uncovered. Then let it be incised, or sawn.
And it is necessary that you place linen cloth on all sides, and that the saw not touch the healthy place: and an added injury and a hot abscess may happen to the patient.
But if internal bleeding should occur while you are operating, then cauterize the place quickly; or apply some powders that staunch blood. Then return to your medication, until it is completed.
Then bind the wounded member with a bandage suitable to it, and take care of it until it is healed.
And I tell you that which happened to a man in his foot, this same accident that I tell you.
That is, because there happened in his foot a blackness, with a burning which is similar to the burning of fire: and that corruption first happened in his toe, until it began to affect the whole foot.
Therefore the man hastened of himself, after he saw the corruption increasing in the member with its intensity, which he found from its pain and burning: and he cut it at the joint, and he was healed.
And after a long time passed for him, that same corruption happened to him in the index finger of his hand.
Therefore he came to me, and I tried to recover that excess with what I put on his hand from medicines after the cleansing of his body: and the excess was not repelled, and it began to go into another finger: until the corruption began in the hand.
Therefore he called me, to cut off his hand: and I did not want that, hoping from myself concerning the repulsion of that excess: and I also feared for him concerning the cutting off of his hand, death. Because the man's strength was above the event.
Therefore, after he despaired of me, he returned to his own land: and it came to me concerning him, that he hastened, and cut off his whole hand: and he was healed.
And I have not brought this account, except that it may be a help concerning that which falls under the kind of this illness: and that there may be a meaning by which it may be signified, and according to which it may be acted.
Concerning the care of sinuses, and the quality of clysterization with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in certain fleshy members, and the space of the abscess is prolonged until it makes a collection of pus: then it breaks or is perforated, and that which is in it of pus goes out, and a vacant place remains as if it were a vessel: and the skin which is over it is like a cloth which has been thinned: and it does not arrive at the end in its corruption, nor does it press upon a bone, nor upon a nerve, nor upon a ligament: it deserves to be named a Sinus: and it is not named a Fistula: however, if its space is prolonged until the corruption presses upon any of these members, then it is named a Fistula, or zuken.
And its cure is, that you incise that whole skin as it is rolled up: and especially when it has been thinned, and made like a cloth: and you are confident that it does not adhere to the place, because of its corruption.
But if you hope that the skin adheres, and that ultimate corruption does not follow from it: and if there is thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with a clyster: and it is, that you consider the Sinus.
Then if it is large, and the pus which is emitted from it is foul-smelling: then clysterize it with the Egyptian medicine, which is vinegar, and oil, and ziniar, taking equal parts, they are to be combined in a vessel: and they are to be boiled over a fire until the medicine reddens: and comes to the thickness of honey.
Then take of it what you need: and dissolve it with water and honey, and clysterize the Sinus with it: and bind its opening, and leave the medicine in it for the amount of two hours.
Then draw it out by pressing: do this for so many days, until the Sinus is cleansed, and the stench is removed.
And sometimes it is clysterized with ash water, when this medicine is not present to you: and it is, that you take the ash of vine wood, or ash from oak wood: and pour water over it. Then strain it, and clysterize with it, until you are confident that the sinus is now cleansed.
But if the patient does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine, nor the ash water, then clysterize him with water and honey, to which something of crushed ziniar is already mixed. Or clysterize him with honey and wine mixed together, because it is a property of honey to cleanse and wash; and wine causes the sinus to adhere, especially when there is an excess of astringency and dryness in the wine.
When you have done this several times, and are confident that the corruption of the sinus is now removed: then clysterize him with that which causes flesh to grow in it.
And it is [the method] to take some of the diafinicon plaster: and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine: or clysterize him with certain other medicines and ointments, whose properties I have written in the treatise on ointments.
But if the orifice of the sinus is constricted, so that the clyster cannula cannot enter: then enlarge it slightly with iron; or place in it a linen cloth rolled up in Egyptian ointment, or in basilicon ointment, until it is enlarged.
And similarly if the orifice is too wide, then bring its lips together with a suture: and leave from it an amount into which the clyster can enter without addition.
And similarly if the orifice from which pus runs is elevated higher: then incise it in a lower place which is in it, so that the pus runs down from it: because when pus is constricted in the depth of the sinus, it prevents flesh from growing in it.
But if it is not possible for you to incise the sinus around its lower part as you wish: then try to prepare the member with a preparation by which the pus runs from it easily as is prepared for you. Try that nothing of the pus is retained in it at all: and take consolidative medicines which are placed on the wound, suitable to what you wish. And spread them on a linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such a quantity that it makes contact with the whole sinus. Then incise with scissors in a straight line with the opening of the sinus, a hole slightly larger than the opening of the sinus. Then spread some mild ointments on another cloth, according to the size of the hole: and place it on top, at a distance from the opening of the sinus, so that the exit of pus from it is eased: and do not remove the large cloth, except after a few days at once.
But the one which is over the opening of the sinus, is the one which you remove every hour, so that what collects in it from pus may come out: and so that you may know if that which flows from it, the virus, is much, or little; or digested, or undigested.
And with this again consider the place of the sinus itself, whether its owner feels pain in it, or there is no pain in it, and whether there is an abscess in it, or there is no abscess in it.
When therefore your medication is extended thus, and you see the matter which comes out over the opening of the sinus, then know that the sinus is now adhering, or approaching adhesion: then therefore dissolve it in the morning, or on the third day: and when you dissolve the wound, then change to another cloth, and renew the ointment.
But if subtle virus comes out of the sinus after a long time, then do not despair of its adhesion, for its healing is approaching, because there are men who delay the generation of flesh in their wounds, and of them there are who act contrary to that.
But if undigested pus comes out of the sinus after many days, then know that the sinus does not adhere.
If therefore the adhesion of the sinus is delayed, and its matter is prolonged: then add to the drying of the medicines, truly let physicians do, so that the medicines are moist in their substance, and dry in their virtue: such as diafinicon ointment, when an excess of addition from zegi is added to it.
And of the most final by which it is cured, is that you take diafinicon ointment, and dissolve it with rose oil, and sprinkle over it old wine equal in its substance.
Then mix it with it, and use it.
And now it does something similar to this action, when you do not have diafinicon ointment present, honey, when you boil it until it thickens, and you use it. Or take myrrh, and aloe, and frankincense: grind the whole, and powder it over honey while it is over the fire. Then spread over it a cloth, and bind it over the sinus: and spread with the honey which you have boiled until it has thickened: and powder over it the spices, and place over it a cloth, and bind it.
And sometimes there is administered in it long birthwort, and roots of the celestial lily, and there is lily: and herb flour, and centaury. Use them simple, or compounded, grind them, and sift them: and powder over the honey which I have mentioned, and use it.
But if the matter of the sinus is prolonged, and is not healed by this treatment: then know that in its depth is corruption: or it now makes an impression on the bones, or on the other hard members, or ligaments: then treat it with the treatment of fistulas, according to what has been premised.
Concerning the treatment of whitlow and of the worn nail, and the incision of the added finger, and section.
Whitlow is an abscess which is born under the nail of the thumb of the hand, or foot: and perhaps it is born in the other fingers.
Therefore, when its matter is prolonged, and its cure is neglected, and it becomes inflamed with a hot abscess: or it is corrupted, and emits pus, until it corrodes the root of the nail: and perhaps it corrupts it entirely, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone, until it has a foul odor: and the end of the finger becomes wide, and its color becomes dark.
Therefore, when you treat it with what we have said in the division, and your treatment does not help: then it is necessary that you incise with iron all the excess which remains of the nail. Then cauterize the wound afterwards. For cautery is very helpful.
But if the bone is healthy, and the nail is also healthy, and the empty additions of the nail already compress the flesh inwards, and begin to prick it, and injure it: then it is necessary that you place a thin spatula under the angle of the nail which pricks the flesh, and push it upwards: and incise that flesh easily, and put on what remains of the flesh burning, corrosive medicines, until all of it is removed. Then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption already penetrates the bone, then it is necessary that you incise that bone, and extract it: for it is never healed while the corrupt bone remains in it.
But if you see corrosion and corruption proceeding in the finger, then incise it at one of the joints, according to what has been previously mentioned. Then treat it until it is healed.
But if attrition or percussion should happen to the nail, and intense pain occurs in it, then it is necessary that you first bleed the patient. Then cut the nail with a sharp spatula with a transverse cut from above downwards.
And take care that the cut does not reach the flesh which is under the nail, for you will cause thereby intense pain to the patient, and it will be the cause of the growth of additional flesh in the place.
Then treat the place with that which soothes pain.
Moreover, the added digit which is born in some men's hands, perhaps is wholly fleshy; and perhaps in some of them there are bones, and perhaps there is a nail on it; and some of them are born at the root of a joint, or its origin is in some knuckles of the finger. Therefore, that which is born in some knuckles of a finger, does not move; and that which is born near the joint of a finger, perhaps moves.
Therefore, of those which are fleshy, the cutting of it is easy. That is, to cut it at its root with a broad spatula.
But the treatment of that whose origin is in the root of a joint, is difficult. Therefore abstain from its incision.
However, in that which is born in a finger at the last joint, then it is necessary that you first cut its flesh with a round incision down to the bone. Then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for it. Then treat it until it is healed.
Now, the consolidation which happens to fingers mutually, very often happens either from a person's birth, that is, they are born with it, or from the consolidation of a wound, or burning by fire, and similar things.
Therefore it is necessary that you cut that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form. Then place between them linen cloths, or cloths soaked in rose oil, so that they do not consolidate quickly, and separate them; or place between them a thin lead plate until the place consolidates as it should.
And similarly if a consolidation happens to some fingers with the palm, then cut that consolidation, according to what is prepared and suits the shape of the limb.
Concerning the incision of vices.
Varices are twisted veins, thick, full of melancholic superfluities which occur in many limbs of the body; and their occurrence is most common in the legs, especially in the legs of those who travel and labor, and who carry burdens.
Therefore it is necessary that you administer a shaking of the body from black bile, sometimes with a strong shaking.
Then bleed the patient in the basilic vein.
The treatment of them with iron is according to two methods. One of them is that they are cut, and the black blood is drawn out. And the other method is that the vein is raised, and the whole thing is drawn out.
Its incision is according to this method. First bathe the leg with much warm water until the thick, foul blood is dissolved. Then bind the patient's leg from the upper part of his hip to the lower part of the knee with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one, or two, or three places with a wide incision.
Then raise, that is, draw out the blood in your hand from the lower part of the leg upwards, and from above the phlebotomist ought to [proceed] first. Then cut the vein downwards until a quantity of blood flows out which you see is sufficient, and which the patient's strength can bear.
Afterwards bind it: and instruct him to abstain from foods generating black bile: and repeat the evacuation and phlebotomy when the veins are full, and that harms the patient.
Its elevation is in this manner. Shave the patient's leg if there is much hair on it. Then place him in a bath; or rub his leg with warm water until it reddens and the veins swell; or let him perform vigorous exercise, if a bath is not available, until the limb is warm.
Then cut the skin in a line with the vein, lengthwise; or at its end near the knee, or in its lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks, and uncover the vein on all sides until it is visible; and when it appears, you will see it is red tending towards blackness. When it is freed from the skin, you see it white, like a cord.
Then insert a spatula under it, until it is raised and emerges from the skin; suspend it with a blunt hook, gently.
Then make another incision near that incision, three fingers apart. Then strip the skin from the upper part of the vein until it appears. Then raise it with spatulas as you did before, and suspend it with another hook, as you did at first.
Then make another incision, or several incisions, if you need it. Then raise it, and incise it with the last incision near the heel.
Then pull it out to the incision that is above it.
And do this until you pull it out from the incision which is superior, or higher than all the incisions, until when it comes out entirely: then incise it.
But if it does not obey you to be pulled out and raised: then insert a needle with strong double thread, and tie it, and pull it out: or insert a probe under it: and rotate your hand to every part, until it comes out. And beware lest it be incised. For if it is incised, it becomes difficult for you to raise it very much, and harm from it enters upon the patient.
Therefore, when you have pulled it out entirely: then place over the place of the wounds wool submerged in wine and rose oil, or oil: and treat it until it is healed.
But if the splinter is contracted, having a tortuosity to which there is a winding around the parts, and is not as we said according to straightness: then it is necessary that you split it in every part of its tortuosity, and the places of its winding.
Then suspend it with hooks, until you raise it entirely: and beware in your section upon it lest you cut a vein, or wound it: for it will become difficult for you to raise, or extract it. Therefore preserve your prudence.
The form of the incision by which a splinter is healed.
The form of a blind hook.
It does not have a curve like the other hooks, nor is it sharp-ended, so that it does not wound a vein: and it is of thick, gentle curvature; because if it were thin, it would cut a vein by its thinness: indeed, let it have superfluous thickness, as we have said.
Of the extraction of a vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, such as Algezira, and the land of the Arabs, and in arid, hot lands of little softness, that is, in which grass or trees do not grow.
And perhaps it is generated in other places of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is indeed from putrefaction which happens under the skin, just as serpents and ascarid worms and worms generated between the skin and flesh happen within bodies.
And the sign of the beginning of this vein is that a vehement inflammation happens in the leg. Then the place is blistered. Then the vein begins to come out from the place of that blister, as if it were the root of a plant or an animal.
Therefore, when its extremity appears from it, then it is necessary that you wrap around it a piece of lead, whose weight is from one kl drachm up to two drachms, and tie it: and let the lead hang from the leg.
Therefore, as often as something comes out of it to the exterior, wrap it in lead and tie it. But if it is prolonged much, then cut part of it and wrap the remainder. And do not cut it at the root before it has all come out. Because if you cut it, it contracts and enters into the flesh: and it causes an abscess and putrefaction in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore it is necessary that it be treated gently and pulled out little by little, until it comes out completely, and nothing of it remains in the body: and from this vein there comes out in some men that which is five and ten palms in length. And it has already come to me that it came out of a man twenty palms long.
But if it is cut into during your treatment, then insert a probe into its opening, and perforate it with a long opening, together with the body, until all that is in it of matter is evacuated: and treat the putrefaction of the place with medicines for several days. Then treat it with the treatment of abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears in the joint of the foot, or in the foot itself: and many openings happen to it, and a branch comes out of every opening.
Therefore treat it, as we said in the division, and with what has been stated before.
Concerning the incision for worms generated under the skin, and it is called bovine illness.
This illness is called in a certain land among us bovine illness, because, for it very often happens to cattle, and it is a small single worm which is born between the skin and the flesh.
And it goes throughout the body, ascending and descending; and its movement from limb to limb is perceived, until it breaks through wherever it breaks through the skin, and emerges.
And its generation is from the putrefaction of certain humors. Just as worms and serpents, that is, roundworms and threadworms, occur in the belly.
And it is feared because of its malignancy. Because when it goes in the body and rises to the head and arrives at a place, then perhaps it opens there and emerges, and destroys the eye: and this happens many times.
Therefore, when you wish to cure it and extract it, then this is not done except at its movement and its appearance to the sense.
Therefore it is necessary that you bind what is above it and what is below it with a strong ligature. Then cut above it and extract it.
But if it penetrates deeply into the flesh and you do not find it, then apply a cautery with fire to the place until it dies.
And what is more feared is its corruption, by which it corrupts the eye, as we have said.
But if you see it has already reached the head near the eye, then bind the eyebrow below it with a good ligature. Then cut above it and extract it.
And it is necessary that the patient use a cleansing of his body with medicines that resolve putrid, bad humors.
And let him beware of foods that produce putrefaction.
Concerning the incision for the disease Nakir.
This disease is called Nakir in our land: and it is a pain which happens in certain members; then it is transferred from member to member.
And I indeed have already seen it, according to what I relate to you. I was called to a sick woman in certain villages. And she uncovered her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm's cord.
And after I remained one hour, I saw one swelling proceeding with speed, just as a worm proceeds, ascending to her shoulder more quickly than it can be: so that it is like quicksilver, when it runs from place to place.
Therefore the pain departed from that place, and was fixed in the shoulder.
Then I sat for one hour, and it ran into the rest of the body, until it arrived in the other arm.
Then she recounted to me, that it was turning in her whole body, according to what I saw.
Therefore I marveled at the speed of its change from member to member. And indeed I had not before seen this illness with my own eyes in this manner. Nevertheless I saw many who found pain change from member to member: and I did not see it with my eyes, as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think that it is, except that it is because, the woman was of those who are from the villages of dry bodies and exposed veins: hence therefore that changing windiness appears to the senses.
And it is necessary that they not appear according to this reason in those who are delicate, and with moist bodies, and hidden veins.
When therefore you wish its cure, and the patient feels that pain: then if it is apparent to you to the eye, as we said, then bind above it, and below it quickly: and cut above it, until that constricted windiness departs: and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it with your eyes: then cure it with shaking of the body, and with that which expels windinesses, and purges them with foul pills, and pills of serapinum, and similar things.
Concerning the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their kinds, and according to the places in which they fall in the body.
Their diversity is this: for among them are those which have large and small points; and concave and blunt ones. And among them are those which have three angles. And among them are those which have barbs. And among them are those which have small points.
Those, however, which are according to the members in which they fall, are according to two methods. For either the members are of the principal concave members, such as the brain, and heart, and liver, and lung, and kidneys, and intestines, and bladder, and similar things. When therefore it falls in any of these members, and the signs of death, which I shall relate to you later, appear to you, then it is necessary that you avoid the extraction of that arrow from them, for death follows him in whom it is, in most instances. And when those bad signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not hidden in the depth of the member, then extract it, and treat the wound.
For the signs of the brain when an arrow falls into it, and penetrates the bone, and exits the membrane which is above the brain, are: that there occur from it violent thirst, and dimness of vision, and dizziness, and redness in the eyes, and swelling, and redness of the tongue, and spasm, and confusion of mind, and vomiting of bile. And perhaps blood comes out from the nostrils, or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off, and the voice is destroyed. And a white moisture similar to pap emerges from the place of the wound. And something like the watery part of flesh comes from it.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment, and extract the arrow if it does not come out.
However, the signs of an arrow when it falls into the heart are: that it is near the left nipple; and one feels with it as if it were fixed in something hard, not in something empty. And perhaps the arrow has a movement similar to the movement of the pulse; and black blood runs from the wound. And that is followed by coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting. Know therefore that death approaches without doubt.
And a sign of an arrow when it wounds the lung, is the exit of frothy blood from the wound: and the vessels which follow the neck become inflamed, and the color of the sick man changes. And he breathes a large, deep breath; and he seeks the intake of cold air.
But if the arrow falls on the covering which is in the chest: then it is near the small ribs: and there is great shortness of breath with vehement pain, with sighing: and all the limbs of the shoulders are moved.
And if the arrow falls into the stomach, then perhaps something undigested from food comes out of the wound.
And if an arrow falls into the belly, and is fixed in it: something of the excrement comes out of the wound, or pus, or intestine which has already been broken. Therefore there is no hope in his cure, nor in the extraction of the arrow.
But if an arrow falls into the bladder, and urine comes out: and something proceeds from it to the outside, and the pain is stronger upon the patient: then know that it is mortal.
The remaining members, such as the face, and neck, and throat, and shoulder blade, and clavicle, and the vertebrae of the back, and breastbone, and hip, and leg, and similar members are saved according to many dispositions: when the arrow does not find an artery, or nerve: and the arrow is not poisonous.
And I tell you what I saw of the nature of these arrows, that you may signify by that concerning your cure.
That is, because an arrow fell upon a certain man in the tear duct of his eye, at the root of the nose. Therefore I extracted it for him from the other side, under the fat of his ear: and he was healed, and no harm happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which fell upon him in the fat of his eye under the lower eyelid: and the arrow was already hidden, and I obtained from it only a small part of its point, with which it adhered to its shaft: and it was a large arrow from the bows of the Turks, with a smooth iron head, in which there are no barbs.
Therefore the Jew was healed, and no evil accident happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And it was an Arabian arrow, and it is one which has barbs. Therefore I cut over it between two guides: and it was already submerged in his throat. Therefore I worked delicately with it, until I extracted it: and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man which had fallen into his belly: and we thought that he would die from it.
After therefore thirty days had passed for him, and nothing of his conditions had changed, I cut over the arrow: and I worked on it, and extracted it: and he was healed, and no bad accident happened to him.
And I saw a man into whose back an arrow had fallen, and the wound had healed over it. After therefore seven years had passed, the arrow came out at the root of his hip.
And I saw a woman into whose belly an arrow had fallen, and the wound had healed over it, and the arrow remained inside her: and none of her conditions changed, and the woman found no harm from it in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man, into whose face an arrow had fallen: and the wound healed, and it remained, not causing him much pain: and many things similar to these.
And I extracted an arrow from a man of the King's princes, which had fallen into the middle of his nose, and had now deviated a little to the right side: and the whole arrow was hidden.
Therefore I was called to his treatment after the arrow's impact for three days. Therefore I found the arrow wound very tight.
Therefore I searched for it with a slender probe, and I did not feel it: and he indeed felt a pricking and pain beneath his right ear. Therefore I hoped that that pricking was from the point of the arrow.
Therefore I plastered the place with a plaster in which there was attractive and digestive power, trusting in myself that the place would suppurate, and the sign of the arrow would appear to me, so that I might cut over it: and there did not happen in the place an event signifying that the arrow had reached the place.
Therefore I persisted with the plaster on him for many days: and no event happened, and the wound meanwhile healed: and he remained ill from its extraction for a space of days, until he felt the arrow one day inside his nose. And he told me that.
And he told me that. Therefore I put a sharp, corrosive medicine on that wound for many days, until it was opened: and when I probed it, I felt the point of a slender arrow adhering to the wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound by means of that sharp medicine, until the point of the arrow appeared to us visibly: and about four months of time passed with him.
Then after the wound was enlarged, and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it, and move it, and it did not yield to extraction: then I did not cease to refine and devise means concerning it with ingenious methods, until I began on it one day with decently made forceps, according to what their form will come at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And indeed, physicians judge that the cartilage of the nose is not restored. Therefore I restored it, and the wound consolidated, and the patient was healed with complete health: nor did anything whatsoever harm him.
And I relate to you the quality of the extraction of certain arrows: so that you may put that as a rule and explanation concerning that which I did not say.
Because the parts of this art and their distinctions are not grasped with narration: nor does the book grasp that, and the prudent artisan compares with a little concerning much: and with that which is present, concerning that which is absent: and finds a new operation, and a new instrument in the course of extraneous things of this art when they happen.
Therefore I say that arrows do not exit from the members in which they are fixed except according to two methods. Or by traction from the place from which it entered. Or by the opposite of the other part.
And that which exits from where it entered, is either an arrow proceeding in a fleshy place: and it is drawn out, and exits.
But if it does not obey exit at the hour in which it falls: then it is necessary that you leave it for a few days, until the flesh which is around it putrefies: and its traction will be alleviated, and its exit.
And similarly if it is fixed in a bone, and does not obey you for exit, then leave it also for a few days: and repeat it with traction and movement every day, until it exits.
But if it does not obey you for exit after days, then it is necessary that you perforate around the arrow in the bone itself from every side with a thin drill, until the arrow is widened. Then draw it, and extract it.
But if the arrow is now hidden in the place of the body, and is concealed and hidden from the senses: then search for it with a probe. But if you feel it, then draw it out with certain instruments which are suitable for its extraction.
But if you cannot get to it, because of the tightness of the wound, and because of the length of the arrow deep within: and there is no bone, nor nerve, nor vein there: then cut it open above it, until the wound is widened, and it is possible to extract the arrow.
But if it has barbs by which it is held, then free the flesh fixed in them on every side, with every means that you can, and the means, if it cannot be done without damaging the flesh in breaking the barbs and twisting them, until it comes out.
And when you are managing the extraction of the arrow, wherever it is, then use the twisting of your hand, with forceps on all sides, until you make it come out: and subtle things with subtlety, so that the arrow is not broken, and its extraction becomes difficult for you.
But if you cannot reach it within an hour, then leave it for a few days, until those things which are around it putrefy. Then try again; for the swelling will be lessened. But if the flow of blood opposes you, then administer what we said of treatment in its chapter: and guard your strength from cutting a vein, or nerve, or tendon.
And administer the means in every way that is possible to you for the removal of the arrow: and let it be with ease and calmness, or slowness and firmness, as I have told you.
And it is necessary that you do, as you pull out the arrow, that the patient be positioned in the same manner as he was when the arrow struck him. Because it is more helpful. But if that is not possible for you, then administer what is possible to you from the positions.
But an arrow which emerges from the opposite side, or something already proceeds from it to the exterior: or the tip of the arrow makes a swelling by sensation from the nearby upper part of the skin, and you see it prominent: therefore cut over it, and let the incision be such that forceps can grasp it.
Then pull it out: for it will then be easy to remove.
But if it is retained in the bone, then twist your hand according to its roundness, until the arrow is embedded in the bone, and enlarges itself: then pull it out.
And if not: leave it for a few days: then repeat this, until it comes out.
But if the wood of the arrow is in it, then push it with that.
If, however, the wood has fallen off, and you wish to administer a push: then insert a concave instrument into it, so that its concavity enters the tail of the arrow: then push it with that.
But if the arrow is concave, then push it with an instrument entering into it. For the arrow is lightened by that.
But if the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary that you hollow out all the flesh in which the poison is, if it is possible for you. Then treat it with what is suitable for it.
But if the arrow is lodged in the chest, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side: and it is near to what you touch with a probe, and it is possible for you to cut over it: then cut, and beware of cutting a vein or nerve, and extract it.
Then sew the wound, if you see it necessary, and treat it until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps with which arrows are extracted.
Let the ends be similar to a bird's beak, which are made like iron: when they grasp the arrow, they do not let go of it.
And many types are made of them, large and small and medium-sized, according to the size and smallness of the arrow, and the size of the wound, and its narrowness.
The form of a concave pusher.
And the form of a blunt pusher.
This is a blunt end like a radius, so that its entry into the concave arrow is eased, and it is pushed with it.
Concerning the phlebotomy of veins.
The veins to be phlebotomized, whose custom has run in the body, are thirty.
Of these, sixteen are in the head: two pulsating veins, which are behind the ears, called the two Herbae; and two arteries, which are apparent in the two temples; and two veins which are in the two lachrymals of the two eyes, which are called Aspicientes; and a prepared vein in the middle of the forehead; and a vein which is at the tip of the nose. And two Guidegi, which are in the neck. And two veins which are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins which are in the lower lip. And these are the veins which are called Algecherich. And two veins which are under the tongue.
The veins, moreover, which are bled in the arms, are five veins in each arm.
One of them is the Cephalic, and it is from the outer side: and the common people call it the vein of the head. And the dark one. And there is the median vein, and it is composed of a branch of the Basilic and a branch of the Cephalic: and the common people call it the vein of the body. And the Basilic, and it is placed on the inner side: and it is also called Titillicaris. And the common people call it the vein of the belly. And the rope of the arm, and it is situated above the forearm; and it is in this that bloodletting is performed, and it is that which appears above the thumb by clear observation.
And the Salvatella: and it is the vein which is between the ring finger and the little finger: and it has two branches.
And in the leg and in the foot there are three veins. One of them is that which is under the curve of the thumb from the outer side. And the second is the Saphena: and its location is near the heel from the inner side. And the vein Nesse: and its location is near the heel from the outer side.
And in the other leg there are three veins similar to those.
Concerning the phlebotomy of two veins behind the ears.
Indeed, phlebotomy of the two veins which are behind the ears is helpful for chronic catarrhs, and hemicrania, and  deafness, and bad old head ulcers.
And the quality of phlebotomizing it is as I relate. And it is so that the head of the patient is shaved: and his occiput in the place of the two veins is rubbed with a rough cloth with a good rubbing. Then let the patient constrict his neck with a band, until the two veins appear. And their locations are behind the ears in two depressed places of the head. Therefore seek them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, there mark with a cautery.
Then take a lancet phlebotome, and it is that which is called Incidens. Then insert it under the vein in the skin, until the phlebotome arrives at the mouth. Then raise your hand with the vein and skin upwards, and incise the vein with the skin with an incised incision. And let the length of the incision be the amount of two joined fingers, or about that: and let out as much blood as you wish.
Then bind it with lint, and leave it until it heals.
And already the incision of them, and their cauterization, preceded in the beginning of the book.
Truly, of the two arteries which are in the two times of phlebotomy, the help is for old hemicrania: and bad toothache, and persistent ophthalmia: and the course of sharp effused superfluities to the eyes.
And the quality of phlebotomizing them is according to what I relate to you. Let the sick person strongly constrict his neck until two veins appear manifestly to the sense of sight: and let their pulse be demonstrated under your finger. Then mark them with cautery.
Then raise the skin above the vein upwards with your index finger, and insert the cutting phlebotomist below, and raise the vein upwards. And incise it, as you did in the other two veins: and make the blood flow according to the amount of your necessity.
Then loosen the constriction of the sick person, and place your finger on the vein for one hour. Afterwards place a compress and pledgets on it, and bind it above with a strong constriction: and leave it until it is healed.
And now the recollection of them, and their incision, and their extraction has preceded in the beginning of the book.
The benefit of phlebotomy of the vein of the forehead is, after phlebotomy of the Cephalic vein, for old facial illnesses: and for hemicrania, and redness: and foul ulcers.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is, according to what I relate to you. The infirm person constricts his neck with a band until the vein appears. Then take the instrument which is called a Fossorium.
Place the prominent point which is on the head of the Fossorium upon the vein itself, and strike upon it with a comb, or with another thing in its manner: and let the blood run according to the quantity which you wish. Then loosen the perforation of the infirm, and bind it until it heals.
And sometimes it is opened with a broad phlebotome. However, it is not necessary that the phlebotome be of a sharp extremity, like the other phlebotomes, but rather of a broad extremity. And phlebotomize it according to the transverse: because the bone is near: and perhaps in it the phlebotome will be broken when it is thin.
But the help of the two veins which are in the two lachrymals of the two eyes, is for the eye from scab and redness: and sebil in the eye, and sicknesses of the face.
And the manner of phlebotomizing them is that the infirm person should bind his neck with a fillet. Then phlebotomize them: and you be standing over his head.
And let the phlebotomy be made across to a little length, with a small, somewhat broad phlebotome, for there is no flesh in the place. For if the phlebotome is of a thin extremity, perhaps it will break.
Then make run out of blood as much as is necessary for you. And place over them a pledget, and bind them one night, and unbind them.
Moreover, the phlebotomy of the veins of the nose is beneficial for acute fever, and violent headache, and diseases of the face, such as hemicrania, and redness which happens in the face; especially when it is of long standing.
And the manner of its phlebotomy is that the infirm person should bind his neck. Then hold his nose with your left hand, and take a thin, long phlebotomy instrument; and insert it in the middle of his nostril, between that which divides the nose according to its straightness. Because the vein does not appear to the sense there, and because the blood begins to flow immediately.
And it is necessary that you hold your hand with the phlebotomy instrument a little; and you cause the blood to flow according to your need. Then bind it one night. For it is restored quickly.
Indeed, the phlebotomy of two Guidegi helps constricted breathing, and the beginning of leprosy, and melancholic illnesses, and impetigo and bad, corroding ulcers, that is, herpetic esthiomenes.
And the manner of their phlebotomy is that the sick person binds himself under both in his neck with a ligature; and the surgeon stands over the head of the sick person, and the sick person sits on a seat.
Then the phlebotomist opens the vein to the length of a sufficiently wide phlebotomy. Then draw out a moderate quantity of blood, or according to what you see is necessary for it. Then do similarly with the other vein.
Afterwards, loosen the ligature, and bind the two veins with a moderate compression, so that the sick person is not strangled. And leave it until morning. For the wound heals.
Concerning the phlebotomy of veins, Albucasis. Moreover, the vein according to Albucasis is an aid to phlebotomy, because it contributes to canker in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and bad ulcers, and fissures of the lips, and bad ulcers, even those which are in the nose and in its circumference.
And the manner of phlebotomy of them is that you make the infirm one sit before you, and bind his neck with a fillet. Then roll back his lip and look at the two veins; one of which is on the right of the lip, and the second on the left of it; and the indication of each is by the blackness of both. And this is true: because in their circumference are small, black veins. Therefore incise each with an incised incision.
But if there is doubt upon you, and you do not know which of them it is, then attend to the incision of the larger of them, and the more manifest of them.
And similarly do with the two veins which are in the upper lip: and indeed custom runs very much with it: and incise the two veins which are in the lower lip.
Concerning phlebotomy of the vein under the tongue after cephalic phlebotomy. Truly, the help of phlebotomy of the two veins which are under the tongue is that cephalic phlebotomy is for obstructions which are in the throat, and disease of the uvula, and diseases of the mouth.
And the quality of phlebotomizing them is that the infirm person sits between your hands before the sun: and lift his tongue, and look under the tongue on one side of it for a vein, and on the other side of it for a vein: and the color of each inclines to blackness.
Therefore phlebotomize both: and take care that you do not wound your hand in phlebotomizing them. For arteries are beneath them, and perhaps a flow of blood from those arteries will happen.
Moreover, the three veins which are phlebotomized in the elbow are those in whose phlebotomy custom runs in all men.
And the phlebotomy of them is done according to two methods. Either by pricking with a broad myrtle phlebotome: or an olive-shaped one, sloping to a point: or by cutting with a knife-shaped phlebotome, and that is Alnesil. And this is its form.
And of them there are broad and subtle types according to the width and narrowness of the veins. The form of the myrtle phlebotome is broad, as you see. And it is suitable for opening concave, full, prominent, apparent, thick veins, and those which contain thick, turbid blood.
And this phlebotome is of less breadth, and of a more subtle point.
And it is suitable for the phlebotomy of subtle veins which contain subtle, yellow blood.
The Basilica, moreover, which is one of these three veins, is an aid to phlebotomy. Because it draws blood from illnesses which are under the throat and neck, from that which follows the chest, and the belly: and it befits the phlebotomist, when he phlebotomizes it, that he be cautious, and be on guard against it, for under it is an artery. If therefore he errs, and adds to the puncture of the phlebotomy, he will cut that artery, and a flow of blood will result. Therefore it is necessary that he not phlebotomize it with a pointed phlebotomy instrument; rather, let it be cut with an Alnesil.
But if the Basilica does not appear in manifest appearance, then it is necessary that you abandon it, and change to another, or seek some branch of it: or phlebotomize in its place the Funiculus of the arm. For it itself is manifest, and cut it with the Anesil phlebotomy instrument, as we said.
But if you wish the phlebotomy of it itself, then it is necessary before constriction of the arm, that you touch the place, until you know the place of the pulse. Then mark the place with a cautery, afterwards the arm, and cut the vein with a concave incision with the Alnesil phlebotomy instrument, as we said. And beware lest the incision fall through the space from the place of the artery.
And when you see, when you tighten the ligature, swelling in the place which you marked with the cautery, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery: therefore avoid it.
But if you see the blood at the phlebotomy leaping up, like the urine of a boy, and it is thin blood, red: then know that it is from the blood of the artery. Then therefore hasten, and put your finger on it for a long space, then remove your finger. If therefore the blood is cut off (for it is often cut off) then bind the arm, and release it: and let the patient beware of its movement: and let him be upon his guard.
And let him not move it for several days, until it is healed.
But if the blood is not cut off, and it overcomes you: and medicine is not present to you in your moment, then cut the artery, if it appears to you. For its extremities are constricted, and the blood is cut off.
Or take the bark of a fistuca, and split it: take one half, and bind it over the place of the vein's constriction decently, with a bandage and lint until the next day.
Then if the blood is cut off, it is good. And if not: then treat it with that whose mention preceded from the place of powders cutting off the flow of blood: and the cutting off of its blood is not difficult in most instances, because of the smallness of the wound's place, and the possibility of a bandage from the arm.
Moreover, the help of phlebotomy of the dark vein is that it draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; therefore, because it is composed of a branch of the basilica and a branch of the cephalic, as we have said.
And it is fitting for the phlebotomist that he be wary of its phlebotomy, for under it is a nerve. If therefore he adds to the puncture of the phlebotomy, and finds a nerve, numbness happens in it, and its healing becomes difficult, and perhaps it is not healed completely.
And this nerve often appears to the sense. But if it is hidden in some men, and is subtle, and is not shown: then it is necessary that you place your phlebotomy where you phlebotomize, an incision with the lancet, and avoid the nerve with your strength.
But if the vein is between two nerves, then split the vein lengthwise.
Concerning the Cephalic vein. Moreover, phlebotomy of the Cephalic vein is helpful, because it draws blood from the head, and contributes to the illnesses of the eyes.
And it is necessary in this vein properly, if you wish, that you phlebotomize it by puncturing with an olive-shaped phlebotome, or with a myrtle-shaped phlebotome: because it is the safest of all veins, because there is no artery beneath it, nor nerve.
Nevertheless it is fitting for you in phlebotomy, that you avoid with the phlebotome only the head of the biceps, and seek a softer place: and it does not harm it, if it is not found with the first incision, that it is repeated upon it with phlebotomy several times.
Nevertheless perhaps it becomes inflamed in some men when it is not phlebotomized at the first incision, but that inflammation does not harm it in anything.
Concerning the usefulness of phlebotomy, and the precautions to be observed in it. Moreover, the quality of phlebotomy, and its consequences therefrom, in whose rectification it is necessary for you to think.
First therefore of it is what you ought to know, that phlebotomy is not administered, except in the preservation of health, and its continuance, and caution from the advent of illnesses.
And so that phlebotomy may be done in one of the three veins which are in the elbow, namely the Cephalic, and the Median, and the Basilic. And that phlebotomy be done at the beginning of Spring upon fasting, in spring and summer: and in autumn and winter after a meal, when signs of repletion appear.
And let phlebotomy be done on the first day, or on the third, after three hours have passed from the day.
Phlebotomy however which is administered in illnesses, does not have a determined hour: but when expediency and necessity prompt to it, by night or by day, and at every hour, and at every time.
And it is not fitting that boys be bled until fourteen years have come upon them. Nor let a pregnant woman be bled except from the fourth month to the seventh. Nor let old men be bled who have already passed sixty years.
Therefore when anyone wishes to be bled for whatever reason it may be: it is necessary that he cleanse his bowels before phlebotomy with a gentle clyster, if much retained dung is in them: so that the veins do not draw up during phlebotomy putrid superfluities from the bowels, harmful to the principal members.
Nor let a patient suffering from nausea, that is, sickness, be bled; nor one suffering from indigestion, nor one recovering from drunkenness, until that has been removed from them. Nor one ill with dropsy.
And let phlebotomy be avoided again after colic, vomiting, looseness of the bowels, excessive sexual intercourse, labor, exercise, wakefulness, fasting, and everything that weakens the strength of the body or the animal spirits.
Then consider the subtlety of the humors beforehand, if the blood is thick with food and drink and medicines: if it is possible for him to have that.
Then let him enter a bath, if nothing prevents him; or let him take some exercise, so that the blood may be thinned.
And place his phlebotomy in the chest, around the third hour of the day, as we have said: and strive that his chest be evacuated that day of all evil accidents, both animal, such as anguish, anger, and fear; and bodily, such as labor, pain, excessive sexual intercourse, and the like.
And let there be present in his seat things with whose use the custom of men runs, from aroma and fragrance, and dancing and similar things, to the extent possible.
Then let the phlebotomist sit on a cushion which is higher than the cushion of the person being bled; afterwards let him draw out his arm, and let the phlebotomist rub it with his hand twice or thrice. Then let him tighten the ligature with a strap, and wrap it around twice.
And let the constriction be moderate, because when the constriction is uneven, or excessive, then it prevents the flow of blood; or if it is too loose, then also it prevents the flow of blood.
Then after the constriction it is necessary that the person being bled rub his hands together until the veins swell and become apparent to the sense.
Then let the phlebotomist wipe the place to be bled with a little old oil.
Then let him place the index finger of his left hand on the vein itself a little below the place where he wishes to bleed, so that the vein does not slip away and the incision is not missed. For among the veins there are some which you find like a fleeing string during phlebotomy; and there are some which are full of wind; and when you place the phlebotomy instrument on it, it contracts under the instrument, and the phlebotomist is deceived, and does not open the vein; and if he opens it, it is narrow.  Therefore it is necessary that the phlebotomist be firm and careful in all these matters.
Then let him remove the phlebotomy instrument. If therefore the vein is opened at once, then it is good. And if not, then repeat it another time a little below that place, or above it quickly, if the place is not inflamed.
But if it is inflamed, or the patient is frightened, then leave him for a day or two days; and do not tighten the ligature. For perhaps it will draw a hot abscess. Nor should he enter a bath.
Then let him repeat the phlebotomy, if he wishes.
But if the opening is small, or the blood flows thinly, and you fear that the quantity of blood you desire will not come out, then repeat the phlebotomy in the same hole, lengthwise, and add a little to the operation.
And do this quickly, before the place becomes abscessed, for an abscessed place is in many men a small operation.
If therefore you see it already abscessed, then do not repeat it at all, for it will excuse nothing in you. And place something of oil dregs upon it; for it alleviates the flow of blood. And it is better in this place than oil itself, and than other oils.
And similarly administer oil dregs in all your phlebotomy, which phlebotomizes veins against the prohibition of blood flow: and they do this, theriac alfarach and sagzenea, when one of them is placed upon the place.
For blood is thinned and resolved when it is thick.
But if a large abscess should occur at the place of phlebotomy, for it often happens, especially to him who is not phlebotomized except at that time, or the opening of the vein is small, then hasten and place upon the place a sponge infused in water and salt, warmed a little: and bind it for one hour: for it is dissolved.
And it is necessary that you do this after the exit of blood from that vein, with its completion, or from another vein.
But if something of blackness or greenness remains in the place after some days, it does not harm: and if you wish, place upon it something of aloe, or myrrh dissolved: or something of the juice of mint, that is, calamint or something similar to it.
And very often indeed abscesses and swellings occur at the phlebotomy of the basilica.
Place therefore your hand upon it, then if you find softness at your compression upon it, then that swelling is bad. Beware therefore, that you place upon it something of that which we have said. For perhaps blood of the artery will flow from it.
But make a plaster for it with that in which there is astringency, so that the place may be hardened. Then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
And it is necessary that you extract from every man from the blood according to the quantity of his strength. And that which appears from the color prevailing over the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it go out until it reddens: and similarly if you see it thick: then let it out until it becomes thin: and similarly if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for him who is full, strong, and needs bloodletting once, that the vein phlebotomy be widened, and the phlebotomy be wide: and in him who is weak, it should be the opposite of that.
And it is necessary that he extract it in stages, and that the opening be narrow: and better that which is administered in vein phlebotomy is that it be a transverse section, not a puncture.
And this method of phlebotomy is safe from the flow of blood, and from the cutting of a nerve: and it is more laudable, and safer than phlebotomy by width and length.
And to whom syncope is accustomed to come upon in phlebotomy, it is necessary that he eat before phlebotomy a little bread soaked in pomegranate juice: or sugarcane, if it is warmed: and extract from him of the blood in three times, or four.
And if he is of a cold complexion, then let him take before phlebotomy bread soaked in grape syrup, or in syrup of honey spiced with spices, or in fragrant spiced syrup.
But if syncope happens during phlebotomy, and its cause is the exit of much blood: then it is necessary that he take in drink meat water, and thin fragrant syrup: and let him use aromatherapy with aloes wood, and let his chest be anointed with it: and let him use the other things which we said in the division in the chapter on syncope from evacuation.
But he who wishes to evacuate his arm, and to release his blood a second time: it is necessary for him whose phlebotomy is for much evacuation, and whose strength is weak, that he release blood gradually according to the amount of strength in continuous days.
But he who wishes to evacuate his arm, and to release his blood a second time, and his body is strong: then let him do it after seven hours, or nine, from his first phlebotomy.
But he who wishes to draw blood from his body to the opposite of the part to which it inclines: it is necessary that he be evacuated on the second day, or on the third.
But in whose body is much blood already heated, and sharpened, and causes fever to happen: it is necessary that blood go out from him at one time, and that a large quantity be drawn from him: and the opening be widened until syncope happens: after you have observed all the conditions of phlebotomy: and that you place your hand upon his pulse during the flow of blood, so that death does not happen instead of syncope. For often that happens when the phlebotomist is ignorant, and negligence falls.
And it is not necessary, when you wish to open an arm and release blood a second time, when the opening of the vein is already closed, and the exit of blood becomes difficult, that it be pressed upon forcefully, and bent and extended quickly. For that is very bad; indeed, leave it until it is bled a second time.
Or remove with the sharpness of a phlebotomy instrument that which is coagulated from the blood in the opening of the vein, and place upon it something of salt dissolved in water. And place upon it something of theriac alfaruch, or sagzene, and apply gentle compression until the blood flows out.
