But if the vein is already abscessed, then leave it, and do not cover it until the abscess settles.
But if necessity demands that blood be let a second time, and there is no excuse, then either phlebotomize in that same place, or in another vein in the other arm.
The rope of the arm is phlebotomized in the place of the Median and Basilic veins, when they are not found and are hidden, because it is composed of them.
And the manner of this phlebotomy is that the sick person should put his hand in warm water until it readily reddens, and the vein clearly appears.
Then it should be bound above it with a ligature of moderate tightness. Then phlebotomize across it gradually, not laterally nor lengthwise: and let the opening be wide, and let your phlebotomy be such that you phlebotomize it slightly above the joint of the hand.
If the flow of blood is prevented, then put the hand back into warm water, and let the blood flow into the water, until you obtain your requirement.
But if it is in the summer months, then you are excused from putting the hand back into warm water. And indeed, the letting of blood into warm water is most often done in winter.
And phlebotomy of this vein is safer than all veins, because there is no pulsating vein nor nerve beneath it.
Phlebotomy of the Salvatella vein of the right hand, however, benefits liver diseases.
And the method of its phlebotomy is that you constrict the Salvatella vein of the hand with a ligature, or with your hand, after you have put it into warm water, until the vein swells and is very clearly seen. Then phlebotomize it transversely. And if you incise it completely, that will not harm it in any way.
And be careful not to excessively move your hand with the phlebotome. For beneath it are the nerves of the fingers, denuded of flesh.
Then return the hand to warm water, and let the blood flow into it. For if you do not return it to warm water, the blood coagulates in the opening of the vein, and is prevented from flowing.
Therefore, when you have drawn the necessary quantity of blood, then place oil and salt on the vein: so that it does not quickly clot.
And similarly it is necessary that you do with every constricted branch.
The benefit of phlebotomy from the left hand is: because it is helpful for diseases of the spleen; and similarly do in its phlebotomy as you did in the second, equally.
The benefit of phlebotomy of the saphenous vein is for diseases which are in the lower part of the body, such as diseases of the womb, and retention of menses, and weaknesses of the kidneys, and old ulcers of the hips and legs, and similar diseases.
And the manner of its phlebotomy is that the infirm person should put his foot in warm water: and make friction upon it, until the veins swell; and tie above the joint of the foot with a strap. And indeed the place of the vein is near the heel, appearing towards the thumb; and many branches ramify from it in the surface of the foot.
Therefore phlebotomize it in its larger branch, or near the heel in its confluence: because it is better and safer.
But if you phlebotomize it in the surface of the foot, then beware of the nerves which are beneath it on the surface of the foot.
And place your phlebotomy, by which you phlebotomize it, transversely, as if you wished to cut it; and let the phlebotomist be skillful.
But if the exit of blood is prevented, then bathe his foot in warm water: and let the blood flow into it, until it is evacuated.
But if the phlebotomist misses the vein at the first attempt, then let him repeat the phlebotomy a little higher, for the place is safe, and you do not fear harm from it, when you avoid the nerves, as we have said.
And similarly with the saphenous vein of the other foot equally.
The location of the sciatic veins, as we have said, is near the heel on the outer side.
And the benefit of its phlebotomy is for pain in the hip, when it is due to hot blood.
And the manner of its phlebotomy is that the patient should enter the bath and hurry; and bind his leg from the side of the hip to above the heel with four fingers' breadth of a long, thin band; for it does not appear except for that.
When therefore it appears, then phlebotomize it according to whatever method is possible for you, or according to a transverse cut: and it is better, or to incise it with an incision, or to cut it with a section. For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in most men.
For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in most men. But if you do not find it, and it does not appear to the sense at all: then phlebotomize one of its branches, and that is the one which appears on the dorsum of the foot, and towards the little toe: and beware of the nerves, and let the quantity of blood you wish flow.
Then loosen the binding, and place a leather over the place of phlebotomy, and bind the place. For it heals quickly.
Concerning cupping, and the quality of its use.
Cupping instruments are made from horns, and from metal, and from glass.
And cupping is done in two ways. With scarification, and extraction of blood: and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping which is without scarification, is according to two methods. Or it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And there are fourteen places on the body for cuppings which are administered with scarification and bloodletting. One of which is the cupping of Alnocrati, and it is at the back of the head: and Alkehel, and it is the middle of the neck: and the cuppings of Alhagdahim, and they are two plates of the neck on both sides together: and the cuppings of the chin, and it is under the jaw in the lower part of the mouth: and the cuppings of the two shoulder blades: and the cuppings of Alachosos above the end of the tailbone: and the cuppings of the two groins, and they are the middle of the two thighs: and the cuppings of the two legs: and the cuppings of the two ankles.
And cupping indeed does not draw blood from you from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and because of that, strength does not fall from them, as is the case with phlebotomy. Nor is it necessary that cupping be administered, whether with fire or without fire, in any illnesses which arise from repletion, until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity prompts cupping because of illness or custom, we administer it at any time at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, and in the middle of it, and at whatever time it may be.
Which is because there are men in whom blood multiplies until it needs extraction by cupping, and they find heaviness and headache in their head.
And there are some who find fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. And there are some who find itching in their face and forehead, and darkness and dullness in their eyes.
And there are some who scratch the place of their cupping. And there are some whose laughter increases. And there are some who find the taste of blood in their mouth: or the tongue is abscessed, and they spit blood. And there are some whose sleep increases. And there are some who see in their sleep blood and redness, and the slain, and wounds, and things similar to them.
When therefore we see something of these things, and especially if it is in the middle third of the month, then we order a bloodletting to be done, after two or three hours of the day have passed.
The help of cupping the nape of the neck is: because it contributes to heaviness in the head, and what is poured out into the eyes. But truly it is necessary that it be after evacuation of the whole body.
And indeed this cupping is in place of cephalic phlebotomy.
And it is not permitted that one use it who is of cold complexion, or in whom there is catarrh. For it inflicts great harm on him.
And because of that it is not necessary that the elderly use it, and those in whose head there are cold illnesses.
But whoever is assiduous over it, forgetfulness is generated in him.
And because of that it is necessary that you instruct the cupper that he put down his hand with the cupping glass a little to the lower parts, fearing the generation of forgetfulness.
Cupping or Alkehel is in place of the median vein phlebotomy, and the basilic vein phlebotomy, and because of that it benefits asthma, and constriction of breathing, and fissure of the instrument of breathing, and cough, and repletion.
And it is necessary that the Alkehel cupping glass be applied a little, because if it is placed lower, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
Cupping, however, alhagdahim, benefits pains happening in the head, and ophthalmia, and hemicrania, and perforation, and pain in the roots of the teeth: and it is in place of the basilic vein phlebotomy.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper, that he not press his hand with the scarification: so that he not cut an artery, and a flow of blood happens.
Cupping, however, under the chin, benefits canker sores in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar illnesses which are in the mouth: and it stands in place of the phlebotomy alieherich, which are in the two lips.
Cupping of the shoulder blades, however, benefits alchafacham, which is made from repletion and heat.
Cupping, however, of the inner parts of the two buttocks, benefits in assistance of phlebotomy of the three veins, Basilic, and Median, and Cephalic: because it itself draws blood from all those small veins which are in the flesh: and those small veins draw from other veins thicker than them, until the drawing reaches the three veins.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper, that he not proceed much in scarification: because the place is denuded of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
Cupping, moreover, which is cupped over the alchausos, benefits the hemorrhoids of the anus, and lower ulcers.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper, that the cupping glass be large, and that it be of brass: because the place needs strong suction, and perhaps thin cupping glasses are broken: and let it be scarified with a large scarification.
Cuppings, moreover, of the legs diminish with manifest diminution: because they draw blood from the whole body, and benefit old pains in the kidneys, and womb, and bladder: they provoke menstruation, and benefit bothe, and ademenul: and they stand in place of phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins, and alhorcobeni.
However, they weaken the body greatly, and cause syncope to happen in many men.
And the benefit of cupping of the alhorcobeni is close to the benefit of cupping of the two legs.
The quality of the placing of cupping glasses is that you place the cupping glass first empty, and suck with moderate suction: and do not prolong the placing of the cupping glasses, but place them quickly, and remove them quickly, so that the humors come to the place with equal arrival: and do not cease to iterate that and continue, until you see the place now redden and inflate, and the redness of blood appear: then therefore scarify, and iterate the suction gradually. Then consider the disposition of the bodies.
For he who is of men of tender flesh, of rare pores, it is necessary that you scarify him once, not more: lest the place be ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to enlarge the scarification, and deepen it a little: and moderate the suction in ease, and speed, and gentle movement.
But if there is thickness in the blood, then it is necessary that you scarify him twice. In the first instance indeed a way is opened for the thinning of the blood, and its watery part: and in the second indeed for the thorough examination of the extraction of thick blood.
If however the blood is very foul, then let a scarification be made a third time: that the ultimate result may be achieved.
And finally, when we wish to extract blood gradually, or a little, one scarification suffices for us. But if we wish the extraction of much blood, we scarify with many scarifications.
And if we measure that the blood is thick: then it is necessary that it be scarified with a deep scarification: and the equal limit in scarification is the depth of the skin.
What is that which ought to be administered of oils at the application of cupping-glasses, and what the cupped person should avoid.
In him indeed whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are constricted, it is necessary that the places to be cupped be anointed with opening, softening, resolving oils: if it is in the time of summer, such as is oil of alkeiri, or oil of violets, or oil of sweet almonds, or oil of pumpkin seed.
And if it is in the time of winter, then oil of narcissus, or oil of lily, or oil of chamomile, or oil of sarbatine: and similar things.
But if the superfluity is thick, cold: then let the oil be oil of marjoram, or oil of costmary, or oil of rue, or oil of dill: and similar things.
But if the person to be cupped has large pores, tender flesh: then it is necessary that he be forbidden oil, and it is necessary that those wash the places of the cupping-glasses after cupping with rosewater, or with cold water, or with water of solanum, or with water of pumpkin, or with water of purslane: and similar things.
But he whose blood is of much humidity, let him wash the cuppings with vinegar, or with myrtle water, and sumach: and similar things.
But he whose superfluities are thick, let him wash his cuppings with old wine, or with water of wormwood, or a decoction of dill, or chamomile: and similar things.
And it is necessary that cupping be avoided in a bath, or immediately after a bath; indeed, it is necessary that it be administered after leaving the bath for an hour, or for two hours.
And it is not necessary that anyone sleep after cupping.
What is it by which the cupper, or phlebotomist, ought to be governed before cupping?
And after it, it is necessary that you consider first: then if the cupper, or phlebotomist, is choleric, and sharpness and inflammation prevail over his blood: then it is necessary that he take cooling things, such as pomegranate, and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and succory, and julep: and similar things.
And put among his foods chickens, and the flesh of young sheep, and foods seasoned with vinegar: and similar things.
And whose complexion is cold, it is necessary that he take in drink syrup of honey, or of almiua, or alsecaniabin made from seeds: and let him take moderately fragrant wine, which is between old and new: and let him be instructed to use little food. And put among his food chickens, and canebir; and sparrows, that is, small birds; and let him eat young pigeons.
And it is necessary that drink on the day of cupping and phlebotomy be more than food: and it is necessary that in some men, in certain hours, theriac alfaroch, or a medicine of musk, that is, nutmeg, or  alesselithe be given to drink before cupping: and before phlebotomy, or after: so that the principal members may be strengthened, and the blood may be thinned. And it is not necessary that they take it in heated drink.
Concerning cupping without scarification. Cuppings, however, which are without scarification, are cuppings which are placed upon the liver, and spleen, and gout, and upon the belly, navel, and the regions of the kidneys, and the hip joint: because these members do not tolerate scarification upon themselves.
And nothing is sought by them, except either the attraction of blood from a member to a member: just as we place a cupping glass over the breasts in the illness of a nosebleed. And we use them to resolve from a member cold, hidden flatulence in a member: just as we place a cupping glass over the belly and navel. For it itself rarefies the member, and warms it, and removes pain because it resolves that flatulence.
It is also placed over the kidneys, when there occurs in them obstruction, or a stone. For by the virtue of its attraction perhaps it opens the obstruction, or removes the stone from its place.
And similarly it acts when placed over the liver and spleen with regard to grinding flatulence in both of them.
And these cupping glasses are administered empty with only an incision. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of warm water in cases of pleurisy: and that is so that the cupping glass is filled, and it is large, with warm water alone, or water in which suitable herbs have been decocted.
The form of a cupping glass administered with fire.
Let the width of its opening be two open fingers, according to what we have formed: and let its depth be half a palm: and let there be in its side, in the place which is about its middle, a small hole, according to the quantity that a needle enters it: let it be made of bronze or brass, of thick edges, smooth, even, polished: so that it does not injure the limb in its placement upon it: and let there be in its middle a transverse rod of bronze or iron, where a candle with fire is placed.
And now this cupping glass is made large, larger than that which we have described, and a little smaller than that, according to the illnesses and the age of the one who uses it.
For cupping glasses for children and thin people are different than cupping glasses for men and those with thick bodies.
The method of placing this cupping glass with fire upon a limb is to light a small linen wick with fire neatly, or a small wax candle: and place it upon the middle of the transverse rod which is in the middle of the cupping glass: so that the lit fire is around the lower part of the cupping glass, so that the body of the patient is not burned.
Then let it be placed upon the limb: and a finger should be placed over the hole which I mentioned, until when we hold the cupping glass: while we need to, we remove the finger, and the vapor will exit through that hole, and the cupping glass will be immediately removed.
Then relight the linen wick in its own way, and repeat it, if you need it.
In the cupping glass, however, which is administered in the illness of pleurisy with water, there is no transverse rod, nor hole: and it is not administered unless it is filled with water, and placed upon the limb only.
And this is its form.
And this cupping glass, the larger it is so that it may contain much water, is better.
Concerning the suspension of leeches.
Leeches are not administered in many [places], except in those members where the placement of cupping glasses is not possible, because of their smallness, such as the lip, and the gums, and similar things. Or in a member denuded of flesh, such as a finger, and a nose, and similar things.
And the manner of using them is: that you take leeches which are in fresh waters, clean from putrefaction.
Then let them be left day and night in fresh water, until they are hungry, and nothing remains in their bellies. Then let the body be evacuated first with phlebotomy, and with cupping glasses. Then wipe the member of the sick person until it reddens. Afterwards, let them be placed upon it.
Therefore, when they are full and fall off, and suction of the place with a cupping glass is possible, then it is most helpful: and if not, then wash the place with vinegar. Then with much water, and let it be rubbed, and squeezed out.
But if the flow of blood is prolonged after the falling off of the leeches, and it is oozing: then dip a linen cloth in cold water, and put it on top, until the oozing stops.
But if the blood increases, then powder over it crushed alum, or galls, and similar things from among the styptics, until the blood stops.
Or let halves of shelled beans be placed on the place: and let them remain until the beans stick to the place, for the blood will be stopped.
And it is necessary, if a repetition of leeches is necessary, to change them when it is possible.
But if leeches are prevented from adhering, then let the place be wiped with fresh blood. Or prick the places with a needle until something of the blood comes out. Then let them be applied, for when they sense something of the blood, they will adhere immediately.
Therefore, when you wish them to fall off, sprinkle on them something of powdered aloes, or salt, or ashes: and they will fall off immediately.
This chapter is also very necessary in the art of medicine, and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations occurring in bones.
Know, O sons, that foolish doctors and the common people boast of this chapter, and those who have never inspected the book of the ancients, nor read a letter from it.
And because of this reason, this method is lost from the sciences in our land. And I never encountered anything good in it, and I did not acquire from it what I acquired except through the extensive reading of ancient books and the study of their understanding, until I extracted its knowledge from them.
Then I adhered to experiment and study for the length of my life.
And now I have described to you from it in this chapter all that my knowledge contains: and what my experiment surpassed, after I abbreviated it for you, and freed it from the burden of prolixity, and shortened it with a fine abbreviation, and showed it with a fine arrangement: and I formed in it many forms of instruments which are used in it, as it is from the addition of arrangement, as I did in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter II.
It is necessary that we say in the prologue of this chapter the summaries of the discourse and chapters, the understanding of which is required first by the craftsman to stand upon their truth: so that you and those who are prepared, may know or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say, therefore, that when a fracture, or dislocation, or sprain, or fall happens to someone, then it is necessary that you hasten first to bleed him, and loosen him, or both together: if nothing prohibits it, such as weakness of strength; or if the one to whom something of these things happens is a child or a decrepit old man; or if it is a time of intense heat or intense cold.
Then let him be content with his food, cold vegetables, and the flesh of birds and kids; and forbid wine and rich meats, and being filled with foods and every food that fills the veins with blood. Until when you are secure from a hot abscess, and you do not fear the effusion of matter to the limb: then let the infirm return to his first regime with which his custom has run.
When therefore the broken bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary that the infirm be fed with nourishing foods, with much, thick, heavy nourishment, in which there is viscosity: such as foods made from wheat cooked with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish: and thick wine, and things similar to them. For with this regime the coagulation of the fracture happens more quickly and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in early old age, and the decrepit, it is not possible that they are joined and consolidated according to their nature always, because of the desiccation of their limbs: and those which are in them at the end of softness are consolidated and joined, like the bones of little children.
However, nature attaches to the broken bone on every side something similar to glue, in which there is thickness, by which it adheres and binds it, until they are glued together, and bound together, until it comes to the end of strength and firmness, as it was at first, until nothing prevents it from its operations: and for this reason it is necessary that you give the infirm nourishment of foods, in which there are heaviness, and viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the kinds of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of the limbs: because a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head: and a fracture of the bone of the chest is different from a fracture of the bone of the back: and similarly all the other limbs are all different from one another: and I have come with a remembrance of every kind of fracture expounded in its Chapter, distinct from another. And the kinds of bone fracture are also diversified in themselves.
Because there is an arched fracture of it, besides which splinters occur in it. And sometimes its fracture is according to the length of the bone: and its fractures are splinters, that is, splinters, and additions proceeding, as they are in a reed, when it is sharpened with a knife for writing. And not proceeding: and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head: and there is a fracture with a small fissure.
And every kind has its own method, or instrument, in its restoration, according to what will come to its remembrance, distinct in its places.
And from that by which a bone fracture is known, is its crookedness, and its prominence: and its appearance to the sense, and its sound when you compress it with your hand.
When therefore there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor sound on your touch when you touch the bone with movement, nor does the infirm find great pain: then there is no fracture there, indeed it is possible that there is crookedness, or a small fissure: therefore it is not necessary that you move it with your hand and compression completely, indeed place on it of the medicines, of which the remembrance comes, after that moment which suits the place: then let a subtle bandage be tied.
And know that when a place is bent, and split in two, besides what happens in it, truly every part is removed from its like: then it is necessary for you that you begin from your hour to its rectification, equalizing it before a hot abscess happens to it.
But if a hot abscess happens to it, then leave it for a few days, until the abscess rests. Then equalize it according to whichever way can be done with gentleness and skill.
And know that its restoration, and its equalization, is easier than a bone in which splinters occur.
Then bind it, according to what will come to your remembrance of it.
But if there are splinters in the bone, then extension of this broken member is necessary from two parts, if it is a hand or foot; or with your hand, if the member is small; or with two ropes, or with a rope and hand.
And let your position be such that you place the member on a level place, according to its natural form, until when the parts of the broken member are extended: then try to return those additions with all skill, and in whichever way you can, to their places; and beware, according to your strength, lest pain or injury befall the infirm through your operation; and try according to your strength to apply one of the two bones to its fellow, according to the best forms.
And it is necessary at that time that you try and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see anything different there, rectify it, and equalize it according to the measure of your strength.
And beware of vehement extension and strong compression, as many foolish men do. For very often they cause by that operation of theirs a hot abscess, or a contraction in the member: as we have already seen that from their operations very often.
Then, after the equalization and proper operation and the binding of that member, and rest and tranquility, give advice to the sick person about it. And make him beware lest he move it at the hour of his waking, and his sleep, in its turning and tossing, and in his evacuation, and all his movements with his utmost strength. And that he beware lest the position of the member be a position which is followed by pain. Which is, because when he feels pain or injury in the disposition of the position of the member, then it is necessary that you change it to another disposition from that with which he does not feel pain.
And be careful with it, that its position be equal, straight: lest crookedness occur in the member when it is restored.
And the quality of the binding of the broken member is as I relate.
Know that broken members are diversified in their smallness and their size and their shape.
Therefore, those of them which are small, such as the arm and finger and the shin and similar things, then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of soft, fine cloth. But those of them which are thick, such as the hip and leg, it is necessary that there be in them a broad, firm wrapping.
Because a broad bandage connects a large member and binds it on every side with an equal binding, so that no blemish enters it.
Then, after your evacuation, begin to put a suitable liniment on it evenly, on soft lint over the place of the fracture. Then begin to wrap the bandage over the place of its fracture with three or four wrappings, according to what the member deserves; and tighten your hand a little with the bandage.
Then proceed with it to the upper part from the place of the fracture, and tighten less than was your binding with which you bound the broken place. Then extend the wrapping from the place of the fracture a little, and soften the binding gradually, until you take in a large amount from the healthy place.
Then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken place with wrappings. Afterwards proceed with the wrapping to the lower part from the fracture. And let your work be in the tightening of the bandage, and the softening of it, according to what we said in the first upper wrapping.
Then place between the wrappings some soft tow, or cloth, with which the crookedness of the fracture is evened out, if there is crookedness in it; and if not, do not put anything in it.
Then wrap another bandage over it. Afterwards evenly place over these bandages properly made splints immediately, if there is not swelling in the place, nor a hot abscess.
But if there is swelling or an abscess in it, then place over it that which settles that abscess and removes the swelling; and leave it for some days. Then tighten the splints over it.
And let the splints be from halves of broad, smooth, skillfully shaped cane; or let them be from the wood from which sieves are made, which are made from pine, or gerard, that is, palm branches, or calingi; and let the size of each splint be according to this form and this same figure.
However, it is necessary that the splint which is placed over the affected area be somewhat thicker and broader than the other splints; and the length of the splint should be according to what is allowed to the limb by its size and smallness.
Then bind over the splints with another bandage, according to the same first binding. Then bind over it neatly with threads, as we said of the binding: and it is so that your binding over the place of the fracture is tighter; and the further it extends from the fracture, the looser the binding.
And it is necessary that the threads be medium in thickness and fineness, and be of soft linen.
For if the threads are thick, as I have seen from the practice of foolish men placing their threads of twisted linen cords, it is a great error: because the binding falls with them outside the proper quality.
And thin threads again are not suitable: because you will not achieve with them by binding what you wish.
And it is not necessary that there be less than a finger between splint and splint.
But if the patient is injured by the ends of the splints after binding in healthy places: then place under them some soft tow, or carded wool, until nothing is injured by it.
But when there is a fracture with a wound, and a disruption occurs in the skin, I will deal with its treatment in a separate chapter.
And know that not every broken limb needs to be bound with splints from the first day. That is because, when a limb is large, it is not necessary to place splints on it until after five days, or seven, or more, according to your assurance from the outcome of a hot abscess.
And this is the method of the plasters which the ancients used, which are placed over a fracture and dislocation.
The method of a common abbreviated plaster, restoring a fracture, suitable for many complexions, especially women and children: because it is made of ingredients that are neither overcome by heat nor cold.
And it is that you take from the dust of the mill, and it is the marrow of the flour which hangs on the walls of the mill during the movement of the millstone: and prepare it as it is, except that you sift it with egg white: and make its consistency neither thick nor thin. Then use it.
The method of another plaster, restoring fracture, and dislocation, and torsion. Take almess, and laudanum, and acacia, and enula, and mugath: and the juice of all, of each 10 drachms; of myrrh, and aloes, of each 5 drachms; and of alethil 20 drachms.  Let all be ground, and sifted, and mixed with alethil water: or with egg white, if the patient's complexion is heated. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, it restores broken bones quickly, and benefits many people.
The method of another plaster, which also benefits fracture and torsion. Take mugath, and mess, and white mallow, of each 10 drachms; of myrrh, and aloes, of each 5 drachms; of acacia 6 drachms; of Armenian bole 20 drachms. Let all be ground very finely, and sifted, and made up with water, or egg white: and let them be applied.
The method of a plaster for joints, and bones separated from their places: and it soothes the pain occurring to them, and the fissure of a bone, and fracture. Let greasy wool be taken, and submerged in vinegar, and boiled oil: and let it be placed on the place.
And in this plaster indeed there is not the virtue of restoration, but it is good in the sedation of a hot abscess: and it expels pains properly.
The method of a plaster which restores a broken bone. Take leaves of the wild fig, and leaves of wild poppy: and grind everything, and make a plaster from both.
The method of a shortened plaster, administered in the restoration of a bone fracture: and when you wish the resolution of the remainder of an abscess. Take of marshmallow root, and chamomile, and violet flowers, and herb flour, of each one part: let all be ground, and made with wine, if the member is not heated.
But if it is heated, then prepare it with moist coriander water, or with water, and use it.
The method of another stronger plaster in resolution than the first, when a hard abscess occurs in the restoration of a member. Take of marshmallow root, and linseed, and pomegranate, and melilot, and bugloss, and violet flower, and chamomile, of each one part, let all be ground, and prepared with willow water, or with sweet water, or with wine. The whole according to the heat of the member, and the quiet of its heat.
Hippocrates however did not say in his book that anything should be placed upon a broken member during its restoration except cerate made from wax and oil: nothing else: and he related that it should be a mean between thickness and thinness.
To Galen however it seemed that things should be placed upon a broken member during its restoration in which there is a drying of heat, such as myrrh, and aloes and frankincense, and similar things to them from those which we have related.
Method of a plaster which contributes to injury and pain. Take mugath, and chickpea, and cut human hair, or bird feather, and mallow, and salt, equal parts: let them be ground, and sifted, and made up: and let a plaster be made from it.
The quantity however according to which it is necessary that the ligature remain, then be loosened, is that you consider then, if no pain happens to the patient, nor itching, nor is the broken bone moved from its place: then do not loosen it for many days. But if intense itching or disturbing pain or swelling happens to him in the place: then hasten, and loosen it at once, and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, moistened: and immerse it in warm water, and wash the place with it, until the itching rests, and the pain settles.
But if intense itching or disturbing pain or swelling happens to him in the place: then hasten, and loosen it at once, and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, moistened: and immerse it in warm water, and wash the place with it, until the itching rests, and the pain settles. Then let the limb rest for an hour. Then place on it cut wool, immersed in vinegar and oil, or rose oil: and bind it on for one night, until you are secure from a hot abscess, and the swelling of the limb rests, and its pain is removed.
Then repeat it to a subtle constriction, and a small plaster: and do not bind it with your first constriction, and subtly with it, until it is healed.
But if you see that the abscess, and redness, and pain, and swelling, and all the symptoms are now completely removed, and you need a plaster and constriction, then repeat it as you did at first equally.
But if nothing of what we have said happens to the limb, then do not unbind it, except after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when all that is left is twenty days, according to what appears to you from the condition of the limb, as we have said, until when the fracture adheres, and the coagulation of flesh approaches it: add then also in the constriction more than your whole first constriction: and add also in the thickening of the weak food, according to what preceded its recollection.
But if you see the place of the fracture already drying up, and becoming thinner than it ought: then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. Therefore bathe it with warm water at every time when you unbind it, and let that be for three days in all, and lessen the constriction a little.
By this operation therefore nourishment flows to the member, and it heals quickly.
But that which foolish restorers of a broken limb do in another way, when it is not restored at first as it ought, and is restored according to crookedness, is an error of their operation, and a great danger: and if it were right, the ancients would have said it in their books, and would have operated with it.
And I have seen nowhere any trace of them at all in that, and it is better that it not be done.
Concerning the making of an accident in the head.
The kinds of fracture are diverse, and its forms many: and its causes are distinct.
For there is a fracture which is made by a blow with a sword: and the blow removes the bone entirely until it reaches the siphac, which is under the bone, just as an axe does in wood: and on account of this this kind of fracture is called Axial.
Or a sword cuts something of the bone, and cuts its surface, and the incision does not penetrate to its end: and this kind of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, Elevation absolutely.
And it is a wound of two fractures, either large or small.
And there is a fracture which is a contusion or attrition: and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall upon a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which approaches the membrane which is under the bone: or it is in the surface of the bone: and the wound of these two fractures is also either small or large.
And there is a fracture which is hidden in the bone in the subtlety of a hair: and it is a small fissure, and because of that this kind of fracture is called Capillary.
And there is a fracture which is made by a fall, or by a blow from a stone, and similar things, which cause a plate of bone to enter inwards, and a concavity is made in the place: just as happens to copper cauldrons, when a blow meets them, and a part of them enters inwards.
And this happens most often in the heads of moist bones, such as are the heads of children.
And for all these kinds of fractures separate treatments are made, and I will come with the recollection of that whole thing in its place.
And we know all these kinds of fracture by examining it, and investigating them with probes, and removing the corrupt flesh which is upon them.
The capillary kind however is known by this, that it is detected upon the bone, and is wiped, and is smeared upon it with encaustic: for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment however of a fracture, consider first the accidents of the patient: then if you see from the accidents that it signifies manifestly concerning danger, such as vomiting of bile, and extension, and loss of understanding, and loss of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and protrusion of the eyes, and redness of them, and similar things to these from the accidents in many dispositions, undoubtedly it is not cured: and if you see accidents not frightening you, and you hope for recovery: then begin its treatment.
That is, if a wounded man comes to you first when he is wounded: and it is in the winter days, then it is necessary that you strive in the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to every disposition.
And if it is in the summer days, then it is necessary that you hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before that which is under the bone from the membrane is corrupted, and those accidents which we have narrated happen.
But if the fracture of the bone has already reached the membrane touching the brain, and is with small pieces, and attraction: then it is necessary that you cut the broken and worn part, according to what I relate to you.
And it is necessary to shave the head of the wounded man: and uncover the bone in whichever way is possible for you: and according to the shape of the wound, and what is light upon the patient.
But if a flow of blood, or a hot abscess, happens to you upon the uncovering of the bone: then oppose it with that which is necessary. And it is necessary to fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil: and to make the wound rest, until the abscess settles, and you are secure from the flow of blood. Then begin the treatment of the bone, and its removal.
And that is done according to one of two methods.
One method indeed is to cut the bone with a sharp-edged incisor: and this is its form.
Then use another incisor after the incisor, slightly wider than it: and this is its form.
Then use yet another incisor, even wider than the second.
And the second method is that you have a number of different incisors: some of which are wider than others, and some shorter than others: and let them be sharp at their ends, and let them be made of good iron or steel: and use care in striking the incisor, so that it does not shake the head and injure it.
But if the bone is strong, hard: then it is necessary that you perforate its circumference before you use incisors, with drills which are called non-penetrating drills: and they are not so named except because they do not pass through the limit of the fleshy bone to that which is beyond it: because the drill has a round end above that which is beneath its sharp head, similar to a margin, and a small circle prevents it from sinking and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is fitting for you to take of these drills a large number, each of which suits the quantity of the thickness of the bone, until there is for you for every skull a drill: the quantity of whose sharp end in length and shortness is according to the quantity of the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three kinds of drills, or trepans.
The quality of perforating around the fractured bone is: that you place the drill on the bone, and revolve it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then change the drill to another place, and place what is between each hole a quantity of the thickness of a finger, or about that.
Then incise with the incisors what is between each hole of bone: and make it thinner than you can on that from the end of lightness, as we said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand, or with something else from among certain instruments which you prepared for that: such as forceps, and fine scissors.
And it is necessary that you take every precaution, lest the drill or incisor touch anything of the dura mater.
When therefore you have gone around the bone by perforating, and it has been separated from the dura mater, if it was continuous with it, and you have extracted it, it is necessary that you shave and smooth the end of that which remains in the bone with another instrument similar to an incisor. However, it is necessary that it be thinner and finer than other incisors.
But if something remains of small bones and splinters: then take them away easily, with that which is among your instruments. Then take care of the wound afterwards with linen cloths and ointments which we have mentioned.
And another method of operation is easy, altogether far from fear: which Galen mentioned, and praised it with great praise: and this is his saying. It is necessary for you first to begin uncovering the part of the bone from the place where the fracture is more severe and more obvious, until when you have uncovered that part, and placed under it the end of this lenticular incisor, of which this is the form.
And the lenticular part, being smooth, does not cut anything; and its sharp part on its two sides going lengthwise, as you see, so that the lenticular part is supported on the soft tissue, its sharp side falling on the bone.
Then strike the incisor from one side with a small hammer until you cut the whole bone easily, as it is turned; and you will be safe from the membrane, that nothing happens to it that you fear at all.
And if the physician were not the most foolish of men, and most ignorant of this, nor were asleep, then if something of the membrane remained attached to the bone in some places, he would cut it from it with the end of that lenticular incisor, and free it from it easily. For it is freed from it without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the membrane, and that which is cut away from the bone is its surface, and roughness and fine splinters remain in it: then it is necessary that you shave that roughness and remove those splinters with fine rasps of which you have prepared a number of different sizes, so that you may be able to use those most suitable and straightest of them in every place, according to that to which the operation leads you, and the broken bone, and its shape. And it is necessary that you use in your reasoning, by which you first rasp the bone, the wider of those rasps. Then use a finer one after it.
And it is necessary that you use in your reasoning, by which you first rasp the bone, the wider of those rasps. Then use a finer one after it. And do not cease to do this continuously until you arrive at using the finest and most delicate of them all.
In each of the remaining small, hair-like fissures and delicate fractures, it is necessary that you administer care according to that which leads to its rectification.
And it is a matter which is not hidden from anyone who has any study in this art, and has considered what we have written and shown in large fractures.
But if the exposed membrane remains when you remove the bone, then it is necessary that you take a linen cloth, according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth: and do this as easily as you can so that the gut is not burdened.
Then apply a wide bandage above it: and do not tighten it except to the extent that it only holds the wound: and leave it with this dressing for one day or two days, until you are sure of avoiding a hot abscess. Then unbind it, and afterwards apply medicines with which there is superfluous dryness: such as lily roots, and herb flour, and frankincense powder, and birthwort, and similar things: and it is necessary to make a powder from these medicines, which is to be sprinkled on the wound as it is dry.
And universally apply to it any medicine whose property is to cleanse and not to bite. And take care, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment is clean: nor should there be any dirt of ointment, nor filth.
And take care, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment is clean: nor should there be any dirt of ointment, nor filth. And do not allow pus to gather in it at all: because when pus gathers on the membrane of the brain, it corrupts it and putrefies it, and great harm happens to the patient afterwards: and it already happens in the siphac of the head, when the bone is cleaned from it, especially when its care is neglected, blackness on its surface.
Consider then, if the accidents which we have mentioned happen to the patient, know that it is undoubtedly harmful.
And if blackness does not happen except from the medicine which is put upon it, and it is in the virtue of that medicine to make that blackness: then it is necessary that you take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and apply a cloth with them. Then put it on the siphac; afterwards treat it with the kinds of treatment which are suitable, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that only the two upper parts of the nose are broken together, or one of them; because these are two bones: for the lower part of it is cartilaginous, it is not broken, and nothing happens to it except abrasion, and crookedness, and similar things.
If one of the two parts of it is broken, then it is necessary that you insert your little finger into the hole of the nose, and set that fracture with your index finger and thumb on the outside, until the nose returns to its natural form; and let this be done skillfully and easily. And beware that no pain comes to the patient through your operation.
But if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it is necessary that it be set with the end of a radius, on which there is a small thickness.
But if the fracture is in both parts, then do likewise: and hasten with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, with the abatement of the warm inflammation.
Then insert into the end of the nose a pledget of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part; or insert two pledgets if the fracture is in two parts; and let the pledget be of such a quantity as to fill the opening of the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers said that the pledgets should be soaked in butter, and changed every day. That, however, does not seem to me good; rather it is necessary that the pledget be soaked in egg white, mixed with powdered millstone. Then leave the pledget until the bone is set and the cartilage hardens.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the nose instead of a pledget, after a soft cloth is wrapped around it; and it happens that this holds the fracture of the nose more firmly, and that the patient is not prevented from breathing.
And this is not necessary. If you wish, do that; and if you wish, make pledgets.
But if a hot abscess should again occur in the nose while you are operating, plaster the nose with cerate, or with cotton infused in rose oil, or a little diachylon.
But if a hot abscess does not occur, then it is necessary that you plaster it with sesame flour and powdered frankincense mixed with egg white. Then place soft lint over it, and do not bind the nose with anything at all.
But if the nasal bones are fractured by a small fracture, or are comminuted, then it is necessary that you incise over them, and extract them with instruments which are suitable for them. Then sew the incision, and treat it with that which produces granulation and consolidation from suitable plasters or ointments.
But if a wound occurs between the nose, then it is necessary that you treat it with linen strips; and apply a lead cannula until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the lower jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken and its fracture is not accompanied by a wound: then see if its fracture is only external, and it is not broken in two, and is bent inwards: then its recognition is easy.
Therefore, if its fracture is on the right side, you should insert the index finger of the left hand into the mouth of the sick person: similarly, if the fracture is in the left jaw, then insert the index finger of the right hand: and push with it the hump of the fracture from the inside to the outside with your other hand from the outside of the bone gently, and with it equalize it.
But if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary to administer extension from two parts, according to straightness, until its equalization is possible.
But if shaking or disruption has already happened in the teeth, then bind those which you hope will remain with gold or silver wire, or bristle. Then place cerate over the broken jaw. Then place a doubled cloth over it, and place over the cloth a large, properly made splint; or a piece of sole leather equal in length to the jaw.
Then bind it from above, according to how its bandage is prepared and its application is suitable, until it is not destroyed; and instruct the patient to use tranquility and quiet; and give him as food light drinks.
But if you estimate that something of the shape is already altered in some way, then hasten and unbind it on the third day. Then correct what is altered of it, and plaster it with mill dust, with egg white, or with sesame flour, after you have removed the cerate; and place soft wool over the plaster.
Therefore, while that plaster remains attached to it, and the arrangement of the bone is not altered, leave it, lest you unbind it, until it is healed; or until the fracture is firm. For often this fracture is made firm in three weeks.
But if in the meantime a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have often said in its sedation, until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider. For if a piece is already separated from the bone, or from the splinter: then be skillful in this, so that you remove those splinters with what is suitable for you in their removal from among the instruments.
But if the opening of the wound is narrow, then enlarge it with a fleam according to the amount of your need.
Then when you have removed those splinters, and nothing of them remains, sew then the opening of the wound, if it is large; and if not, place upon it of the plasters which are suitable for it, and they will fill it in, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the clavicle, when it is broken.
Indeed, the clavicle is most often broken in the anterior part, on the side of the shoulder, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
For it is either broken and separated into two, besides which splinters occur in it: and this is easier for its restoration.
Or so that splinters occur in the bone: and this is more difficult for restoration.
Or so that there is a fracture with a wound.
And the operation in it, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you cause two assistants to be present, so that one of them holds the aid which follows the fractured clavicle, and the other extends the neck toward the part.
Then equalize the fracture with your fingers, until its form is made, according to what is necessary: and let there be neither prominence nor concavity in it.
But if you need more extension, then it is necessary that you place under the armpit of the patient a sphere of cloth or wool: and let its size be according to the quantity of your need: and extend and elevate the clavicle, and compress the sphere with your hand, until you equalize the fracture, according to what is necessary.
But if you cannot draw the end of the clavicle outwards because it has reached the depth, then it is necessary that the patient be laid supine on his neck: and you place under his shoulder a cushion moderate in size, and let an assistant press his shoulder downwards, until the bone of the clavicle which is deep is raised upwards: and then rectify the fracture, and equalize it with your fingers.
But if you feel a spicule from the clavicle, and it moves, then it is necessary that you incise over it, and extract that spicule with ease.
But if it is retained in the bone, then make an incision upon it with one of the scalpels which you have prepared for it, after you have placed under the clavicle an instrument which preserves the pleura; and this instrument is of wood or of iron. And this is its form.
It is similar to a spoon which has no concavity.
And let its width be according to that which you need of it from the size of the bone, and its smallness. Its length however, also according to what is possible for you in the operation.
And let it have two ends, as you see: and one end be wide, and the other narrow.
But if the disruption of the wound which you cut when you extracted the splintered bone is wide, and you are secure from a hot abscess: then bring together the two lips of the wound with suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear a hot abscess: then fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's fissure.
But if a hot abscess should occur, then dip a cloth in rose oil, and vinegar, and wine, and place it on top.
However, a constriction of the bone when it is without a wound and without a cut, is that you place on the bone a plaster made from mill flour with egg white. And place a soft pledget over it. Then place a sphere under its tickle, if you need it. Then take a himematem, that is, a wheel which the Saracens hold, which is very long: and let its width be one palm, or thereabouts. Then place a doubled pad over the pledget and plaster. Then take a small plate of thin wood, whose width is three fingers, and in length similarly. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the plate in the place of the alhimemati which falls upon the fracture and place. Then place the alhimemati over the fracture as it is rolled up, and wrap it over its neck, and under its healthy little finger, and under its infirm little finger: and bring it over every part, and as you see that the bandage holds the fracture with a decent bandage: and it is that, which is not hidden from you, and all, so that the plate is not removed from above the broken bone. And for this reason it is necessary that you look at the patient every day. Whenever therefore the bandage softens, and you see the plate being removed: then straighten it, and tighten the bandage.
Then place the patient's bed on his back, and place a small cushion under his little finger at his bed at night, so that with it his arm may be raised from his side. Therefore, the fracture of the clavicle is elevated by the elevation of the shoulder.
Or bind his arm to his neck; and do not untie the bandage, unless an accident happens in the place from itching or from an abscess, up to twelve days.
Then renew the plaster, if you see his face [i.e., the appearance of the wound] turning to it. And add to the constriction, and leave it until it is restored and coagulated.
And indeed, the fracture of the clavicle is tightened and strengthened mostly within twenty-eight days; and sometimes it happens in some men in less time than that.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the scapula.
The scapula is rarely fractured, and in its broad part; and only its margins are fractured from it.
When therefore a place is fractured from it, and it is fractured in its middle, and this is not known except by touch: then, according to what the figure of the fracture is, attempt its preparation. And reduce it to its natural form in every way ingenuity allows you.
Then place over the place mill dust with egg white, and soft lint, and place over it a small pillow of doubled cloth. Then place over it a broad splint of thin board according to the size of the whole scapula, or a little larger.
But if there is a concavity under the splint in any place of the scapula, then equalize that concavity with soft lint until the splint descends according to equality.
Then bind over it with a long bandage, with decent constriction and firmness of the splint, until it is not removed from its place.
And examine the bandage every day. Whenever, therefore, the bandage loosens, tighten it, and adjust the splint if it is removed from its place; and let the patient lie on his sound side.
And indeed the fracture is healed in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When therefore that number is completed, then unbind the bandage: and you shall be secure. For it is of bones whose union is not feared, nor their separation.
But if a fragment of bone protrudes and pierces under the skin: then incise over it, and remove it. And do as we said in the fracture of the clavicle from the settling of a hot abscess, if anything should result from it.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the breastbone.
The breastbone is sometimes fractured in its middle, and this rarely happens. Its extremities, however, are very often fractured and crushed by accidents.
When its middle, which inclines downwards, is fractured, and intense pain, difficulty in breathing, and coughing occur; and perhaps he vomits blood, and a hollow in the fractured bone is apparent and not hidden from the sense, its restoration is that the patient be laid on his back; and place a cushion between his shoulder blades.
Then press his shoulders, and bring together the ribs on both sides or flanks with your hands; and carefully adjust it evenly, in every way that is possible and appropriate, until the shape of the bone is restored as it should be.
Then place a plaster and lint over it, and place on top a splint of thin willow or linden wood, or similar light woods, after you have wrapped it in cloth. Then carefully bind it over the fractured bone so that it does not move; and carry the bandage around the back several times, and tighten it with a suitable constriction.
Then examine the bandage every hour, and whenever it loosens, tighten it; and if necessity requires its loosening because of itching or ulceration occurring at the site, or pain, or an abscess, then hasten and loosen it, and remove the plaster, and correct what has happened according to the method of its treatment.
Then replace the plaster, if you see it is necessary, and apply a bandage until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur except in the thick parts which follow the back, and at their extremities on the anterior part. And attrition does not happen to them except because they are cartilaginous. And its recognition is not hidden from the sense upon investigation with the fingers.
And its restoration is done in such a way that you equalize the fracture with the fingers as much as possible, until the shape is equalized, as is necessary. Then apply plasters, and bind the broken bone with a splint, if it needs it.
And if the fracture of the ribs is sloping inwards: then there happens to the sick person intense pain, and a puncture, like the puncture that happens to someone who has pleurisy: because the bone pierces the membrane, and also there happens to him shortness of breath, and cough, and vomiting of much blood: and this is difficult to cure.
And now the ancients have devised many things for it: and among them are those who say that you should give the sick person foods which produce inflammation and flatulence, so that the belly is inflamed, and distended, and the fracture is pushed outwards.
We however avoid causing a rapid hot abscess, if it does not happen. If however it has already happened, then add to it.
And some of them said: let a cupping glass be placed over the place, then let it be sucked with force, and it is more similar to reasoning. However, it is feared that the cupping glass will draw superfluities to the place, because of the disposition of weakness.
And some of them said: it is necessary that the place be covered with wool which has already been submerged in hot oil: and let cushions be placed between the ribs, until it is filled: so that there is an equal binding when you wrap it according to the roundness.
Then let the sick person be treated with a cure for pleurisy from food and medicine.
But if a severe thing constricts the sick person, which he does not tolerate, and the bone pierces the covering with a wounding puncture, and we fear for the sick person: then it is necessary that we incise over the place, and uncover the broken rib. Then let us place over it the instrument which preserves the sifac, the remembrance of which preceded: and let us incise the bone easily, and let us incise it.
Then let us bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with a suture: and let us treat it with plasters, until it is healed.
But if a hot abscess should occur in the meantime, then hasten, and dip small pads in rose oil, and place them over the place: and let the sick person be treated with what is suitable for the abscess.
Meanwhile also let him be laid on his side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
A fracture sometimes occurs to the bones of the neck, and attrition rarely happens to them; and similarly also to the vertebrae of the back. Therefore when this happens to someone, and you wish to know whether he will be healed or not, then consider. For if you see his hands already softened, and numb, and dead; and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor contract them; and when you contract them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, and does not feel pain in them: then know that he will not be healed, and in most instances it is fatal.
But if he moves them, and feels the contraction with the fingers and the pricking: then know that the bone of the neck is safe, and that the patient will be healed with treatment.
But if something similar to that happens to the vertebrae of the back, and you wish to know whether he will be healed or not: then consider his feet: then if you see that they are softened, and that happens to them which happened to the hands: then when he lies on his back, flatulence and excretion exit involuntarily; and when he is placed on his stomach, excretion exits involuntarily; and when he is placed on his back, and wishes to urinate, and cannot: then know that it is fatal. Therefore do not labor in his cure.
If, however, nothing of that happens to him, the matter is less serious.
And the treatment of what arises from this is that you strive for the sedation of the hot abscess: so that you place on the affected vertebra rose oil alone, or with the yolks of roasted eggs.
Place that on it several times a day, until the hot abscess is sedated: then place on the location one of the strengthening, drying plasters, and bind it on tightly with a bandage: and instruct the patient to use rest and tranquility. Nor should he sleep on the side where he finds pain, until it is healed.
But if it happens at the abrasion of the bone, saturated, or something which is already separated from it: then it is necessary that you cut through the skin, and remove it. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with a suture. Then treat it with some incarnative, from among the ointments, until it is healed.
But if the last bone of the alhausos is broken, and it is the end of the tail: then it is necessary that you insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus, and set the broken bone with the other hand, as far as possible, and with a second setting.
Then place it on it, and remove it: and treat the wound with the treatment which preceded, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the hip.
Chapter XI.
Rarely are the bones of the hips broken. But if they are broken, then their fracture is nothing but a comminution at their extremities: and they are split lengthwise, and they are deflected inwards: and there happens to the sick person pain in the place, and a puncture: and the leg which is broken becomes numb.
And its restoration indeed is that you extend your hand over it, until you stand over the fracture in the way that is its form.
But if the fracture is only at its extremities, then equalize that fracture, according to what is prepared for you from equalization, until it is assimilated to its natural form.
But if the fracture is lengthwise, or it already deviates inwards: then make the sick person lie on his belly, until the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. When therefore you have equalized it, place a plaster over it; then place over it a splint of wood, or of leather; and bind it tightly, over which you do not fear a change of the fracture, nor removal of the splint.
And equalize the concavity of those with that which fills it, until the binding receives it according to equality; and instruct the sick person to sleep on his back, or on his sound side.
But if a hot abscess happens to him, then cease from its extension and its restoration, until the hot abscess subsides; and put on it that which subdues it, according to what preceded, then return to its restoration; and bind it as is necessary.
But if it happens in a projecting bone, or is comminuted, extract it.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the ulna.
The ulna is that which is between the elbow and the head of the scapula.
If therefore it is broken, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: that you take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this form which follows.
And two bandages are tied at the two ends.
Then let it be hung from a high place, and let the patient sit on a seat. Then let him throw his broken arm onto the wood, until it reaches its armpit, in the middle of which the curve of the wood is attached.
Then hang something heavy above it, so that the assistant stretches it downwards. Then let the physician set the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture is restored as it should be.
And another method is that the patient lies on his back: and let his hand be suspended from his neck with a ligature. Then instruct two assistants, that one of them hold what is above the fracture with his hands: and that the other hold what is below it, and each of them extend to his part.
And if you wish the extension to be stronger, then bind below the fracture with a ligature, and above it with another ligature: and let each of the two assistants extend to his own two parts.
If therefore the fracture is near the end of the humerus, then it is necessary that one ligature be placed under the armpit, and another under the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow: then it is necessary that a ligature be placed above that place, and above the elbow itself.
Then set the fracture easily without effort, until when the fracture is set as it should be, and a good application is applied: then bind it, if a hot abscess does not occur.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then leave off the binding until the seventh day. And place on it cut wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil, until when the abscess is quiet: then bind it: and the method of binding it is to place a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a bandage of new cloth over the plaster. Then let him place the arm on a support, and place his open hand on his shoulder.
And place a cloth and bind the arm to the support so that the arm rests in a position like a prop: if nothing prevents you from this, nor is anything altered in you from the broken bone.
But if you fear that something will be altered in you from the broken bone, then use splints: and it is that you place over the fracture itself a splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints. And place between each splint the width of a finger; and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on each side.
Then bind over the splints the bandage which I described at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your bandage should be tighter over the place of the fracture. And the more the fracture is lengthened, the less tight should be the bandage.
If therefore you see the placement of the splints and the bandage as we have said, at the hour in which you restore the limb immediately, then do so; and if you fear a hot abscess, then leave off the bandage and splints until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then examine the bandage on all three days, lest itching or swelling occur in the place, or food be prevented from reaching the limb, because of the tightness of the bandage. Therefore rectify all that, according to what we have said.
But if you are confident that nothing will happen from it, then do not loosen the bandage except after three days, or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on his back, and his hands on his stomach; and place under the arm a cushion filled equally with wool.
And observe at every hour of the night or day, lest the shape of the broken limb be loosened, or the bandage softened. Therefore rectify all that with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order which we have previously stated, so that the food is light at first, until the mouth begins to tighten; then it is necessary that you increase his food.
For it is the custom of the arm and leg that it is bound for forty days; then therefore it is necessary that it be unbound, and a bath be administered, and it be treated with ointments which restore it.
But if the fracture is foul, and crushed, then do not unbind the bandage and splints until fifty days, and even up to two months.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the two forearms.
Of these, one is small; and it is that which follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is that which is placed under the small one from the lower part.
Perhaps therefore only the larger forearm is broken, or the smaller; and perhaps they are broken together.
When therefore the small upper forearm is broken, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower forearm is broken, its fracture is bad, and its healing difficult.
And it is worse when both bones are broken together.
If therefore the bone that is broken is the small upper forearm, then it is suitable for the physician, in its restoration, to place a small extension easily, until he sets it.
But if it is the large forearm that is broken: then it is necessary that he place a much stronger constriction.
And it is necessary that he place the shape of the hand in its restoration, and its extension stretched out upon a cushion. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers; and let the lowest joint be lower than all the fingers, or the rest; and let the patient sit squarely upon himself, and let the cushion be before him, or directly in front of him, at a height, so that the patient does not incur labor.
Then let the assistant extend the arm from the lower part, either with his hand or with a bandage. And let another assistant extend from above similarly.
Then let the physician set the bone until he restores it to the best possible shape.
But if in a fracture of the bone there are splinters, then try to restore every fragment to its place by your skill.
If however a separated splinter appears in it, and pierces the skin, and there is no hope for its restoration: then incise over it, and remove it according to the method which we described in what precedes.
But if the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a separate chapter for that, therefore take its treatment from there.
But if a hot abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then smear a cloth with cerate made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be of medium thickness, neither too thick nor too thin; and bind the cloth over it with a thin bandage, until the abscess subsides: then remove the cerate, and apply a plaster prepared from the powder of the mill with egg white. Then apply splints, and let the splint which is placed over the fracture itself be somewhat wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most cases, whether the fracture is in one bone or in two bones together.
Then apply your bandage over the place of the fracture, stronger and tighter, and somewhat more tightly to the upper or lower parts of the fracture; or apply the bandage a little looser, according to what has been previously mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths which are wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, and not hard or rough. And let the thread with which it is bound be of linen, properly of medium thickness between fine and coarse, as we have related.
And examine the limb and the bandage after a few days. But if something happens which needs correction, such as itching which affects the limb, then it is necessary that you bathe the limb with warm water until the itching ceases, and leave the limb unbound until it rests, then reapply the bandage.
But if the bandage is now softened, and the bone is now set, and similar things to these, then correct all that with your skill, and examine again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb because of the excessive tightness of the bandage, then it is necessary that you soften it a little, and leave it for a few days until nourishment flows to it, then bind it.
But if nothing of what we have said happens to the patient, then it is not necessary to unbind it, except after twenty days, or about that.
Then the patient's hand should be hung from his neck. And let his neck be straight, and let it be guarded by its own strength from agitated movements. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. All that is according to the dispositions of complexions, and dispositions of strength.
Concerning the restoration of fractures of the hand.
Know that fracture rarely happens to the knuckles of the hand and the joints of the fingers. And it does not happen to it except by much rubbing.
Therefore when a fracture or rubbing happens to the palm, then it is necessary that the sick person sit squarely; and opposite him let there be a seat of equal height. Then place his hand upon it extended.
Then let the assistant extend the broken bones, and let the physician set them until when they are joined with a good joining.
Then it is necessary that he apply a plaster and lint, if a hot abscess does not occur. Then place a splint on top according to the size of the place; and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
But if the fracture is towards the lower parts around the sole of the foot, then make something similar to a sphere from cloth: and instruct the patient to bind it over it with his broken sole: then let it be bound with a long cloth.
And let the splint be of leather, in which there is softness, so that the leather may be applied with the body of the sole: and bind it, as is necessary.
But if the fracture is on the outside, then it is necessary: that you place one splint on top, and another splint underneath on the sole: and let the hand be open and straight. Then apply a bandage, as the hand is rolled: and extend between the fingers with a ligature.
But if a fracture happens to any of the joints of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, let it be set as is necessary. Then let it be bound with a sole: and if you wish, place a small, straight splint on it: so that the fracture may be raised, and not moved.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle finger, and index finger, or little finger, and ring finger, then let it be set, and bound with the healthy finger which follows it: or let them all be bound together continuously: and this is better. Or place a small splint on it, as we said in the case of the thumb.
