And consider in your restoration, and after the disposition of a warm abscess: and counteract it with what is necessary, when something happens from it, according to what its account reiterates.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the hip.
The hip bone is often fractured, and this is evident to the senses: because it is turned towards the inner and posterior part.
And its restoration is done in such a way that a ligature is tightened above the fracture, and another ligature below the fracture. And the patient is laid prostrate on his face.
Then let the attendant extend each ligature to its part equally; and this, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if the fracture is near the root of the hip, then let the ligature be at the part of the pecten, so that the extension falls upwards; and another ligature below the fracture.
And so similarly if the fracture is near the knee, then let the ligature be near the knee, so that the extension is downwards.
Then let the physician equalize the bone with both his hands, until he reduces it according to the likeness of its natural form; and let the bone be joined with a good joining.
Then it is necessary that you apply a plaster and a bandage, if an abscess does not occur in the limb. But if an abscess occurs in it, then leave it for several days, until the hot abscess is seated, then return to your treatment.
Moreover, its bandage should be like this. It is necessary that you wrap over the fracture a hard bandage, that is, a splint; and double it twice or three times; and let there remain a superfluity from it. Then wrap the leg until the heel reaches the root of the buttocks.
And insert a long thread between the hip and the leg near the knee to the lower parts, and let the ends of the thread come from the upper part of the two parts. Then roll over the leg and hip what is superfluous from the bandage.
Then place over the hip at the place of the fracture itself splints, and place of them one splint over the bone of the leg.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and hip with soft cloths, so that the constriction is even.
Then begin with a strong bandage from the middle over the place of the fracture with three or four turns. And the more you extend with the bandage from the place of fracture, let your bandage be less and softer, and gentler.
Then attend to the two ends of the thread, which you inserted between the hip and leg. Therefore, tie with both three small stars which are above. Then go with the two ends of the thread to the lower parts, until you arrive at the heel of the foot. And with both tighten also the ends of the stars from the other side, so that the bandage is not removed from its place.
Then loosen the binding over it, while it remains, so that neither corrosion, nor abscess, nor swelling, nor things similar to them happen to the limb. If anything of that should happen. Therefore hasten with its loosening, and correct everything that happened from it, according to what we have said many times.
But if there is a piece of bone in the bone which pricks: then it is necessary to smooth it, if it is possible for you. And if not, cut over it and remove it: and treat it with that which was previously mentioned, until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the hip is constricted, besides which splints are added to it, as we said in the case of the arm and shoulder.
However, in the restoration of it thus fractured, lameness does not happen to the patient. And if it is restored alone, except that another leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that he whose it is, always limps.
And know that the hip is constricted in fifty days, or a little is added, or a little is subtracted: all that is according to the diversity of complexions, and the other dispositions.
Concerning the restoration of the fracture of the kneecap.
Know that a fracture of the kneecap rarely happens, but attrition happens to it very often.
If, however, a fracture happens to it, then it is only either a fissure, or a hole in its parts. And this is with a wound, and without a wound. And the whole thing is treated with sense.
And its restoration is thus, that what is disrupted of its parts is made even with the fingers, until it is brought together, and joined as much as is possible by evenness and ease, and making.
Then apply a plaster of sesame flour, or mill flour, with egg white: and place a round piece of linen on top of it, if you need it: and bind tightly over it with a suitable bandage.
Then consider all the measures we have described in the other fractures: such as a hot abscess, and similar things: so that you oppose every eventuality with what is suitable to it, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the leg.
Know that the leg has two bones, of which one is thick and is called by the name of the leg; and the other is thin, and is called the fibula.
And to both of these occurs the same kinds of fracture that occur to the two bones of the arm: and because of that, its restoration is done just like the restoration of the arm equally, and the operation is one and the same.
If both bones are broken, the leg is displaced in every direction.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is displaced to the front.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find it lower down; and it is among those things which are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, and setting, and bandaging of splints according to that equally.
However, if the leg fracture is a bad fracture, having many splinters, the extension should be less, and gentler in your alleviation.
And in the leg indeed there is something in the operation above the arm: and it is that when you set the splints, and have completed all your operation, then take two splints of pine wood, which is used in the making of sunshades which are placed under the boards: and they are made from palm spatulas: and choose from those which are of small thickness, and not from the small ones: and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee downwards.
Then wrap over each one a cloth with two wrappings according to its length. And place one part of the leg on one side, and the other on the other side: and let them be from the knee to the lower part of the foot.
Then bind the two splints in three places, at the two ends and the middle. For when constriction is prevented, the leg inclines neither to the right nor the left, and stands in a good position.
And now indeed let a cradle be prepared of wood according to the length of the leg, and let the leg be placed in it, so that it may be kept from movement.
And it is especially necessary that you do this in a fracture when there is a proper wound with it.
Then examine the leg every day, and treat it diligently against any abscess, swelling, or other complications. If then anything of these things happens, then apply to it what is necessary until it is healed, if God wills.
Concerning the fracture of the bones of the foot and toes.
Indeed, a complete fracture does not happen to the heel bone.
However, fracture sometimes happens to the bones of the foot, and also a little to the toes: and attrition happens to them in most instances, not fracture.
If a fracture happens to the bones of the foot, and you see those bones raised up on top of one another, then let the patient put his foot on the prepared ground, as if walking.
Then stand you, and place your foot on that which is raised up from those bones; afterwards press, or tread on it: and make it even, until it returns to its place.
Then apply a plaster, and lint on top; and place under the sole of the foot a small board, which has two flat ends.
Then bind it with the foot with a suitable bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloths, and other things which it needs.
When therefore three or four days pass, then loosen the bandage. For you will find the bones set, whether they are broken, or dislocated. Therefore the operation in them is as you see.
But if some fingers are broken, then restore them, and set them according to what I have told you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place a small splint along the length of the finger, and let it be slightly wider than it; then place this splint, which I have described to you, under the foot, and bind it with a suitable bandage.
But if what is broken of the bones is two, or three, or more, then place over each finger a splint of its size, wrapped in linen cloth; and bind the splint to the lower part of the foot; and let it stand on one end of the splint outside the belly of the foot, so that it holds with a good retention.
And it is fitting for you, that you not forget, but consider all that I have told you in the other fractures from the accidents which we have mentioned: and apply to each accident what is appropriate.
Concerning the fracture of a woman's vulva, and of the pubic bone, and of a man's penis.
When a woman's vulva is broken, then make her sit squarely. Then incline her slightly towards her back, and let her be held back.
Then let the midwife fill her vulva with lint until she fills it; and let a sphere be made of it in the vulva.
Then let the woman be shaken, and raise her back gradually. For that lint comes out until it becomes like a sphere before the opening of the vulva, and the broken bone returns.
Then place a cushion on her back. Therefore, when she wants to urinate, let her remove the lint easily, then let her urinate; and let her replace it in the same way in which you first inserted it; then let her return to her first cushion.
Let her do this for seven days, or thereabouts, for the bone itself is restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder, and tie a reed tube over its opening, and insert the whole bladder into her vulva. Then blow into the tube forcefully until the bladder inflates inside the vulva, for the fracture will return.
Then let it be filled with lint, and let it remain for some days, as we have described, until it is healed.
And when the pubic bone of a man or woman is broken, then administer for its restoration and alignment that which we have described for the hip bone.
And the proper method is not hidden in these nearby fractures, which occur frequently, especially for one who has some skill or experience.
And understand this book of mine well, because the fracture itself shows you in many situations the right way for its restoration and its straightening: therefore understand.
But when the penis is broken, take a goose's gullet and insert the penis into it. Then place a bandage of cloth over it, and it should be bandaged and left for three days, or thereabouts, and it will heal, if God wills.
Concerning the restoration of a bone fracture when it is accompanied by a wound.
It is fitting for one who has suffered a fracture with a wound, especially if it is a large one, such as a fracture of the hip bone or the collarbone, or similar, that you act quickly and immediately bleed him: if the conditions for bloodletting are present, as we have stated before.
But if the wound is bleeding, then you must hasten to clean it, so that you may sprinkle ground verdigris on it, if you have nothing else at hand.
Then begin to set the fracture on that same day; and do not postpone it, unless an abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then delay setting it until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides. And do not approach it on the third and fourth day, for you would cause bad things to happen to it.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin: then you must try to reduce it, and level it with your hands gently and with slight extension.
But if its extension and leveling with your hands are not prepared, then reduce it with this instrument. And it is an instrument made of iron, its length is seven or eight finger-widths, and its width is according to the size of the wound. And because of that it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of them, according to the quantity which he needs for it in the treatment of every kind of fracture.
And let it be round: and let there be a small thickness in it, so that it is not doubled over during compression upon it at the time of operation: and let it have a sharp end, which has a margin at its ends. And let its upper part be thicker than its lower part.
And this is its form.
It is necessary in this operation that its sharp curved end be placed upon the end of the prominent bone: and push it with it once, until when the bone returns and is made even by a certain evenness.
Therefore, try to even the ends of the fracture together.
But if the ends of it are broken finely, and the instrument does not grasp it well, then it is necessary that you cut the end of that bone, until the instrument can hold it.
But if you cannot reduce them completely with what we have described, then cut it with whatever is suitable from the incisors which we have mentioned: or saw it with one of the saws in whichever way is possible for you.
Then scrape away what remains on the bone of roughness and small splinters.
Therefore, when the bone is reduced, and the patient finds afterwards intense, painful injury, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Therefore do this. For you provide great help to the patient when your restoration of the bone is completed.
Then submerge the cloth in astringent, black wine. And especially if it is summer. And do not place cerate over the wound, nor anything in which there is oil, lest it cause putrefaction and corruption in it.
Then administer splints at the completion of your bone restoration. And leave the wound uncovered, so that you cut a hole in the bandages with scissors according to the size of the wound.
And take every precaution lest you constrict the wound with the fracture. For often foolish physicians have done this, and have caused death, or corrosion, or zuchem, that is, pus, to befall their patients. And let your binding be soft, gentle, different from the binding of other fractures.
But if the wound is bad, or is a large wound, and you fear some of the bad consequences which we have recounted, and you find pain suspended in the place: then it is not necessary that you apply splints. And make for them wrappings of hard cloths in place of splints, and bind it with them.
But when it is after one day, or two days, and you see the wound beginning to generate pus: then remove from above it the cloth which you placed upon it with wine. Then administer lint and ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds: such as tetrapharmacon ointment, and similar things.
And it is fitting for you to loosen the bandage. And examine the wound every day, morning and evening, until it is consolidated and healed.
And it is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that pus flows from it downwards with ease.
But if many days pass for the wound, and it does not consolidate, nor is pus discharged from it: then know that there are small splinters of bone there. Therefore it is necessary that you explore the wound with a probe. However many of those splinters are separated, remove them and extract them. And those which are not separated, and prick the limb, and cause pain to happen, try to cut them and remove them in every way that it is possible for you.
But if a boil, or ekileten, that is, estiomenus, or another kind of corruption and putrefaction happens to the wound: then it is necessary that you apply to each of them that which is suitable for it from the treatment, the account of which preceded in its chapter.
And that in which my speech ought to stand, and to which you should give your present attention when a bone is broken and protrudes above a limb, such as the hip bone, and the bone of the forearm, and similar large members, is that you should not attempt its extraction, for death often results from this; rather, leave it until it putrefies.
For perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then therefore treat the wound, if it seems good to you; and if not, leave it.
Concerning the treatment of a node in the track of a fracture.
This node often occurs in the track of the healing of a fracture. Especially that which is near the joints; and its form is disfigured by it. And perhaps it prevents the limb from its natural function.
Therefore consider then, if the node is recent: administer to it medicines which have astringency, such as aloe, and olibanum, and myrrh, and sarcocolla, and acacia, and similar things.
So that you take some of these, or all of them: and mix them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar: and put them on the node in a pledget, and bind them tightly upon it with a good bandage.
And leave the bandage, and do not loosen it for many days. Then loosen it, and apply another, until the node is removed.
Or bind a lead plate neatly upon it. For lead has the property of removing whatever is feared from the limbs.
But if the node has already turned to stone, and is constricted, and urgent necessity prompts its removal: then incise it from its upper part, and cut away the projecting excess; or shave it with some razors until it is removed: and treat it until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment when it is restored, and the limb remains afterwards thin, not according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is restored, and the limb afterwards remains thin, weak: then this does not happen except for many reasons. Of which one is, the multitude of the loosening of the ligament and its binding not as it ought: or because of the excess of constriction of the ligaments, until nourishment is prevented from flowing to the limb: or because of the multitude of superfluous embrocation at the wrong time: or because of the paucity of blood in the body of the infirm, and its weakness.
And its cure is, to nourish the infirm, and to make his body strong, until the blood is multiplied in him. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and gladness upon him.
Then place pitch upon the limb: so that the pitch may draw to it much nourishment. And diligently embrocate it with warm water, until nourishment flows, and it returns to its natural form.
Concerning the treatment of broken bones when they are set crookedly.
When crookedness and a prominence of the broken bone, or a knot, occurs in a limb already set after its healing, and the form of the limb is marred thereby, yet the limb is not hindered from its natural function: then it is not necessary that you heed the opinion of him who thinks that the limb should be broken again.
And there have been many foolish physicians and bone-setters who have done this in our land.
And this operation is indeed very blameworthy, leading to death, or great fear, so that it is certainly deadly.
But if the lameness and knotting are recent: then it is necessary that it be bathed with warm water, in which softening herbs have been decocted, such as mallow leaves and its root, and melilot, and similar things. And let a plaster be made with a softening plaster, such as properly made diachylon.
Or let the mucilage of mallow root be taken, and shaken with hen's fat, or sesame oil: and let it be plastered with it.
Or let fat figs be taken, and rubbed with pigeon dung, and similar things which are named among medicines that diminish consolidation.
And sometimes the knotting is resolved with frequent, gentle, and prolonged rubbing, which is done with moist hands. And let movement of the limb be administered to every part at all hours.
But if tortuosity has preceded, and has become strong, and has been converted into stone, and the necessity has been provoked to cure it with iron: then it is necessary that its upper part be cut, and the continuity of the bone be loosened, and that which superabounds from the knotting or bone be incised with fine scalpels: and let ease be administered in it with diligence and care.
Concerning the speech on dislocation.
Dislocation is the exit of any of the joints from its place. Therefore it is prevented from its motion, and the shape of the member is deformed. And it causes intense pains and injuries to befall the infirm.
Therefore, when a dislocation happens to someone, then it is necessary to hasten to its restoration; nor should one delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the place becomes inflamed, and perhaps with it the reduction of the dislocation.
Therefore, on account of that, it is not fitting that it be moved, nor extended at the time of its inflammation. Because it very often causes spasms and injuring pains to happen.
But when that happens, then it is necessary that you hasten to the bloodletting of the patient. Then let it be left alone until the inflammation is quiet.
Then let the limb be moistened with warm water and oil. Then let it be reduced easily.
And let every limb be treated with that whose recollection will come in its place.
I indeed have already arranged the chapters on dislocation, even as preceded in fracture, from the upper part of the body to the lower part of it.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two jaws are rarely dislocated except rarely.
And the dislocation of both happens according to one of two methods. For they are either removed from their places by a small removal, and are somewhat softened. Or they are dislocated by a complete dislocation, until they are softened to the breast, until the patient's saliva flows, and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is involved with speech.
But when its dislocation is small, then it returns in most instances by itself with ease.
If, however, the dislocation is complete: then it is necessary that its reduction be administered quickly, and not be delayed at all: and it is so that an assistant holds the head of the patient; and the physician inserts the thumb of one of his hands into the root of the jaw of the dislocation between its bone, if the dislocation is on one side. Or he inserts his two thumbs together, if the dislocation is on two sides. And the remaining fingers of his hands should be outside, with which he pulls.
And instruct the patient to clean his jaws, and free them for eating on every side. And the physician pulls the jaw, and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
But if its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are involved at the same time: then administer a vaporization with warm water and oil, until the reduction of both becomes easy; and do not delay the reduction of both completely, as we said.
Therefore, when they return and are set, and the mouth of the sick man is closed, and they are not softened: then place on them small cushions of cloths, with cerate which is made from wax and rose oil.
Then it is bound easily with soft bandages.
And let the sleep of the sick man be on his back; and let his head be supported between two pillows, so that he does not move it to the right and left; nor let him try, or be forced, to chew anything; indeed, let his food be a gentle sucking, until when the pains are removed, and the jaw is bound; then let him eat what he wishes, and let it be administered easily.
Let him not labor over the operation of his mouth in eating and drinking, and yawning, until the jaw is bound and healed.
But if the reduction of the two jaws becomes difficult, when they are dislocated at some time, and are not returned to their places: then fevers and constant thirst often result from this; and perhaps the belly of the sick man is loosened, and perhaps he vomits pure bile.
Therefore when you see this, then know that it is destruction, and very often he to whom this happens dies within ten days.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle, and the extremity of the shoulder.
Indeed, the clavicle is not dislocated from the inner side, because of its connection with the chest. And it is sometimes dislocated outwards; and this is evident to the senses.
And its restoration is that the patient lies on his back, and extends his arms. Then press the place with your palm with a strong pressure, for it will return.
Then place a plaster and compresses on it, and bind it.
However, the extremities which follow the elbow, and are continuous with it, are not dislocated, except rarely. But if it is dislocated on some day: then it is necessary that it be reduced and made even according to what we have said, and what is prepared for you.
Then place a plaster, compresses, and a bandage on it. And instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And with this same treatment the extremity of the humerus is reduced when it is also separated from its place.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the humerus.
Know that the humerus is not dislocated except in three ways.
Of which one is that it is dislocated towards the armpit to the lower parts. The second is that it is dislocated towards the breast. And perhaps it is dislocated to the upper part of the humerus: and that happens rarely.
And it is not dislocated posteriorly, because of the scapula. And it is not dislocated anteriorly, because of the nerves.
And it is mostly dislocated and exits towards the lower parts towards the armpit: especially in those whose flesh is scant. Because it exits quickly in them, and enters quickly.
But in those whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite of that, namely because it exits with difficulty, and enters with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some men through a blow, or a fall, and the humerus becomes inflamed with a hot abscess, and it is thought because of that, that it is already dislocated.
Therefore, it is necessary that you examine it, until you stand upon its certainty, and then you proceed in its cure.
And a dislocation is known when it is towards the lower parts near the armpit: and distinguish between a dislocated humerus and a healthy humerus. For you find between them a manifest difference, and you find in the head of the humerus a concavity, and under the armpit on palpation the head of the humerus as if it were an egg: and the infirm cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it in all movements.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest, or upwards: then you find a manifest touch, nor is it hidden.
And indeed, the reduction of this dislocation is easy when it is recent, or the infirm is a child.
And its reduction is that the attendant raises his hand upwards. Then place your thumbs under his armpit, and raise the joint forcefully upwards to its place: and let the attendant raise his hand, and extend it upwards. Then let him place it lower, for it returns quickly itself.
But if it does not return by what we have said, and many days have passed since the dislocation occurred: then it is necessary that the infirm person be bathed in warm water, and that a softening and soothing embrocation be administered, such as if the roots of mallow, fenugreek, and melilot are decocted in water, and administered.
Then let the infirm person be laid on his back: and let a ball of wool be placed under his armpit, which is equal between softness and firmness.
Then let the physician place his heel on the ball, and raise the head of the humerus forcefully, and pull the patient's hand downwards.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this method: and it is that you cause a man to be present who is taller than the infirm person, and let him stand at the side, and insert his shoulder under the armpit of the infirm person: and let him raise his armpit upwards, until the infirm person is suspended in the air. And let another assistant pull the hand of the infirm person towards the lower part of his belly. But if the infirm person is light, then it is necessary that something else be suspended with him, to weigh him down; for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also reduced according to another method: and it is that a long piece of wood be fixed in the ground, the head of which is round in shape, like the handle of a mortar: which is neither thick nor thin. Then let it be placed under the infirm person after soft cloths are placed on top of the wood; and let the infirm person stand along the length of the wood.
Then let his hand be extended downwards from the other side, and let his body be extended from the other side with force; for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But if its reduction is difficult by all that we have said, then use this treatment: and it is that you take a piece of wood whose length is the size of two arms, and its width the size of four fingers, and its thickness the size of two fingers: which has a round head, so that its entry into the depth of the rectum is eased, according to this form.
Then soft cloths are tied around the round head, so that the wood does not injure the infirm person.
Then let it be placed under the rectum of the infirm person, so that the hand or arm is extended over the wood downwards; and let the wood be tied over the support and arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then let the arm be placed on the step of a ladder across its width, and let the hand be extended downwards, and let the rest of the body be suspended from the other side. For the joint immediately enters.
Therefore, when its entry is completed in whichever way is possible, then it is necessary that you place a sphere of equal size made of wool under the rectum. Then apply the plaster prepared from mill dust with frankincense and egg white over the whole shoulder, as it is rolled over.
Then bind below the rectum with a suitable bandage; and let the bandage be rolled over the plaster from above; and let his hand be bound to his neck, and let it be left, and let not his hand be moved for seven days.
And it is necessary that the patient's food be meager until the limb is strengthened; for this is quicker for its healing.
Then let more be given after seven days, or five, and let it be exercised with movement. If therefore it is strengthened and not softened, then it is now healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times, because of the moisture which happens to it, or because of another cause: then it is necessary that a cautery with three holes be applied to it, according to what was set forth in the chapter on cautery.
But if all this is done, and the ligament is loosened after seven days, and the joint is not strengthened, and the plaster and bandage are applied again to it several times, and it is not strengthened, and it falls, and he cannot raise it upwards: then know that its nerves which are at the head of the humerus, are now cut, or stretched, or softened: and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
But the reduction of a dislocation which occurs towards the chest and breast, or to the back, is done with pushing and pulling with the hands: until it returns, and the remaining bandage and care is given to it, until it is healed.
But if after healing heaviness occurs in the limb, and slowness in its movement, then let the patient use a bath many times, until that heaviness is lessened, and it returns to its first nature.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocation of the elbow.
Know that the joint of the elbow is dislocated with difficulty, and similarly it returns with difficulty also: and indeed it is dislocated to all parts, and especially to the front, or to the back.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you: because it is falling under sight, and under touch, according to whichever figure it is dislocated. And when you join the dislocated elbow with the healthy one, that is declared to you manifestly. For the joint is concave, and it cannot double the arm, nor touch with it its shoulder.
And it is necessary that you hasten, and reduce the dislocation immediately, before a hot abscess happens to it.
For if a hot abscess happens to it, its reduction becomes difficult: and perhaps it is not healed completely, especially when it is at the back. For it is worse than all kinds of dislocation, and of more vehement pain than them: and very often death descends with it.
And its restoration, when it is of those which it is possible to reduce, is that his hand be extended with two hands; and his arm be expanded, and the physician's hands be above the elbow and below it, and he himself push the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the root of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or indeed the dislocation is to the front: then it returns thus, that he doubles the hand with his hand, until he strikes with the root of his palm the elbow which is dislocated.
But if the dislocation does not obey reduction, then administer vehement extension, very strong: and it is that two assistants extend the arm; and two assistants also hold him, so that he is not moved during the extension.
Then let the arm be revolved to every part after a long doubled cloth, or a broad ligament, is wrapped around his hand.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, let him anoint it with oil: so that it be of those things which aid in the lubricity of the joint with ease. Then let him impel the joint with a vehement impulse, until it returns.
And after its reduction it is necessary that a plaster be placed upon it, in which there is stypticity, and desiccation with egg white: and let it be bound with a decent bandage. And let the arm be suspended from the neck of the infirm one: and leave it for a few days.
Then let it be unbound. If therefore the joint is fixed in its place, then unbind the ligature from it, and leave it.
And if you see the joint not well bound, then repeat the plaster and the bandage, and leave it for a few days, until it is bound. Then unbind it.
But if heaviness occurs to it after the reduction of the joint, and the condition is in motion: then administer moistening in bathing, and gentle friction, and compression, until it is softened, or place upon the joint fat of iliati, that is, the fat of a ram's rump. Then let it be bound, and leave it upon it day and night. Then remove it, and put it into the bath. When therefore it sweats, then rub the joint with a moderate rubbing. Then repeat upon it the application of fat of iliati once, and twice, and three times with the entry into the bath, until it is softened.
And if you wish, place upon it cow dung, moist, warmed with butter, and bind it over it.
Do that several times. For it is thus softened, and returns to its proper condition.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation, by Almahassem.
The hand is very often dislocated by Al-Mahassem, that is, the joint which is between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. Contrary to the other joints. However, it is necessary that there be speed in the reduction of its dislocation at the hour in which it is dislocated, before the place becomes inflamed: or a hot abscess occurs in it.
And the reduction of the dislocation in it is that you place the weakened hand of Al-Mahassem on a board, and the assistant extends its hand: and the physician places his palm on the prominence of the joint, and pushes it until it returns.
However, it is necessary that it be considered if the palm has already receded to the belly of the hand: then let the infirm person place the back of his hand on the board during extension and reduction: and if the dislocation is proceeding to the back of the hand, then let the position of his hand be inwardly on the board, that is, let the inner part of his hand be placed on the board, so that the hand of the physician may fall upon the prominence of the joint itself.
If therefore it returns immediately, it is good: and if not, then bind it with a plaster soothing the abscess: and leave it, nor repeat it.
For if it does not tolerate, nor can you perform its reduction after days have passed, but the joint remains according to its crookedness: and it does not harm the infirm person anything, except that the hand is softened, and cannot grasp anything: then know that the nerves are severed, or abraded.
Therefore there is no remedy in it, except that it be bound with a cautery near its fall. And perhaps that does not contribute anything.
When therefore Almahassem does not return, then place upon it the plaster which we have narrated. Then let it be bound, and left for five days; afterwards let it be unbound and left, and let the hand be exercised.
But if its movement is deprived, and something serious happens to it: then soften it with warm water, and rub it several times, until it is softened.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of fingers.
When a finger is dislocated in any direction, or a finger is dislocated towards the back of the palm, or its belly: then extend the finger, and push back the dislocation with your thumbs, until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger, and hang it towards the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend it, and extend it until it is straight on that day.
When it is night, bind it according to the same account: and do not cease to unbind it during the day: and exercise it with movement: and bind it at night.
Do this for several days, until it is tightened.
And similarly do with it, if it is dislocated towards the belly of the hand: and bind it to that part, and do with it your first operation, until it is healed.
And similarly do with it when it is dislocated to the other parts.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation of the vertebrae of the back or neck happens to someone, or many vertebrae are removed from their places, then there is no cure in it, because death hastens for the sick person.
And the sign of that is that the excretion of the sick person goes out without will, and he cannot hold it back: and very often some of his limbs are softened by it, or his two legs, or his two arms, or one of them; or one vertebra is removed from its place.
For often it is removed, which often makes the removal of them to four parts.
That therefore which is removed to the hinder parts, is named gibbosity.
The cure however of it is, that you consider then, if gibbosity happens from childhood: then there is no cure in it, nor is there any healing from it at all.
But in that which happens from a fall, or blow, or similar things, the ancients have already described methods of cure at great length. Of which very much does not return with profit.
I however have now shortened it from that, which excuses little from much of it by my nearness to the intention: and by my explanation by which I have explained it, and have formed its instrument in a different way from that by which they showed it, and explained it.
I say therefore, that in gibbosity which happens from the front part, there is no skill, nor healing from it.
And similarly in that which happens to both sides also.
And it is not cured from them, except that which happens in the back properly, with that which I relate: and it is, that the infirm person be extended upon his face upon a level platform near a wall: and a wet mattress be spread under him, so that his chest is not injured.
Then let a straight piece of wood be placed fixed in a hole in the ground, at the head of the platform: and another piece of wood near his feet at the other end of the platform: and let an assistant hold the wood, and let it not be fixed in a hole. And let another assistant hold the other.
Then wrap around the head of the patient, and under his armpits, a soft, firm band: and extend the end of the band to the wood which is at his head: and tie it to it.
Then tighten another band above his hips, and above his knees, and at his ankles. Then gather all the bands together, and tie them to the other piece of wood which is at his feet.
Then let each assistant pull on the band to his part of the wood, and the two pieces of wood shall not be removed from the places in which they are fixed; however, let them not be firmly fixed, as we have said.
And the physician shall place his hands upon the vertebrae with strength, until it returns; or he shall place a board upon it. Then let him attach his feet to the board, until it returns.
But if it does not return by this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three cubits. And hollow out in the wall, of which we have spoken, that is near the patient, a place into which the end of the board may enter. Then place the middle of the board upon the vertebra. And let the physician place his feet upon the other end. And let him pull strongly, until the vertebra is tightened, and returns to its place.
Then if you wish, work with a lathe which is turned with your hand: and it is, that you fix in the ground near the head of the patient at the end of the bed two pieces of wood, whose length is that of one arm: and at his head at the end of the bed two pieces of wood.
And let the length which is between each piece of wood be one palm: and now a hole has been made in every place, in which the lathe runs: and let every piece of wood be firmly fixed in the ground, so that it does not move at all.
And let a round piece of wood be inserted: and it is a lathe in which the cord revolves in the two holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its extremity is a hole, in which is fixed the wood, whose length is a palm together with that which revolves: and in the other two pieces of wood there is similarly that.
Then tighten the bandages which you have tightened on the chest of the patient with the lathe which is at his head: and the bandages which you have tightened on his legs with the lathe which is at his feet.
Then let a servant stand at each lathe, who turns his hand with the cord, with which the lathe revolves: and let the physician adjust the vertebra, as we have said.
This is the form of the lathe, which follows.
Then after the vertebra returns and the place is straightened, then it is necessary that you place a drying plaster with egg white. Then lint. Afterwards place on top of the plaster a small plate of wood, whose width is three fingers, or about that. And its length a quantity which takes in the place of the hump, and above the healthy part of the vertebra.
Then let it be bound with the bandage that we have described. And let the patient use a light diet, until he is healed.
But if something of a prominence remains in the place at the end of healing: then it is necessary that medicines which soften and soothe be administered, with the application of the plate which we have described for a long time. And now a lead plate is applied to it.
And sometimes a prominence occurs in the last vertebra of the back: and a dislocation is thought to be caused by it, and it is that bone which now protrudes. Therefore, a presumption should not be made about it with this medicine: for perhaps it will cause death.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that in the hip joint and the shoulder joint, only dislocation occurs: nor does that happen to them which happens to the other joints from a small displacement and concavity.
And the hip joint indeed is dislocated in four directions. That is, because it is dislocated anteriorly and posteriorly.
And it is indeed most often dislocated inwards; and it is rarely dislocated forwards and backwards.
And the treatment of its inward dislocation is: because when you join the sound leg of the injured person with the injured leg, it is longer; and the knee of the injured person protrudes more than that of the sound person, and the injured person cannot bend his foot at the groin; and the place that follows the groin is inflamed with a hot inflammation, because the head of the femur has already arrived there.
And the sign of someone who has suffered an outward dislocation is that its symptoms are contrary to these symptoms.
And the sign of someone who has suffered a forward dislocation is that he extends the sound leg completely; however, he cannot bend it without pain in the knee; and if he tries to walk, he cannot put weight on it forwards; and his urine is retained, and his groin is inflamed, and when he walks, he treads on his heel.
And the sign of someone who has suffered a backward dislocation is that he cannot extend the knee, nor can he bend it before bending the groin; and his leg is also shorter than the other; and the groin is softened, and the head of the femur is at the place of the ilium.
The reduction, however, of this kind of dislocation is such that you should consider then, if the dislocation is old, which is already long-standing in the person having it. Then if you administer its reduction, and it remains in its position, then there is no cure at all in it. Therefore, no presumption should be made about it completely.
But in the case of one whose dislocation is recent, and is of one of the four kinds of dislocation, then hasten to this, that you may rotate the joint, and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and left: for perhaps it returns, and does not need other treatment.
But if it does not return in this way, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong assistant, and he should extend his leg from below; either with his hands, or with a ligature which is tied above his leg above the knee; and another assistant should extend his hand from above, so that he may put his hands under his armpits. Then he should bind with a soft ligature above the hip joint, and hold the end of the other ligature.
And let its extension be either from the front of the groin to the part of the clavicle, or from behind to the part of the back.
And let the extension of all of them be at once, until the sick man is raised with his body from the ground, and remains hanging.
And this kind of extension is a common kind of the four kinds.
If therefore the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good: and if not, then it is necessary for each kind that which I shall say of its proper cure.
In its proper reduction, when the dislocation is inwards, it is necessary that the sick man be laid on his sound side. Then let him place a bandage above the root of the hip, in that which is between the head of the hip and the place which is under the groin. Then extend the bandage upwards from the groin to the higher part of the body, to the part of the clavicle.
Then let another strong assistant take his arms, and diminish the thickness of the affected hip, and extend it outwards with a strong extension. Now indeed it returns to its place.
And this method is easier than the other methods of treatment by which this limb is reduced.
But if it is excused concerning you, and does not obey you in entering with this method of treatment completely: then it is necessary that the two feet of the infirm be bound together with a strong, soft bandage, over the two heels, and over the two knees: and let the length of each be from its counterpart a quantity of four fingers: and let the infirm leg be extended more than the other by a quantity of two fingers.
Then let the infirm person be suspended over the head of a beam, which is in the house: and let its length from the ground be a quantity of two arms.
Then order a strong youth to hold the head of the hip; and let another youth be suspended with the infirm person; and let another youth push, holding the hip with strength, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
In its proper reduction, however, when it is a dislocation to the outside, it is necessary that the infirm person lie on a bench, according to what we have narrated in the case of one having a hump. And let a bandage be tightened over his leg, the infirm one properly, and over his chest.
Then let two pieces of wood be placed, one at his feet, and another at his head. Then let an added piece of wood be placed in the middle of the bench, fixed well: over which let wet cloths be wrapped, so that it does not injure the infirm person; and let the wood be between his hips, so that he is not pulled downwards during extension.
Then let each assistant extend to his part, and let the physician equalize the reduction with his hand.
If therefore it obeys a return, it will be good.
And if not, place it upon the board, and press it, as we said in the case of a hump, equally. However, it is necessary that the infirm person lie on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it is necessary that the leg of the infirm be extended quickly: and it is according to this same account upon a support: and let the physician place the palm of his right hand upon the groin of the infirm. Then let him constrict it with the other hand, and with that constriction place it extended downwards towards the part of the knee.
And if the dislocation is to the rear: then it is not necessary that the infirm be extended downwards, and be raised above the ground: rather it is necessary that he be placed upon a hard thing, just as it is necessary that it be done if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to that which we said concerning his lying upon two hips: and it is upon his face, and the ligaments extended: according to what we recently said.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a board also upon the place to which the joint goes out, when the reduction is completed as is necessary.
And the sign of its return is not hidden from you: and it is that you extend the two legs of the infirm: and when you see them equal, and the infirm tightens his leg, and extends it without excuse: then know that the member is now returning, as is necessary: then join the two hips, and apply a plaster, and bind it with a bandage, that is, a roller: so that the hip does not move to any part: and let the infirm adhere to rest for three days, or four.
Then untie the bandage and plaster, and compare the leg with the other: then if you see them equal in condition, then know that the dislocation is now fixed: therefore allow the infirm to walk.
And if you see any softening: then return, and plaster it, and bind it according to your first binding: and leave it also for three days.
Then untie it and make him delay in walking on both for several days, until it is well strengthened.
Concerning the dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outwards, inwards, and downwards, that is to say, backwards.
And it is not dislocated forwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you instruct the patient to apply his leg to his hip. But if it does not adhere to the hip, then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration for all types of its dislocation is that you make the patient sit upright, so that he extends his leg on it if he can, and you place your back against his face; and place his foot between your feet.
Then apply his knee with your palms, and straighten it between your fingers above his knee. Then apply with your palm the two sides of his knee with strength. And another assistant should extend his leg until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg adheres with the hip gently, not laboriously.
Then plaster it, and make the leg adhere to the hip. Then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days. Then unbind them.
And let the infirm one not use walking for some days, until it is healed.
But if the reduction of it is refused to you, then administer strong extension with bandages, the account of which preceded in the treatment of the hip, until it returns.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocation of the heel bone.
The heel bone is sometimes removed by a small removal, and sometimes dislocated according to the whole. And its dislocation happens, either inwards, or outwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel bone swollen, protruding towards the part to which it is dislocated.
The treatment, however, of its removal is easy restoration: and it is that it is extended easily with the hands, and is made level, until it returns.
In the treatment, however, when it is dislocated completely, it is necessary that you make the sick person sit upright: and a strong assistant should hold him from the posterior part of his back, in the middle of it.
Then hold you with your right hand his foot from its upper part, and with your left hand from the lower part of his foot at the place of the heel.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. Then with the left twice.
Then extend with the left, and push the front of the foot with the right towards the leg without effort.
Do that twice, as we have narrated.
Then push the front of the foot to the leg a third time, and you pull the peg.
If therefore it returns in one or two times according to this method, and you see the foot set, it is good: and if not, then repeat the operation on it; because it returns.
But if the reduction is prevented by what we have narrated, then make the patient lie on his back on the ground, and fix a peg firmly in the ground, so that it falls between his hips; and you have already wrapped a cloth around it, so that it does not injure the patient.
Then let the assistant take his hip; afterwards let another assistant extend the foot, either with his hands or with a ligature which he ties around the neck of the foot. Then let each assistant extend against the extension of his companion; and let the peg stand between the two hips of the patient, restraining him so that his body is not drawn downwards during extension.
Then let the physician set the dislocation with his hands, and let another assistant hold the sound leg downwards. For the dislocation returns quickly.
When therefore the dislocation returns, and the soundness of its return is made clear to you: then apply a plaster and lint, and bind it firmly with ligatures, and bind the foot with a ligature to the leg.
And it is necessary that you beware or fear the nerves which are above the heel behind, lest the ligature be too strong upon them and injure them.
Then leave it for two or three days. But if the ligature loosens, tighten it: then loosen it on the third or fourth day: and forbid the patient from walking for forty days. If however he attempts to walk before that time, it is not certain that the dislocation will not be loosened and damaged, and that it will not receive a cure afterwards.
But if a hot abscess should occur to him, then it is necessary that you administer for its sedation that which our account preceded in another place concerning treatment, and an embrocation, until it is removed.
Concerning the dislocation of the toes, and the cure.
It is necessary that what is dislocated of them be set with a little extension without effort; and this indeed is not difficult, indeed it is easy.
But if the dislocation is in some knots of the back of the foot, then it is necessary that the infirm person make his foot sit upon a place level with the earth, or upon a board; and he is standing as if walking.
Then you stand, and place your foot upon that which protrudes from those joints. Then tread on it with your foot with force, until it returns, and you see it now set, and no prominence appears in the place.
Then place under the belly of his foot a board which holds the whole foot, to which there are two heads. Then bind it with a suitable bandage for three days. Then unbind it, and cause him to rest from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and secured by the reduction.
Concerning the types of dislocation with a wound or fracture.
When something of that sort happens, and you attempt its cure and restoration, death often follows: and on account of that, no one ought to presume to undertake its cure, which is of this kind, except one who is skilled in the art through long practice, merciful, pious, slow, not rash, nor presumptuous: and so that he uses in the beginning only such medicines as soothe hot abscesses: and leaves the patient to divine judgment. Unless you see, or hope for salvation from destruction with mildness of the illness, and in which something of hope appears. Therefore, attempt its reduction immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a hot abscess occurs.
If then the member returns as we wish, then administer a regime which soothes the abscess: and treat the wound with what is suitable to it from drying ointments.
But if the dislocation is with a fracture, and splinters occur in the separated bone, then attempt their removal, and do in that what we have said in simple illnesses from what preceded in their places.
And beware according to your ability, and guard yourself from entering the path of fear: according to what my previous instruction to you was.
For that is better for you with your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and safer for your body with those in your time.
